Table of Contents Preface Chapter 1 The Rauzer Variation: doubled f-pawns for Black Chapter 2 The Maroczy Bind Chapter 3 The Hedgehog Chapter 4 The Sveshnikov Variation Chapter 5 French Defence: Winawer Variation Chapter 6 Italian, Petroff, Ruy Lopez Chapter 7 Exercises 2 Preface Fifteen years ago (in 2008) I started with my work on middlegames in chess, and my first book on this topic was titled Winning Chess Middlegames. That book was (mostly) based on middlegames arising from 1.d4 openings and was divided into four basic pawn structures: • White has doubled c-pawns (like in the Nimzo-Indian Defence and similar openings); • White has hanging pawns in the centre; • White has an isolated pawn; and • White has a pawn majority in the centre. That book was well received and I was often asked if there would be a similar work involving middlegames coming from 1.e4 openings. At that time I was still an active player; my White-side openings were based on 1.d4 while my main weapons with Black were different variations of the Ruy Lopez. Understandably, at that time I was not in a hurry to delve into all the different Sicilians, Frenchs, and Caro-Kanns. At the end of 2013, I finished my career as an active player and started coaching. My early 'training jobs' were with promising young players like Alireza Firouzja, Parham Maghsodloo and Salem AR Saleh. Obviously, 1.e4 openings and the middlegames arising from them became my daily routine now... when I had spent enough time investigating those middlegame positions, the old question resurfaced: why not a book on Winning Chess Middlegames with 1.e4 Structures ? I considered I was up to the job now, and this book is a product of that decision. The middlegames arising from 1.e4 openings are very diverse, and it was next to impossible to include all of them in one book in a proper way. So I had to make choices. I understand that some readers may be disappointed that, for example, the Sicilian Pawn Storm, the Sicilian Dragon, the Open Ruy Lopez, the French Tarrasch and the Caro-Kann are not included in this book, but... I had to make choices. It is quite possible that sometime at the end of 2025 I may find the time and energy to write 3 another edition including the middlegames coming from these openings. As for the opening lines that did find their way into this book, I wanted to analyse the positions thoroughly, and the material presented is predominantly aimed at improving the reader's playing level. I have tried to present the knowledge I have acquired during twenty years as a top-level player plus a coaching career of ten years, working with some of the world's best players of today, in an accessible way so that readers of different chess levels can benefit from it. As its name suggests, the book is aimed at middlegame improvement; however, I also share a sizeable portion of my opening knowledge. I hope that the chosen games will inspire the reader on his/her journey to chess improvement. Ivan Sokolov Amsterdam, October 2023 4 Chapter 1 The Rauzer Variation: doubled f-pawns for Black In these first four chapters, we will discuss four typical Sicilian structures: The Rauzer Variation (Chapter 1), the Maroczy Bind (Chapter 2), the Hedgehog (Chapter 3) and the Sveshnikov Variation (Chapter 4). Understanding the dynamics of the Rauzer Variation where Black has doubled f- pawns is, in my view, very important for an understanding of open Sicilians in general. We will start with two examples involving very strong players landing in practically lost positions with White without blundering anything or (seemingly) doing anything stupid. In both cases, bad judgement by the white player of the timing of central pawn structure changes had disastrous consequences. A dream position for Black that White should avoid This is a dream position for Black in these Rauzer structures. He has an excellently placed Bh6, his king is safe on e7, his rooks are connected (the Rh8 is ready to join the attack on the queenside, and his pawns are rolling towards White's king. 5 White's pieces are poorly coordinated; the Bd3 and the Nf3 are doing nothing, and the d5-square in Black's camp is a purely 'academic weakness'. It will take White a very long time to create threats while Black's attack is swift. Standard mistakes by White In many Rauzer lines where Black's f-pawns are doubled (after the trade Bxf6 gxf6; both in the Kozul Variation with 8...Bd7 and the classical Rauzer with 8...Be7), White's basic positional idea is to push f5, provoke Black's ...e5, and try to capitalize on Black's light-square weaknesses. There is however one common mistake many top players often make! At the moment when the central pawn structure transformation has taken place (White has pushed f5, Black has responded with ...e5), the future of White's c3- knight is crucial for judging the timing (and effects) of the f4-f5 pawn push! Most of the time Black has already pushed ...b4, so White's knight is no longer on the c3- square and hence cannot immediately jump to d5. The ideal future for White's knight is to be placed on h5-square (hitting f6) and preparing a pawn storm with h4, g4, and g5, shattering the black king's pawn defences. Often White players make a mistake by closing the centre while their knight does not have such a future! In such cases White's position becomes cramped, active is play difficult to find for him, while Black's king is safe in the centre and Black can quickly launch a strong attack on White's monarch. I will show a few instructive examples where this mistake was committed by some very strong players. Game 1 Nigel Short 2660 Zdenko Kozul 2560 Belgrade 1989
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 d6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 Nf6 5.Nc3 Nc6 6.Bg5 e6 7.Qd2 a6 8.O-O-O Bd7
Zdenko Kozul started exploring 'his' variation in the mid-1980s and had many instructive ups and downs with it. In this chapter we will also deal with the 'Classical Rauzer' (8...Be7).

12.Nxc6
White can delay this decision by opting for 12.Nce2. Later, he came up with an interesting idea in Saric-Vovk, Douglas 2019 (one of the games selected in this chapter).
12...Bxc6 13.Bd3
White wants his bishop 'out' before moving his knight to e2, but this loses precious time. In the modern treatment of this variation, White takes immediate action with 13.f5 b4 14.Ne2 (see Grischuk-Lupulescu).
13...b4 14.Ne2

14...h5!
Black wants his bishop on the h6-c1 diagonal, to stop White's g4, h4, g5 pawn storm. White now needs his knight on h5, playing something like h4, Ng3, Qe2 (or Be2) followed by Nxh5, but with 13.Bd3 White has lost time and getting this done proves difficult. White now needs to be careful to keep the balance!
15.Rhf1?!
Nigel Short was not the only strong player (by far) to aim for a wrong plan here; I will give a few other examples. Likely, White's best move is 15.f5!, but after
15...Bh6 he needs to find the right follow-up!
8 We have examples of two strong players who got in the same kind of trouble Nigel Short did in the game: A) The seemingly forced move 16.Qe1? puts White in a horrible situation: 16...e5 and just like in our main game, White's knight has no future and he has no active play. Black's plan is relatively obvious: place the king on e7, connecting rooks (the king will be safe there), then at some stage or immediately start pushing his pawns on the queenside, creating a frontal attack. 17.Bc4 (17.Ng3 h4 18.Nf1 Ke7 was seen in Kryvoruchko-Ivanchuk, Tromsø 2013. White would love to play Ne3-Nd5, but how?? Black had a clear advantage and went on to win) 17...Ke7 18.b3. White's bishop is well placed, but this is far from enough for any serious counterplay here. Kozul now found a beautiful idea to lock up White's knight: 18...Rag8 (your engine will tell you that the pawn can be taken: 18...Bxe4μ) 19.Ng3 h4 20.Nh5

15...a5
White continues on the wrong path!
16.f5?
Clearly overestimating the weakness of the d5-square.
16...e5
White has succeeded in weakening the d5-square, but at what price? His Ne2 is a problem child. The ideal h5-square is impossible to reach here! White now spends time hoping to improve his knight while Black builds up an attack risk-free.
17.Ng1
17.Bc4 Qc5 18.Bd5 does not help White, as after 18...Bxd5 19.Qxd5 Qxd5 20.Rxd5
Rg8! he loses material: 21.g3 Rg4 and Black wins a pawn (to start with) while White's light-square domination will be temporary.
17...Qc5 18.Qe2 a4
Black has a risk-free textbook Rauzer attack.
19.Bc4
The queen trade does not bring White salvation as Black is much better after 19.Qf2 Bh6 or 19...Qxf2 20.Rxf2 Bh6.
19...Ke7!
Black's king is safe while his rooks are connected and ready to attack.
20.Nh3
The unfortunate knight is still looking for employment.
20.Bd5 does not help; the endgame after 20...Bb5 21.Qf2 Rc8 22.Qxc5 Rxc5 23.Rf2
Bh6 is hopeless for White (Black's bishop pair rules!).
20...Bh6 21.Rf3 Rhc8
Next to activating his rook, Black also has a direct threat - 22...Bxe4.
22.b3
This typical Rauzer move secures White's bishop position but compromises his king.
22...axb3 23.cxb3 Ra3
A mating net around is woven around White's king and typical Rauzer tactics occur. White is already beyond salvation. Black has a devastating attack, and the knight on h3 will never enter the battle.
24.Rfd3

13.Qe1
Currently the most popular move is 13.Nxc6 as for example in the game Grischuk- Lupulescu, Bucharest 2021. Another possibility is to aim for an immediate determination of the central pawn structure with 13.f5 when after 13...e5 14.Nxc6 Bxc6 15.Ng3 we have a typical situation where White tries to make use of his knight by bringing it to the attacking h5-square and Black tries to stop this: 15...h5 16.h4. White should now indeed aim to collect the pawn on h5, however this takes time. We have here another instructive example of a (very) strong player going wrong very quickly and getting in serious trouble: 16...Qc5 (readers interested in opening theory should investigate the pawn sacrifice 16...Bh6 17.Qxd6 Rd8 18.Qxd8+ Qxd8 19.Rxd8+ Kxd8 20.Bxa6. analysis diagram Black is active and now gains back some of his sacrificed material: 20...Rg8 21.Rh3 Bf4. We already have some grandmaster-level games here and there will probably be further theoretical discussion. It seems to me that Black is active enough to hold a draw) 17.b3?!. This 'bishop improvement move' (planning Bc4) is typical but not White's best here; principled and critical is the immediate pawn grab 17.Qe2! Ke7 18.Nxh5. After the text, Black went for the typical king improvement 17...Ke7! and now Ponomariov took a strange decision (not following up on his previous move) with 18.Bd3? (for better or worse, White had to follow up on his previous move and develop 18.Bc4)
18...Bh6 19.Qe1. Ruslan understood the trouble he'd got himself into and took
advantage of the 'respect element' by offering a draw which was accepted in Ponomariov-Blehm, Ohrid 2001. In reality, Black has a winning advantage here! Similiar to Short-Kozul and our main game, White is much too late creating active play while Black has a simple and straightforward attack. To give an idea of the trouble Ponomariov was in, after the simple 19...a5 (starting a straight attack) my Stockfish 15 (at serious depth) evaluates the position as close to minus four!!
13...h5
I will give another instructive example of a (very) strong player quickly getting himself in terrible trouble by playing 'logical' moves: 13...Rc8 14.Nxc6 Bxc6
15.Nd4 Bb7.

(this queen sortie, aiming to win a pawn, loses precious time; however it was already hard to give White any god advice. Similar to Short- Kozul, White's problem child is his knight and perhaps the best decision was to try to improve it with 18.Nd2 a5 19.Nc4 Qc5. In order to get a strong attack, Black needs to develop his Bf8 and Kozul uses this moment to get it done: 19...h5!. A) The pawn cannot be taken as after 20.Qxh5 Bxe4 21.Bd3 Bxd3 22.Rxd3 (22.cxd3 allows 22...b3) 22...Qc5 White has no good defence; his pieces have terrible coordination. 23.Rd2. An excellent example of things gone terribly wrong without having made any obvious mistake!) 24.a3 Black has various ways to annihilate White here: A) 24...Rc3 25.Qxh5 Rb8 26.Qxh6 bxc2+ 27.Rxc2 A1) 27...Ba6?. 14.Nxc6 Bxc6
Now White commits a mistake (known to us already) by hurrying to fix the pawn centre at the wrong moment.
15.f5?
White had to keep the tension (and keep the central pawn structure flexible!) with
15.Nd4 Bb7 16.Bc4 with probably some advantage.
15...e5 16.Qh4
Black happily improves his king:
16...Ke7!
Only two seemingly logical moves and yet such a dramatic change! Similar to Short- Kozul or Shirov-Kozul, Black gets an unstoppable, brutal frontal attack on White's king. White takes the best decision and continues his pawn-grab plan.
17.Ng3
To make his attack a crushing one, Black needs his dark-squared bishop, and now is the time.
17...Bh6! 18.Nxh5 Bg5 19.Qg4
The pawn gain is small consolation for White's troubles. Black gets a deadly attack.
19...Qc5 20.h4 Be3 21.Rh3
Time for the standard pawn roll:
21...a5
White tries to bring her Q&N back into play but will not be in time.
22.Qf3 Bd4 23.Ng3 Rhc8
White still needs a lot of time to coordinate her pieces, The situation cannot be saved. Black has various sacrificial ideas, most of them based on the weak white king (the pawn defences can easily be destroyed), the Rh3, Ng3 being totally out of play (in essence, Black has a material advantage) and the white king having no real defenders while Black attacks with all his pieces (the attackers & defenders ratio!). The execution is beautiful and brutal!
24.Rd2
On 24.Bd3, Black has an execution that is typical for these positions (try to remember the ideas!) 24...b3! 25.cxb3 a4 26.Bc4 (the pawn defences need to be destroyed) 26...Bxb2! 27.Kxb2 axb3 28.Bxb3 Qa3+ 29.Kb1 Ba4 analysis diagram and White either gets mated or suffers decisive material loss: 30.Bxa4 (or 30.Ne2 Bxb3 31.Qxb3 Rcb8) 30...Qxa4 and the game may end with a pretty mate: 31.Qe2 Rab8+! 32.Ka1 Qd4+! 33.Rxd4 Rc1#.
24...Bc3
The simple pawn roll 24...a4 followed by 25...b3 should also win. Most likely, Black had already calculated his sacrificial idea.
25.Rd1 Ba4
Another typical sacrificial idea was 25...Bxb2 26.Kxb2 Ba4 and Black has a winning advantage as to prevent mate White will lose decisive material.
26.b3
Now follows an 'instructive destruction'. Black sacrifices first a bishop and then a rook to destroy the white king's pawn defences.
26...Bxb3! 27.cxb3 a4 28.Bc4 axb3 29.Bxb3
Now the rook is sacrificed:
29...Rxa2!!
White's material advantage of R+N is 'sleeping' on the wrong side of the board.
30.Bxa2
30.Kxa2 runs into a forced mate: 30...Qa5+ 31.Kb1 Qa1+ 32.Kc2 Be1+! 33.Kd3
Qa6+ 34.Bc4 Rxc4 35.Ke2 Rxe4#. Now Black 'cleans up' the road to annihilation of White's king:
30...b3! 31.Bxb3 Qa3 32.Kc2 Qb2+ 33.Kd3
Precision is still needed! Black has only one move that wins.
33...Ba5! 34.Bc4
Now White's last defender needs to be destroyed:
34...Rxc4! 35.Ne2
35.Kxc4 runs into checkmate: 35...Qc2+ 36.Kb5 Qc5+ - a beautiful example of a
king chase. The whole army of white pieces is 'sleeping' on the wrong side of the board while White's 'active' king is fighting on his own.
35...Qc2+ 36.Ke3 Bb6+
And White resigned. The next two examples are related to the Kozul Variation in the Rauzer. Understanding the specifics of this variation (doubled f-pawns for Black) in relation to the Classical Rauzer with doubled f-pawns is essential for anyone willing to master these positions on either side of the board. I have tried to give good examples and accompany them with some theoretical information. Favourable situations for White I have tried to give a number of dynamic/strategic examples in my comments to the next two games. However, the two diagrammed positions are typical 'favourable situations for White'. One diagram is with light-squared bishops, the other is without, but both positions are favourable for White because the knight on h5 is excellently placed, helping the attack on Black's king. The pawn push g4-g5 will open the position, Black's king is not safe now on e7 (tactics work for White here) and Black is slow with his attack while White is ready to unleash a storm. Decisive light-square domination At the start of this chapter we saw a few examples of strong GMs as White playing 'logical moves' and getting into terrible trouble. However, it is also very easy to get into a hopeless situation as Black without doing anything particularly stupid. This game is a good example. Game 3 Alexander Grischuk 2776 Constantin Lupulescu 2656 Bucharest 2021
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 d6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 Nf6 5.Nc3 Nc6 6.Bg5 Bd7 7.Qd2 a6 8.O-O-O e6
9.f4 b5 10.Bxf6 gxf6 11.Kb1 Qb6 12.Nxc6 Bxc6 13.f5
Understanding the dynamics of these Rauzer positions with doubled f-pawns has a lot to do with the right timing of transformations in the central pawn structure (when to allow/decide on f5 followed by fxe6, when to block the centre by pushing ...e5). Black now decides to fix the central pawn structure by pushing ...e5.
13...b4
A) An alternative is the often-played move 13...h5 when we can get a totally different pawn structure: 14.fxe6 fxe6. Black no longer has doubled pawns, however his central pawns can be targets and also Black needs to constantly take into account White's central pawn break/sacrifice e5. Black's queen often comes to the e5-square in those positions and White's ability to remove the queen from there (usually by bringing his knight to d3) determines the assessment of the position. We have many high-level games here. While this is not an opening manual, I will give a few examples that are instructive to understanding the plans on both sides: 15.Bd3 and now: A1) A known pawn sacrifice is 15.Qf4 Qc5! and after 16.Qxf6 Qe5 it has been established (computer engines agree) that Black has enough compensation for the sacrificed pawn. A2) Readers interested in theoretical verdicts should also analyse 15.Qe1: A21) 15.Bd3 O-O-O. Here we see how vulnerable Black's pawns are. White needs to bring his knight to f4 when Black has problems to protect his pawns: 16.Rhf1 (also good for White is the immediate 16.Ne2!. Black can put his queen on e5, but then problems appear on the queenside: 16...Qc5 17.Rhf1 Qe5. Now we see the drawbacks of 15...O-O-O: 18.Qa5! Bb7 19.a4 and White has a large advantage, e.g. 19...b4 20.Qxb4 d5 loses to 21.Qb3 dxe4 22.Bc4) 16...Be7. Now the white knight moves to f4: 17.Ne2! Rde8 18.Nf4 Bd8. It may seem that Black has protected everything, but his problems are only starting: 19.Be2! Bxe4 20.Bxh5 Re7 21.Bf3! Bxf3. White has a safe king and open files for hitting Black's pawns. Black has a weak king and no good way to deal with the problems of the central pawn structure. Here, strongest for White was 22.Rxf3! (the game went 22.gxf3 Rhh7 23.h4 and White had advantage and went on to win in Ivanchuk-Piket, Monaco 1996) when Black does not have a good way to protect his central pawns and his position simply collapses:
22...Rxh2 23.Re3 d5 24.Rde1;
A22) 15...Qc5 (Black wants to bring his queen to e5) 16.Rhf1. A221) It is good to know that in case of 16...Qe5 White has a typical tactic: 17.Nd5! and after 17...exd5 18.exd5 Qxd5 19.Bg6+ Kd7 Black does not get away (cheaply) with a worse opposite-coloured bishops ending as White does not capture on d5 but plays 20.Qa5! and wins. Black's queen is attacked, while 21. Bf5+ is a threat too; A222) 16...Rh6 and now: A2221) 17.e5!? is a typical tactic that works here: 17...dxe5 (in case of 17...f5 White plays 18.Bxf5! exf5 19.Rxf5 d5 20.e6! O-O-O 21.Rxf8 and ends a sound pawn up, e.g.
21...Qxf8 22.e7 Qxe7 23.Qxh6 Kb7 24.Rd4±) 18.Ne4 Qe7 19.Qc3 Rc8 (best for
Black was 19...Bxe4! 20.Bxe4 Rd8 when the engine gives many moves around plus one for White. It looks to me that Black has reasonable chances to hold) 20.Be2! (now White wins material) 20...Bd7 21.Qa5 Kf7 22.Qxa6 (Black cannot prevent another pawn loss) 22...Rc6 23.Qxb5 f5 24.Qb3 Kg7 25.Bb5 and White soon won in Inarkiev-Zhou, China tt 2018; A2222) Still, 17.e5 may not even be the strongest move here. The quiet and often played plan 17.Ne2! Qe5 18.Nf4 looks excellent for White. Black's queen will be removed from e5 and Black will be in big trouble. After 18...Bg7 White has a simple and often-seen plan: bishop goes to c2, knight goes to d3, Black's queen is removed from e5 and as a result his position collapses: 19.c3 h4 20.Bc2 O-O-O 21.Nd3 (this spells big trouble for Black here and in similar positions!) 21...Qxh2 22.Qf2 and Black cannot defend against all the threats. If 22...Kb7, White can simply take material with 23.Nc5+ (your engine will indicate 23.a4 as even stronger: 23...Qg3
24.Qg1 (25.a5 is a terrible threat so Black has to grab on a4) 24...bxa4 25.Nc5+)
23...dxc5 24.Rxd8 with a winning advantage.
A23) Readers interested in theory should note that Black's best (at least in my opinion) is 15...b4! 16.Ne2 Bh6! 17.Qe1 Qc5 with complex play. B) The immediate (not often played) 13...O-O-O!? is interesting to explore as after
14.fxe6 fxe6 15.Bd3 Kb8 16.Rhf1 Be7 17.Ne2
White uses a standard manoeuvre but is not in time to put real pressure on e6:
17...d5! 18.exd5 Rxd5 19.Nf4 Rd6 20.Qe2 e5 with approximate equality and a later
draw in Akopian-Ivanchuk, Warsaw 2003.
14.Ne2 e5 15.Ng3
The situation is unpleasant for Black. White's knight should not be allowed to occupy the h5-square here, but if Black pushes ...h5 himself he must be ready to sacrifice material. Black refrains from sacrificing material and quickly gets a lost position:
15...Qc5
Most likely, Black's best option was to go for 15...h5! and now: A) 16.Bc4 is an interesting idea which however should not work provided Black is not so greedy as to collect a pawn: 16...h4 17.Nf1
17...Qc5! (17...Bxe4? is a mistake as after 18.Qe2 Bxf5 19.Ne3 White gets his pawn
back and obtains good attacking prospects: 19...Be6 20.Qf3 Ra7 21.Bxe6 fxe6
22.Qxf6 Rh6 23.Qf3 and White went on to win in Grischuk-Eliseev, Moscow 2014)
18.Bd5 Rc8 and Black has a great position. His bishop comes to h6, controlling the
h6-c1 diagonal making sure the Nf1 can't get to d5; B) 16.h4 and now: B1) 16...Bh6 17.Qxd6 Rd8 18.Qxd8+ Qxd8 19.Rxd8+ Kxd8 analysis diagram To enter such a position is not an easy decision. Normally speaking we have play for two results and it's not easy to say if (in a human game) White is closer to a win or Black to a draw. Which pawn should White take first? Probably the one on a6:
20.Bxa6 (after 20.Nxh5 Ke7 21.Bd3 Black should cut off the knight's retreat with
21...Rg8! when he has enough compensation to hold a draw; not precise is 21...Be3
22.Ng3 Rg8 23.Nf1 Bh6 24.Rg1 and Black did not have enough compensation,
White went on to win in Saric-Jankovic, Bol 2014) 20...Rg8. This position will be further tested in practice. It's not easy to say if after the relatively forced 21.Rh3 Bf4
22.Nxh5 Rxg2 23.Rh1 Bxe4 24.Bd3 Bxd3 25.cxd3
analysis diagram
25...Be3 26.Nxf6 Bd4 27.b3, in a game between humans White is closer to a win or
Black to a draw; B2) Another way to sacrifice a pawn is 16...Qc5. B21) Peter Svidler, playing White, opted for an instructive (standard) exchange sacrifice idea: 17.Qe2 Ke7 18.Nxh5 Bb5 19.Qg4 Bh6. Does Black have enough compensation? No, he does not. White correctly decided to sacrifice an exchange with 20.Rd5 Qc7 21.Qd1! Bc6 22.Bc4 with a great position. Black survived to a draw in Svidler-Dubov, Moscow 2012; B22) Another way to execute a similar idea is 17.Bd3 Bh6 18.Qe2 Qe3 19.Nxh5! Ke7 (19...Qxe2?? blunders a pawn to 20.Nxf6+ Ke7 21.Bxe2). Here, White should keep the queens on the board: 20.Qf1! Bxe4... analysis diagram ... and now White's best option is to go for the exchange sacrifice 21.g4! 21...Bxh1 22.Qxh1. Due to his light-square domination and Black's weak king
White has plenty of compensation here. Grischuk now opts for an improvement of his bishop that is typical in these Rauzer positions:
16.b3! h5!
Enabling ...Bh6.
17.h4
17...Bb5?

18.Qe1!
A strong move, possibly underestimated by Black.
18...Rc8 19.Bxb5+ axb5 20.Qe2
White's rook comes to d5 with menacing force. In a higher sense, the game is decided. Grischuk executes with a steady hand.
20...Ra8 21.Rd5 Qa7 22.Qxb5+ Kd8
Black's pawn structure is horrible. White's king is safe. Black's bishop is now a perfect example of the 'useless Rauzer bishop' (dominated by White's knight).
23.a4 bxa3 24.Rhd1 Be7
Now it's time to collect the pawn on h5:
25.Qe2! Rc8 26.c4!

34...Qb4 35.Rc4 Qe1+ 36.Rd1 Qe3 37.Qd3 1-0
Kingside pawn storm, light-square control Game 4 Milos Perunovic 2628 Alojzije Jankovic 2549 Bosnia/Herzegovina tt 2014 I have picked up this game because it showns how brutal White's attack can be if the knight on h5 combines with the g4-g5 pawn push in a good way.
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 d6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 Nf6 5.Nc3 Nc6 6.Bg5 e6 7.Qd2 a6 8.O-O-O Bd7
9.f4 b5 10.Bxf6 gxf6 11.Kb1 b4 12.Nce2 Qb6 13.Nxc6 Bxc6 14.f5 e5 15.Ng3 Qc5
As mentioned in Grischuk-Lupulescu, Black should strongly consider 15...h5 here.
16.Nh5
Grischuk opted for 16.b3 in the abovementioned game.
16...Ke7?
The standard Rauzer king move, based on the belief that Black's king will be safe in the centre. Yes, the king will be safe... unless his pawn defences are broken down. At the time this game was played, 16...Ke7 was considered quite okay for Black. But the direct brutal approach White took in this game 'closed the line'! Black had to opt for 16...Be7 and after 17.Bd3 (17.Qh6 Bxe4 18.Bd3 does not look clear - there are many options) 17...O-O-O! Black wants to push ...d5 when matters are far from clear. This was probably the reason why Grischuk opted for 16.b3. The pawn grab 18.Bxa6+ is risky as after 18...Kc7 Black has the a-file; he wants to push ...d5 and has good compensation.
17.g4!
All the drawbacks of Black's king on e7 are now apparent! Take note that with the white knight on h5 supported by the g4-g5 pawn push, the king is in a grave danger on e7! The tactical idea 17.Bc4 is a mistake as 17...Rg8 stops the g-pawn push and Black's king is suddenly safe on e7. White's wishful thinking is that Black goes for the pawn grab 17...Bxe4? as after 18.Rhe1 Bc6 19.Qf4! White gets a dream scenario: 19...Rg8
20.Qh4 Rg5 21.Nf4! when White's light-square domination, Black's weak king and
his dead bishop on f8 should prove decisive.
17...Rg8
The pawn grab 17...Bxe4? runs into an obvious refutation: 18.Bg2 Bxg2 19.Qxg2 Rc8 20.Rd5 and everything goes 'with the music'. White now uses the fact that Black's queen must protect d6:
18.Rg1!
Black is already in a bad situation and decides to grab a pawn after all.
18...Bxe4
The alternative was 18...Rg5. Now White should not be in a hurry and calmly prepare h4 and g5: 19.Rg3! (Black's idea comes to the sureface after 19.h4 when he sacrifices an exchange and gets some counterplay with 19...Rxh5 20.gxh5. Now Black uses the fact that the Rg1 is hanging: 20...Bh6! 21.Qd3 a5 and Black has survival chances) 19...Bh6 20.Qg2 21.h4 comes and Black is in a bad shape as the exchange sacrifice ...Rxh5 does not work so well here.
19.g5?!
This seemingly direct blow is a mistake here, allowing Black to stay in the game! It was better to opt for light-square domination with 19.Bg2! Bxg2 20.Rxg2 (the alternative is 20.Qxg2 Rc8 21.h3 with 22. Rd5 to follow; the immediate 21.Rd5?? horribly blunders into 21...Rxg4) 20...Rc8 analysis diagram
21.h4! (Black has no counterplay, White has huge domination, but it's important not
to blunder into something stupid like 21.Rg3?? Bh6) 21...h6 22.Rg3. White has a winning advantage. Black's position is about to collapse, e.g. 22...a5 (or 22...d5
23.a3) 23.b3 (23.Rd3? is too hasty and a blunder: 23...Rxg4 24.Rxd6 Rd4!; but
23.Qg2 followed by Rd5 also wins) White wants to play 24. Qg2 followed by Rd5.
Black has no good moves: 23...Qc6 (or 23...d5 24.Qxd5 Qxc2+ 25.Ka1) 24.Rd3 Rxg4 25.Rxd6.
19...fxg5?
Not seizing the sudden opportunity to escape! A) 23.d4 Qc6 is double-edged: 24.dxe5 (24.Qxg5+? is wrong as only Black can be better after 24...f6! 25.Qxf6+ Ke8 26.Qe6+ Kd8 27.Qb3 Qe4+ 28.Ka1 Qxf5)
24...dxe5 25.Qxb4+ (there is no mate after 25.f6+ Ke6) 25...Ke8 26.Qb3 White
probably has sufficient compensation to hold the balance, but not more; B) In reality, after 23.Qxg5+ Black has 23...f6! and the position is about equal
24.Qxf6+ Ke8 25.Qe6+ Kd8. White has to protect against the 26...Qc2+ threat:
26.Qb3 Bh6! (a direct mate is always a respectable threat) 27.Qg8+ Kc7=.

Now the game ends with a brutal annihilation!
20...Bf3 21.Rc4 Qb6 22.Rxb4 Qc6

23.Rb7+! Kd8 24.Qa5+ Kc8 25.Rb6 Qc7 26.Bxa6+

Flexibility and the rook lift Another idea for White is a third rank rook lift, which is not often seen. Game 5 Ivan Saric 2667 Andrey Vovk 2618 Douglas 2019
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 Nf6 5.Nc3 d6 6.Bg5 e6 7.Qd2 a6 8.O-O-O Bd7
9.f4 b5 10.Bxf6 gxf6 11.Kb1 Qb6
I have picked up this game because White came up with a nice idea: keeping his central situation flexible while coming with an instructive rook lift. To my knowledge, the originator of the idea is Serbian/Turkish GM Dragan Solak.
12.Nce2!?
White is flexible. The knight is pre-emptively moved away from the ...b4 tempo while most options have been preserved. Black decides on a standard Rauzer move:
12...h5
Black can of course also continue the standard plan and push his b-pawn (as if White's knight was still on c3): 12...b4. White can now choose between different options created by the flexible 12.Nce2!?: A) 13.Nxc6 Bxc6 14.f5 can transpose into known lines after 14...e5 (the pawn grab
14...Bxe4? is not a good idea: 15.fxe6 fxe6 16.Qf4 d5 17.Ng3 Bg6 (17...e5? 18.Qh4
only causes more trouble for Black) 18.Qxf6 Rg8 19.Bd3 - White has got his pawn back and has a clear advantage) 15.Ng3 h5 16.h4 and now Black can go for a pawn sac line (as mentioned in Grischuk-Lupulescu): 16...Bh6 17.Qxd6 Rd8 18.Qxd8+ Qxd8 19.Rxd8+ Kxd8 20.Bxa6 and the verdict is still open whether, in a human game, White is closer to a win or Black to a draw here; B) White can still follow the rook lift plan 13.h4 h5 14.Rh3 with some advantage as in Saric-Li Chao, chess.com blitz 2020; C) Another option is to changie the kingside pawn structure in a different way by pushing the g-pawn: 13.g4!?. If Black does not challenge him, White will continue pushing his kingside pawns: h4, g5: 13...h5 14.g5 fxg5 15.fxg5 (if Black consolidates, he will be doing very well positionally, but White has the 'momentum') 15...Nxd4 16.Nxd4 Qc5. analysis diagram Ivan Cheparinov exploited White's momentum with 17.g6! fxg6 18.Qg2 Rg8 19.e5! d5. analysis diagram Black still needs to spend time solving his king problem as queenside castling loses the important pawn on a6 (and with check). Strongest for White here seems to be

Black had to continue with 20...Qb6 21.Bxh5 O-O-O with ...Bc5 to follow and a dynamically balanced game; that game was later drawn) when Black's situation is far from easy. 34.Nd4 1-0
Classical Rauzer positions The last part of this chapter is about Classical Rauzer positions in which Black has doubled f-pawns. The Classical Rauzer is enjoying a renaissance and is indeed a great weapon for Black to play for a win. The main difference compared to the Kozul Variation is that here Black already has his bishop developed on e7 while White has committed his knight to f3. While many ideas for Black in the Kozul Variation are based on the advantages of Black's bishop still being on its initial square (f8), f3 is not a square White is happy to place his knight on in these lines. The ensuing positions are dynamically balanced, however it is easy to make mistakes. The examples I have chosen show White executing his basic strategic idea in this pawn structure while Black needs to be smart with his pawn break and the timing of dynamics to get adequate counterplay. A typical dynamic situation in the Classical Rauzer. White has achieved his strategic regrouping and is hitting on Black's e6-weakness. Black can't say 'pass' and has to react, relying on pawn breaks and tactics. The position is objectively dynamically balanced but it's easy for either side to go wrong. Classical Rauzer plans, pawn breaks, dynamics Now we will investigate the dynamics in what was formerly considered the main line in the Rauzer with doubled f-pawns. Game 6 Markus Ragger 2701 Ivan Salgado Lopez 2624 Germany Bundesliga 2018/19
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 Nf6 5.Nc3 d6 6.Bg5 e6 7.Qd2 a6 8.O-O-O Bd7
9.f4 Be7
White now retreats his knight in order to have pressure on the d6-pawn and hence force Black to recapture 11...gxf6.
10.Nf3 b5 11.Bxf6 gxf6
Compared to our previous games with the Kozul Variation (like Grischuk-Lupulescu or Saric-Vovk), the white knight is on f3 instead of d4 and the black bishop is on e7 instead of f8. One may wonder whether it wouldn't be useful for Black to have made an extra move (like ...Qb6, for example) and have the bishop still on f8 (so that this bishop can be deployed to the active h6-post)? The answer is yes, such a development would indeed have been useful for Black. However, in the Kozul Variation White does not voluntarily play Nf3 while here White has to if he wants to force the recapture with the pawn on f6. To expect White to voluntarily play Nf3 in the Kozul Variation is a bit of wishful thinking.
12.Kb1 Qb6
We have a typical Classical Rauzer position with doubled f-pawns, and this is a good moment to explain the basic plans. White would like to force Black to play ...e5 at the wrong (for Black) moment and seize control of the d5-square (and also the f5-square). How is White going to achieve this? White will push f5, followed by g3, Bh3, and then further increases the pressure on Black's e6-pawn by taking fxe6. After Black recaptures ...fxe6, White moves the Nc3 to the f4-square (Nc3-e2-f4) and often (as Black will likely look for activity and move his Nc6) place his other knight on d4. Black will not be able to conveniently defend his e6-pawn and will be forced to react by weakening his pawn structure. This plan does not sound very complicated, so how is Black supposed to react? He needs to counter with a pawn break - normally either ...f5 or ...d5. This usually amounts to a (temporary) pawn sacrifice. The position opens up and the power of Black's bishop pair is unleashed! Black should also utilize the c-file and he has knight sacrifice motifs too. My impression is that Black needs to be inventive and time his pawn breaks well. The objective assessment is probably 'dynamically balanced'. Let's see how this works in practice.
13.f5 O-O-O 14.g3
White follows the above-mentioned plan; Black wants to get active.
14...Na5!?
Readers interested in the opening theory of this line, please take note! This little- played move is worth investigating and in my opinion gives Black adequate counterplay.
14...Kb8 is more often played and is according to current theory Black's main move
here.
15.Bh3 Nc4
16.Qe2!
White takes away a square from his own knight (the Nc3-e2-f4 plan), but for a good reason! On e2, the queen hits the Nc4 and (temporarily) stops ...b4. Let's see what happens if White plays the seemingly more logical 16.Qe1. First, Black gets ready to utilize the c-file and moves his king to a better square: 16...Kb8. Now White hits on the e6-weakness: 17.fxe6 fxe6. analysis diagram A) White can try the standard knight manoeuvre to f4: 18.Ne2? but won't get it done due to tactical reasons: 18...d5! (a typical pawn break/sacrifice) 19.exd5 e5
20.Bxd7 Rxd7 21.g4 (White controls the light squares but his king will be left
without defenders!) 21...Rc8 22.Ng3. analysis diagram Now Black has a typical sacrifice to destroy White's pawn defences: 22...Ba3!
23.bxa3 Nxa3+ 24.Kb2 Rxc2+ 25.Ka1 b4! and White is helpless against various
threats, 26... b3 being the most scary; B) 18.Nd4 b4 19.Nce2 analysis diagram Black is forced to react, but he does not mind because he has a great (typical) pawn break: 19...f5! 20.exf5 e5 21.Ne6 Bxe6 22.fxe6. analysis diagram Black has a great position and can choose between the sacrificial option and 'easy play': 22...Na3+ (the 'easy' option is 22...d5. Black is only a pawn down and has a great (advancing) pawn centre and plenty of threats, which is definitely more than 'just compensation') 23.bxa3 bxa3+ 24.Kc1 Rc8. White is under a severe attack, though your engine will tell you that he is likely to survive after 25.Rd2.
16...Rhe8
Defending e6 and bringing the rook into play. 16...Kb8 now works less well for Black and in the following variations we see the power of White's forces: 17.fxe6 fxe6 18.Nd4, hitting the pawn on e6 while Black does not have a convenient pawn break. He has to opt for defence: 18...Qc5 19.Rd3! (now White does threaten to take on e6) 19...Qc8 analysis diagram
20.Rhd1! (preparing an exchange sacrifice) 20...Ne5 21.a4! (opening the queenside
after which Black's king becomes a target; here we see motifs similar to Saric-Vovk)
21...Nxd3 22.Rxd3 b4.
analysis diagram White continues his power play: 23.Bxe6! Bxe6 24.Nd5!. White is a full rook down, but the black king has no defenders. A) 24...Bg4 25.Qf1 Bh3 does not save Black due to 26.Qf3 Bg4 27.Qf4 Rhe8
28.Nxb4 and White has a winning attack;
B) 24...Rhe8, trying to keep the material, does not help since Black's king is too weak. However, a precise execution is needed: 25.Nxe6 Qxe6 26.Rb3! Rd7 27.Nxb4 Rb7 28.Nc6+ Kc7 29.Rc3 and White wins; C) Best for Black is to return material and accept an inferior position with 24...Bxd5
25.exd5 Rhe8 26.Nc6+ Ka8 27.Nxe7 when White is only an exchange down and has
full domination. White now puts extra pressure on e6:
17.Nd4 Bf8 18.Qh5 b4
Now the other knight goes to f4, putting extra pressure on e6.
19.fxe6 fxe6 20.Nce2 Kb8 21.Nf4
A critical moment! White has accomplished his plan. Black cannot defend his e6- pawn and cannot say 'pass' either, so he needs to find a tactical remedy. Ivan Salgado comes up with a beautiful idea which unfortunately doesn't work against White's best defence. Black takes the right decision to go for a pawn break!
21...f5!
Black did have the alternative to opt for a sacrificial motif that is useful to remember: 21...Rc8!?. White has to grab the pawn: 22.Bxe6 Bxe6 23.Nfxe6. analysis diagram The Nd4 is a defender and in order for Black's tactical idea to work it needs to be removed: 23...Rxe6! 24.Nxe6. Now Black has his tactic: 24...Na3+ 25.bxa3 (White could change his mind and agree to a draw with 25.Ka1 Nxc2+ 26.Kb1 Na3+)
25...bxa3+ 26.Kc1.
analysis diagram There is no mate - Black doesn't even come close, so he should try to get his material back: 26...Qe3+! 27.Rd2 Qc3 28.Kd1! Qa1+ 29.Ke2 Qxh1 with a very unusual position in which the silicon brain prefers White.
21...d5?? is a wrong break as it simply loses material for nothing after 22.exd5.
22.exf5
22...Nxb2!?
An exclamation mark for the courage and beauty of the idea; a question mark because this sacrifice should not have worked. The position is objectively equal; we have a dynamic balance. The lines are very sharp and it's easy to err. There are entire labyrinths of variations here. I will give some variations that look plausible to me for humans to play - while, of course, I did check the position with Stockfish 15 and 'equal' is the correct verdict. This position is also excellently suited to use as an excellent calculation exercise. A) It is good to note that 22...Bg7? doesn't work due to 23.fxe6 Bxd4 when Black is not in time to execute his 24... Na3+ threat: analysis diagram

however White has full domination and a safer king. White has a winning advantage; B) Black's best move is 22...e5. 41.Qxe4?
The wrong decision! The queens should have been kept on the board: 41.Qf2!. Both kings are weak, while one of the basic rules with similar material imbalances says that minor pieces have better coordinating chances vs rook & pawns if there are more pieces on the board, Most probably Black's best option is a draw by perpetual check with 41...Qb4+
42.Kd3 Qb5+.
41...Rxe4 42.Bf5 Re7
White had probably underestimated this ending. His basic problem is that he is unable to trade the kingside pawns. At some stage (as happened in the game) Black will sacrifice an exchange to get three passed pawns vs the knight and White will be unable to stop the pawns. Salgado's plan is clear and the execution does not need any more comments.

Chapter 2 The Maroczy Bind The Maroczy Bind is one of the oldest Sicilian set-ups. I have selected six games for this chapter to explain what I consider to be the important aspects (strategic points) of this complex opening. Let's start with the 'Classical Maroczy Bind', examining positions that are favourable for Black and those that are favourable for White. In the first diagram we have a position Black is happy to achieve. The first important aspect is that Black's knight is safely placed on the c5-outpost; it will take White quite some time to conveniently hit it with b3-b4. Black's pieces are well coordinated and ready to take advantage of the mobile pawn structure, creating strong counterplay by pushing ...f5. Black can easily take over the initiative in such a position. In the second diagram we have a position White is happy to get. Black's knight is stuck on e6, White controls the c5-square (making it very difficult for Black to regroup), White has a space advantage and easy play while Black struggles to create counterplay. Our first two games illustrate the above-mentioned aspects very well. A straight attack for White Game 8 Bent Larsen Tigran Petrosian Santa Monica 1966
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 g6 5.Be3 Bg7 6.c4 Nf6 7.Nc3 Ng4 8.Qxg4
Nxd4 9.Qd1 Ne6 10.Qd2 d6 11.Be2 Bd7 12.O-O O-O
13.Rad1
By developing the Ra1 to d1, White aims for a strategy combining a kingside attack with space dominance. Just like in the next game, Keres-Petrosian, the question whether Black is able to secure the c5-outpost for his knight plays a very important role here! An immediate kingside attacking idea has also been tried at top level: 13.f4 Bc6
14.f5 (on 14.Bf3 Black can choose: 14...Nc5 (14...Qa5 is a reasonable alternative)
and now White can opt for a pawn sacrifice with 15.b4!? Bxc3 16.Qxc3 Nxe4 with sharp play) 14...Nc5 15.Bf3. Black has to secure his knight's outpost: 15...a5! 16.Bh6 (Black's king is safe here - White will not get anything close to a Dragon attack) 16...Qb6 17.Bxg7 Kxg7
18.Kh1 f6 and Black had a good position and went on to win in Spassky-Savon,
Moscow 1973. White can certainly consider the immediate 13.b4 a5 14.a3, to control c5, when Black has no dynamic follow-up. For 13.Rac1 see the next game, Keres-Petrosian.
13...Bc6
Petrosian probably did not play 13...a5 because he was worried about White starting an attack with 14.f4 Bc6 15.Bf3 Nc5 analysis diagram
16.e5! Bxf3 17.Rxf3 b6 and now White can afford the violent 18.f5!. The position
opens up and White has a strong attack: 18...Bxe5 (also 18...dxe5 19.Qe1 Qe8
20.Nd5 will not end well for Black) 19.Bd4.
14.Nd5
An easy way for White to get a clear advantage here is to immediately deprive Black from the c5-outpost by playing 14.b4!. Black is doomed to passive defence; his knight on e6 is clumsy here. Trying to conquer c5 for his knight with 14...a5? leads to a disaster after the simple 15.b5 Bd7 16.Na4 and in a higher sense White has a winning advantage. It is interesting that two years prior, Bent Larsen played the same position as Black (!!), getting a good game and winning in the end. His opponent did not go for 14.b4!; it would be interesting to know why, playing White two years later, Bent did not go for 14.b4! here and also why Petrosian did not copy Larsen's play - for psychological reasons?
14...Re8
In the mentioned game, Larsen immediately secured the c5-outpost here with
14...Nc5! 15.f3 a5.
Black's situation improved further as White now made the wrong trade: 16.Bd4? when Larsen showed an instructive strategic plan: 16...Bxd4+ 17.Qxd4 e5!. Though the engines give zeros, in human play Black has the better chances in my opinion. White's Be2, locked up by its own pawns, is inferior to Black's excellently placed knight: 18.Qd2
18...Ne6 (Black could have opted for a good strategic trade with 18...Bxd5 with an
excellent position after 19.Qxd5 Ra6) 19.Ne3 Qb6. Black had a pleasant position and went on to win in Porat-Larsen, Amsterdam 1964. In our game, White starts immediate kingside action.
15.f4
White would have obtained a clear advantage with 15.b4!. Black's knight is deprived of the square c5 while the f5 pawn push is coming, with an attack.
15...Nc7?
This passive idea certainly does not help Black. Most likely Petrosian rejected 15...Nc5 because he did not like 16.e5, but still... for better or worse, this was the decision Black had to take here. It's good to know that after 16.Bf3 Black has an equalizing tactical motif that is useful to remember:
16...Nxe4! 17.Bxe4 e6=.
16.f5! Na6
Black is busy with his knight pirouette, achieving nothing. White probably felt the time was right to move to the execution phase and went for the direct:
17.Bg4
Simple 17.b4! was (in a higher sense) leading to a winning advantage. Black does not have the way to activate his knight. In reality White is a piece up on the kingside and will crush Black in attack!
17...Nc5!
Now Black is (very much) back in the game.

The importance of the c5-square Game 9 Paul Keres Tigran Petrosian Bled/Zagreb/Belgrade ct 1959
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 g6 5.c4 Bg7 6.Be3 Nf6 7.Nc3 Ng4 8.Qxg4
Nxd4 9.Qd1 Ne6 10.Qd2 d6 11.Be2 Bd7 12.O-O O-O In my opinion, White has a clear advantage here if Black does not manage to secure c5-outpost for his knight. Without the knight on c5, Black is unable to create counterplay in these positions.
13.Rac1
White has a different set-up here starting with 13.Rad1 - see the previous game Larsen-Petrosian.
13...Bc6
An important moment!
14.Rfd1
This logical rook development is actually a mistake! Black now secures the c5- outpost for his knight and gets the type of position he was aiming for. Precise was 14.b4! with a clear advantage - more space, different plans. Black has no counterplay and is doomed to passivity: A) Fighting for the c5-square with 14...a5? leads to a disaster after 15.b5 Bd7
16.Na4. Black cannot coordinate his pieces at all, and White has a winning
advantage. For the reader to get an idea how bad Black's situation is, I will give the following line: 16.Rfd1 b6 17.Na4 Nc5 18.Nxc5 bxc5 19.a4. White has a passed pawn and more space and obviously should have an advantage here. The question is, how much of an advantage do you think Stockfish gives? The answer is almost plus three!; B) The pawn grab 14...Bxc3? 15.Qxc3 Bxe4 leads to suicide after 16.Bh6 Re8
17.Bg4 f5 18.Rfe1 and Black soon gets mated;
C) Probably Black has to opt for something like 14...b6. White has an easy advantage here and many good ways to continue; for example, 15.Nd5 or 15.h4.
14...Nc5 15.f3
Now Black secures his Nc5 outpost!
15...a5!
Suddenly Black has achieved his initial objective and he has a clear plan: b-pawn to b6, rook moves to d7 via a7, and then it will be time to push the kingside pawns: ...e6 followed by ...f5, with good play. White will be just playing around achieving nothing while Black will execute this plan. Checking with a computer engine may give you a wrong impression here. The engines are very happy with White's position, but in human play matters are not clear. Black wants to (and ultimately will) execute the mentioned plan, so what is White supposed to do here? Well, he has a couple of ideas to explore: a) prepare a3, followed by b4 and kick the Nc5 out; b) improve the Be2 by playing Bf1, g3 and Bh3; c) consider bold action pushing the g-pawn (g4).
16.b3 Qb6
Immediately starting with 16...b6 was the alternative.
17.Nb5
17.Nd5 was an alternative as after 17...Bxd5 (if Black plays 17...Qd8, White can
consider the above-mentioned plans) 18.cxd5 Qb4 the queens will be swapped and White will have an advantage in ending... the only question is how big. Other options are 17.Kh1 Qb4 18.Nd5 Bxd5 19.cxd5 Rfc8 or 17.Kf1.
17...Rfc8 18.Bf1
Black now decides to start the mentioned regrouping plan:
18...Qd8! 19.Qf2
19.g3 deserved attention.
19...Qe8 20.Nc3 b6 21.Rc2 Qf8 22.Qd2 Bd7 23.Nd5 Rab8 24.Bg5
The bold 24.g4!? was an option.
24...Re8 25.Re1
Let's suppose White wants to call it a day with 25.Nc7 Red8 26.Nd5 - well, Black can keep playing on with 26...f6 27.Be3 e6 28.Nf4 Bc6.
25...Rb7 26.Qf2 Bc6 27.Qh4
Black now executes the last phase of his regrouping plan:
27...f6! 28.Be3 e6! 29.Nc3
In the past 15 moves, Black has improved his position a lot while White's pieces are still more or less in the same positions(!). Black is ready for the ...f5 break, taking over the initiative. Note the importance of the stable Nc5 in all this!
29...Rd7
The immediate 29...f5 was certainly possible since Black pieces are well coordinated, e.g. 30.exf5 exf5 or 30...gxf5.
30.Bd4
Petrosian judges that the moment for the pawn break has come:
30...f5! 31.exf5 gxf5 32.Rd2
Black is happy to trade the dark-squared bishops.
32...Bxd4+ 33.Rxd4 Rg7 34.Kh1 Rg6 35.Rd2
Black has attacking potential here, his king is safer, the pawn on d6 is an 'academic weakness' and White's Bf1 is badly placed (dominated by the Nc5).
35...Rd8
Black could have played more directly with 35...Qg7 and ...e5 to follow.
36.Red1 Rd7 37.Qf2 Qd8 38.Qe3
After quite a lot of preparatory moves, Black decides to push his e-pawn.
38...e5
39...f4 looks unpleasant, so White decides to act:
39.f4
How should Black react? Uncharacteristically, Petrosian committed a serious strategic blunder:
39...e4?
Black kills his own bishop and kills his own attack. Petrosian must have overestimated the ...Nd3 jump ideas. Black had to increase the tension with 39...Qh4! when White's situation is not easy as after 40.Qf2 (40.fxe5 allows a strong attack: 40...Rh6 41.Qg1 dxe5 42.Rxd7 Nxd7; Black's king is safe, he has dangerous mobile pawns, the Bc6 is well placed while the Bf1 is doomed to passivity, e.g. 43.Nd5 f4 with ...Nc5, ...Rg6 to follow....Black is clearly better) Black can choose between grabbing a pawn and playing for the initiative with 40...Qh5. After 40...Qxf4 41.Qxf4 exf4 Black may have lousy pawns but one of them is extra. White faces an uphill struggle for a draw.
40.Ne2
As a result of Black's mistake, the situation has changed drastically. Black's d6- weakness is no longer 'academic' - now it is simply a weak pawn (just like f5)! White had to immediately target Black's pawn weaknesses with 40.Nb5! when he has a large advantage. The transaction 40...Bxb5 41.cxb5 does not help Black as the 'stupid' Bf1 goes to c4 and suddenly becomes a monster bishop, while the 'stable' Nc5 will have to leave its post as White will play a3 followed by b4.
40...Rdg7 41.Nd4 Bd7
Now finally White decides to chase away the Nc5 from its post:
42.a3 Qa8 43.Kg1
43.Nb5 led to White's advantage.
43...h5 44.Rb1 h4 45.Rbb2 Rg4 46.Rf2
White is ready to push 47.b4 with a clear advantage. Black needs a strong tactical resource here!
46...Qd8! 47.b4 Rg3!
To come up with this sacrifice, Petrosian needed to envision the pretty mating net that we are about to see. Keres takes a principled decision, accepting the sacrifice.
48.hxg3
White had an alternative in 48.Qe1 axb4 49.axb4 Nd3 50.Bxd3 Rxd3 51.Rfd2 with unclear play.
48...hxg3
The critical moment!
49.Rfd2?
In order to save His Majesty, White needed to return the material:
49.Rf3! Qh4 and now:
A) 50.Rxg3 Rxg3 51.Qe1 Na4. analysis diagram The position is equal but White needs a precise tactical defence: 52.Re2! Qxf4
53.Nxf5! Bxf5 54.Rf2! Qh4! (54...Qe3 leads to a drawish endgame: 55.Qxe3 Rxe3
56.Rxf5=)
analysis diagram White seems to have been tricked. 55.Rxf5?? loses the queen to 55...Rxg2 so Black is a piece up. But White saves the game using Black's weak king and hanging pieces: 55.Qd1! Qf6 (55...Nc3 keeps an extra piece but 56.Qxd6 holds). Now White gets his material back with equality by 56.Qxa4 or 56.Qd5+. B) The less human 50.Be2 also leads to approximate equality: 50...exf3 (the fancy
50...Qh2+ 51.Kf1 exf3 52.Nxf3 Ne4 does not create a real threat: White can calmly
take 53.bxa5! and Black struggles to find a good move as 53...bxa5?? blunders into the simple 54.Rb8+ Kh7 55.Nxh2 gxh2 56.Qh3+) 51.Nxf3 analysis diagram
51...Qh8! (Black wants to play 52...Rh7) 52.Qd4! Ne6 53.Qf6 Rh7 54.Qxh8+ Kxh8.
Now we get a few more trades, 55.Rd2 Nxf4 56.Bf1 Bc6 57.Rxd6 Bxf3 58.gxf3, leading to a probable draw. The 'computer move' 49.Be2 transposes after 49...Qh4 50.Rf3.
49...Qh4 50.Be2
White can no longer be saved as 50.Nf3 loses to 50...exf3 51.gxf3. analysis diagram Black is now only an exchange down while his mating attack continues... still, precision is needed: 51...Rh7! 52.Bg2 Re7! 53.Qd4 Re1+ 54.Bf1 and now the knight needs to join the party: 54...Ne6! 55.Qd5 (or 55.Qxd6 Nxf4). analysis diagram It would be wrong to think that any move wins, as only one move wins! 55...Be8
56.bxa5 Kg7! with 57...Nxf4 and the mating net is closed (56...Bf7? is wrong due to
the simple 57.Qxd6 and if 57...Nxf4 there will be 58.Qd8+).
50...Rh7 51.Kf1
And this instructive game concluded elegantly:
51...Qxf4+!! 0-1
The third game shows a strategically interesting plan of Bent Larsen's which is hardly used nowadays. It doesn't really work provided White reacts well. Larsen's dark-square domination Game 10 Anatoly Karpov 2710 Bent Larsen 2565 Brussels 1987
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 g6 5.c4 Bg7 6.Be3 Nf6 7.Nc3 Ng4 8.Qxg4
Nxd4 9.Qd1 Ne6 In the 1980s, Larsen developed an interesting dark-square domination concept for Black here: keeping the king in the centre, starting with the early queen sortie to a5 and being ready to place his queen in the middle of the board e5-square. The concept had practical success against the world's very best players. However, if White knows what to aim for, Black can get into serious trouble. I will give four examples of top players (Karpov, Kramnik, Anand and Short) reacting as White. Anand was the only one whogot it right! Let's see and try to draw some conclusions.
10.Qd2
A game of Nigel Short playing White (vs Larsen) went 10.Rc1 Qa5 11.Be2 b6 12.0-
0 Bb7.
Already a critical position! What can be Black's problem here? Answer: his exposed queen. White should take action and harass the queen, at the price of a pawn if needed! One 'rule' is that a queen trade is okay for Black in this line. A) Instead of taking immediate action, White played a standard constructive move:
13.f3, and now Larsen went for his dark-square domination plan: 13...g5! 14.a3 (the
pawn sacrifice 14.Nd5 Bxb2 is not clear at all here) 14...Qe5. Due to Black's control of the dark squares, his queen is safe in the middle of the board. 15.Qd2. Black has already decided to keep his king in the centre and takes more space on the kingside: 15...h5! 16.Rfd1 d6. Not being able to disturb Black's queen, White takes the correct decision to push on the queenside: 17.b4 h4 18.Nd5 Kf8 19.Bf1 (probably best was to be consistent and continue 19.a4!) 19...Bc6. Black is controlling a lot of (dark) central squares, however now it's not easy to find a clever follow-up. It would be nice to make a couple of good trades - which is exactly what Nigel Short assists his opponent in doing: 20.Qd3 (the correct decision was 20.Qf2, preparing Rd2 followed by a4, and here we see another drawback of Black's concept: he has difficulty improving his position while White has a plan;
20.b5!? Bb7 21.a4 also deserves consideration) 20...Nf4. Now White makes the
wrong trades: 21.Nxf4? (it was not too late to change his mind and retreat with
21.Qd2) 21...gxf4. White now helps Black with another trade: 22.Bd4?! (22.Bf2)
22...Qxd4+ 23.Qxd4 Bxd4+ 24.Rxd4 Rh5.
Capablanca's rule was: 'Do not have pawns on the squares of your bishop' - have a look at White's bishop here! Black went on to win this ending in Short-Larsen, Brussels 1987; B) Much more to the point was 13.a3!. White wants to play 14.b4, embarrassing Black's queen, and Black is already in a rather unpleasant situation. B1) If 13...h5 (Black wants to play 14...g5, securing his queen on e5, but he won't get the time) 14.b4! Qxa3 15.Nd5 White has considerably more than just compensation - compare this to Anand-Ljubojevic later in the comments; B2) If 13...g5, trying to follow the same strategy Larsen did in the game, White has a pleasant choice between a temporary pawn sacrifice and using the fact that his f- pawn is still on f2 and play 14.Bg4. After 14.Nd5! Bxb2 Black's queen is badly placed: 15.Bd2 Qc5 16.Rc2 Be5 17.Bb4 and Black is in bad shape; B3) Whether he likes it or not, probably Black has to accept White's pawn sacrifice and part with his dark-squared bishop: 13...Bxc3 14.Rxc3 Bxe4 15.Re1. Black's queen is still exposed and he still has to castle, while White's pieces coordinate excellently... White has plenty of compensation here.
10...Qa5 11.Rc1 b6
This game was played in the same tournament as Short-Larsen, but this one was played first (played in the first round of the tournament). Karpov also takes a slow approach.
12.Be2
Vishy Anand, playing the same position ten years later (in a rapid game) did much better: 12.Bd3 Bb7 13.O-O. Ljubo knew Larsen's games and copied his plan: 13...g5. Anand takes the right approach: harass Black's queen and don't care if this involves a pawn sac: 14.a3! h5 15.b4!. Ljubo says: 'if I have to suffer, better to do so with a pawn up'. 15...Qxa3 (15...Qe5 was an option; however after 16.Nd5 White has a considerably better version of Short-Larsen; he will follow up with 17.a4 and open up the queenside, with a clear advantage. The position is similar to the game where White's a-pawn is still on the board) 16.Nd5 Qb2 17.Rc2 Qe5. Black has grabbed a pawn and his queen is, for the time being anyhow, safe in the centre. Black's problem? His queenside will collapse. 18.Ra2 h4 19.h3. Black understands that he won't be able to defend his queenside, so he hopes to create some threats to White's king: 19...Rg8 (if 19...Bxd5? 20.exd5 Nf4 21.Bxb6 Black starts losing material on the queenside without any threats to show for it) 20.Rd1 Bh8 (Black's threats are easy to stop) 21.Be2! Bc6 22.Bf3 Kf8. This was a rapid game, so even for a player of Anand's stature it's not easy to find the best execution. 23.b5! was White's quickest road to victory here (23.Qc2 was played in the game, White went on to win in Anand-Ljubojevic, Monaco 1997)
23...Bxd5 (23...Bb7 loses to 24.Nxe7) 24.exd5 and White wins on the queenside, e.g.
24...Nc5 25.Bxc5 bxc5 26.d6 Re8 27.Rxa7 etc.
12...Bb7 13.f3
Larsen comes up with his concept of gaining space on the kingside:
13...h5 14.O-O
In a rapid game, Kramnik took a cautious approach (not trying to harass Black's queen but trading the queens instead) and got nothing: 14.b3 h4 analysis diagram
15.Nd5?! (this only equalizes) 15...Qxd2+ 16.Kxd2 with a balanced game, later
drawn in Kramnik-Ljubojevic, Monaco rapid 1994. Larsen continues with his plan:
14...g5 15.Rfd1 d6
Now, similar to Kramnik, Karpov makes a wrong trade!
16.Nd5?
The queen trade helps Black here. Karpov probably overestimated his queenside pawn advance, but it would have had been considerably more efficient with queens on the board. Similar to Anand, White had to go for 16.a3! followed by b4, not being afraid to sacrifice a pawn in the process.
16...Qxd2 17.Rxd2
The position is now balanced. Black places his dark-squared bishop in the middle of the board and puts his kingside pawns on dark squares.
17...Be5 18.b4 Rc8 19.a4 h4 20.Bf1 f6 21.Ra2
White's dark-squared bishop is an important piece for his queenside activity, so it makes sense for Black to trade it.
21...Bd4! 22.Kf2 Kf7 23.a5
We now get trades, Black doing well in the ensuing N vs B positions.
23...Bxd5 24.exd5 Bxe3+ 25.Kxe3 Nf4
Black is happy with his N vs B here.
26.Kd2
White does not get a free hand to target Black's queenside.
26...Rc7! 27.axb6 axb6 28.Ra6
Black decides to play actively:
28...Rhc8
28...Rb8 was also good enough.
29.Rxb6 Nxd5 30.Rb5 Nf4 31.Ra5
Now Black improves his knight, targeting White's c4 weakness:
31...Ng6!
White has to be careful as Black is taking over.
32.c5 Ne5 33.Rc3 dxc5 34.bxc5 Rb8
Black's knight is strong while White's passer is not going anywhere.
35.Bb5 Rd8+ 36.Ke2
36...Nc6
Black decides to call it a day. He has a pleasant position here and could have continued without risk for a few more moves with 36...f5 or 36...e6 followed by ...f5, ...Kf6.
37.Bxc6 Rxc6 38.g3 Rd4=
Now we have a drawn rook ending.
39.Rb5 hxg3 40.hxg3 Rd5 41.g4 Rc7 42.Ke3 e6 43.Rc2 Ke7 44.Rc3 Kf7 45.Rc2
f5 46.gxf5 exf5 47.Kf2 Kg7 1⁄2-1⁄2 White plays a Dragon-type set-up A modern development in the Maroczy Bind (and not surprisingly, one the computer engines love!) is that White assumes the set-up on the kingside that is usually employed against the Dragon. Here White has his pawn on c4 instead of c2, making Black's counterplay more difficult. I have picked three modern games that show the problems Black might be facing. In Navara-Jakovenko we see that the g4-push can be dangerous for Black even when the queens are traded! In the other two games, Berdnarskiy-Vakhidov and Caruana- Carlsen, we see White's attacking potential although in both games White does not take advantage of it. A Dragon-like decision Game 11 David Navara 2707 Dmitrij Jakovenko 2698 Jerusalem 2019
1.c4 c5 2.Nf3 Nf6 3.Nc3 Nc6 4.d4 cxd4 5.Nxd4 g6 6.e4 Nxd4 7.Qxd4 d6 8.Be3
Bg7 9.f3 O-O 10.Qd2 Be6 11.Rc1 Qa5
12.b3
In the early days of this variation, it was considered that White was able to force a great ending here by playing 12.Nd5. A) The ending is indeed bad for Black: 12...Qxd2+? 13.Kxd2. Black is forced to part with his bishop: 13...Bxd5 14.cxd5 Rfc8. White plays it very precisely:
15.Rxc8+ Rxc8.
White has a space advantage plus the bishop pair. He has a large advantage here and in a higher sense a technically won position. In a game by Polugaevsky played more than fifty years ago I found a technically instructive win: 16.g3! (White's bishop will be excellently placed on the h3-c8 diagonal; Black has to be careful not to lose immediately) 16...Rc7 17.Bh3 Nd7 18.Rc1! (good judgement by Polu) 18...Rxc1
19.Kxc1 Nb6 (Black has prevented an immediate loss, but in reality he has only
'postponed' losing the a7-pawn and the game) 20.Kc2 Kf8
21.b3! Ke8 22.a4 Kd8 23.a5 Nc8 24.Bxc8 Kxc8 25.Bxa7 (the ending is easily won
for White) 25...e6 26.Kd3 exd5 27.exd5 Bb2 28.Kc4 Bc1 29.Kb5 Kc7 30.Bb6+ Kd7
31.a6 bxa6+ 32.Kxa6 Bd2 33.Ba5 1-0 Polugaevsky-Ostojic, Belgrade 1969;
B) But then it was discovered that Black had the strong reply 12...Qxa2!, leading to a dynamic balance: 13.Nxe7+ Kh8 14.Bd4. Due to White's uncastled king Black has enough counterplay: 14...Rae8
15.Nd5 (Magnus Carlsen showed an interesting idea for White in an internet game:
15.Qc3!? Nh5 16.Bxg7+ Nxg7 and now White is hoping to catch the black queen:
17.b4 Qa4 18.b5 (well... the queen easily escapes) 18...a5 19.Nd5 Bxd5 20.cxd5
(Black has great counterplay) 20...f5! 21.Ra1 Qb4 22.Qxb4 axb4 23.Bd3 Nh5 with approximate equality in Carlsen-Guseinov, chess.com blitz 2017) 15...Bxd5 16.cxd5 Rc8 17.Be2 Rxc1+ 18.Qxc1. A typical position in which the bishop pair is superior to B+N, however with just B vs N we have an unclear situation: 18...Nd7! 19.Bxg7+ Kxg7 20.Qc3+ Kg8 21.O-O - both sides have something to play for, and a draw was agreed in Polugaevsky- Andersson, Hilversum 1973.
12...Rfc8
Black does not have a threat yet. He needs one more move, 13...a6, in order to threaten 14...b5 and now is the moment for White to take a Dragon-like approach:
13.g4!
Black has nothing better but to go for his regular counterplay plan:
13...a6
After a move like 13...Rab8 White gets a clear advantage with the easy plan 14.h4 h5

14...Qxd2+ 15.Kxd2 Nd7
White's dilemma here is: how to push the pawns?
16.Rg1

17.Be2 Kh8

19...Ne8?
The wrong decision.

20.Bxg7?!

22.Rxh7! blow.
20...Nxg7

21.Nd5
21.Kb1 would have been the principled decision here. White does not need to worry

indeed look great, but how great? Well... in Stockfish language White is on plus five here!)
21...Bxd5 22.Qxd5+ Qxd5 23.Rxd5 Ne6
White has spoilt a promising attacking position and turned it into a somewhat worse ending. Black's knight is superior to White's bishop here, while the weakness of the g5-pawn is another worry. From the middlegame perspective, the rest of the game is of little interest to us. I will give just a few comments.
24.Kd2 Rc5 25.Rg1 Ra6
25...Nf4 26.Rxc5 dxc5 looked promising for black.
26.Rg4

30...Ne6 31.Rg4 Ra8 was more precise.
31.Bd1?
The decisive mistake.
31.Bg4! would have kept White in the game as after 31...Re5 32.Bc8! he has
adequate counterplay.
31...Re5 32.Bxa4 Ne6 33.Rg4 Nxg5
Black's two connected passers will be fast. Vakhidov brings it home safely.

A Dragon-like attack Game 13 Fabiano Caruana 2801 Magnus Carlsen 2877 Saint Louis 2014
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 g6 5.c4 Nf6 6.Nc3 d6 7.f3 Nxd4 8.Qxd4 Bg7
9.Be3 O-O 10.Qd2 a5
Caruana does not want to allow 11...a4 followed by 12...Qa5 and has an instructive plan!
11.b3 a4 12.b4 Be6
Black is hitting on the c4-pawn and seems to have enough counterplay, but White has a nice idea.
13.Rc1 Nd7 14.Be2 Nb6
15.Nb5! a3
In reply to 15...Rc8, White plays 16.Na3 and has a large advantage - he has more space and it is easy to improve his position. Black has no real counterplay. Now Fabiano switches to the other side!
16.Nd4 Bd7
A good time for White to start a Dragon-like attack!
17.h4! h5 18.g4! hxg4
Black has to hit White's knight, and it would have been better to start with it here:
18...e5 19.Nb3 (19.Nf5?! looks flashy but there is no mate after 19...gxf5 20.gxf5
Kh7) and now 19...hxg4 when White has two good options. Your computer engine will like both: the typical Dragon idea 20.h5 and the 'conservative' 20.fxg4, transposing to the game.
19.fxg4 e5
White has an annihilating attack. Black's Nb6 is hanging, limiting any counterplay, and it is actually incredible that Magnus escapes here!
20.Nb3
20.Nf3! was stronger as after 20...Bxg4 21.Ng5 ...
analysis diagram White wins in the attack, e.g. 21...Bxe2 22.Qxe2 Bf6 23.Qg4 Qc8 24.Qg3 Nxc4
25.h5 etc.
20...Bc6 21.Bf3 f5 22.gxf5 gxf5
23.Na5
23.Rg1! leads quickly to a winning advantage: 23...Qxh4+? (23...f4 24.Bf2 does not
solve Black's g-file (weak king) problems) loses to 24.Bf2 Qh3 25.Qg5 Rf7 26.Bh5.
23...f4 24.Bf2 Na4 25.Nxc6 bxc6
26.O-O
26.Rg1!.
26...c5 27.b5 Bf6 28.Kh1
Magnus does not defend in the best way.
28...Nb6?
Better was 28...Ra7!, bringing on a defender a.s.a.p. Instead of playing for mate, Fabiano now got greedy for material and spoiled the win:
29.Rcd1
29.Rg1+ Kh8 30.Bg4 led to a winning attack.

The final mistake. 30.Bg4!, playing for mate, should still have won.

Now we get a drawn opposite-coloured bishops ending.
33.Bxe3 fxe3 34.Be2 Bxh4 35.Rf5 Rxf5 36.exf5 Rd8 37.Rxd8+ Bxd8 38.Kg2 Kg7
39.Kf3 Kf6 40.Ke4 c4 41.Bxc4 Bb6 42.Bd3 1⁄2-1⁄2
Chapter 3 The Hedgehog Hedgehog-type positions can arise from different openings, but predominantly from the English Opening and the Sicilian Defence. This middlegame book is based on
1.e4 systems, so I will deal with Hedgehog-type positions arising from the Sicilian.
Hedgehog position usually arise from either of two well-known Sicilian Variations: the Taimanov and the Kan. The Hedgehog is a complex concept and things can easily go wrong for either side. I have selected eight sample games that show what to my mind are the pros & cons of these positions. My analysis of these games should improve the reader's understanding of the topic (and of the Hedgehog in practical play!). After reading this book, the reader will understand that in my personal opinion, Hedgehog positions arising from the Kan Variation are considerably more dangerous for Black. That said, many great players, including Anatoly Karpov, have opted (as White) to face the Hedgehog set-up in the Taimanov throughout their careers. In Hedgehog positions arising from the Taimanov Variation, I do not like White's knight on the a3-square - it definitely needs to be improved. Diagrams 1 and 2 show White taking action with a Scheveningen-type pawn storm while his knight is still stranded on a3. The Scheveningen pawn storm White has just pushed g4, believing that the Scheveningen-type pawn storm will work even with his knight still on a3. My Stockfish evaluates this diagrammed position as around zero... so it might work - certainly in practical play. However, this concept is rather risky for White as he may simply be 'missing a piece' at some stage. Similar to the first diagram, White is rolling his kingside pawns, not caring about the stranded knight on a3. Here, however, Black has just pushed ...g5 (which is usually an excellent reaction for Black in similar situations - try to remember it!), and we will get tactical play, White will be 'missing a piece' and Black has a great position in this second diagram. The first game I have selected features a somewhat different position, but shows well the trouble White can face when he has stranded knight on a3. The passive knight on a3 Game 14 AR Salem Saleh 2690 Nodirbek Abdusattorov 2661 Sharjah 2022
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 e6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 Nc6
Our Sicilian Hedgehog exploration starts with the Paulsen Variation. In this case, White wants to avoid the Paulsen by opting for a Hedgehog set-up. In my opinion, this Taimanov Hedgehog type is less dangerous for Black than the Hedgehog coming from the Kan Variation, and therefore all other examples in this chapter will arise from the Kan. The main disadvantage of this Taimanov-type Hedgehog is that White's knight lands on a3 and needs time to be brought back into play.
5.Nb5 d6 6.c4 Nf6 7.N1c3 a6 8.Na3
I think the Kan Hedgehog, where White's knight is on the d4-square, is much more dangerous for Black. That said, 12th World Champion Anatoly Karpov favoured this line as White throughout his career, so White's set-up definitely has venom. However, in quite a number of games White lost in this variation, he neglected to bring his Na3 back into play. This recent game is a great example!
8...Be7
For younger readers, it may be interesting to know that in one of the most famous games in this variation Black opted for a gambit: 8...d5?! 9.cxd5 exd5 10.exd5 Nb4
11.Be2 Bc5.
We clearly see the influence of the computer engine on chess preparation as the famous 16th game Karpov-Kasparov from the World Championship Match, Moscow 1985, continued 12.O-O (both teams of seconds who were helping the actors missed that White has a simple solution in 12.Be3! Bxe3 13.Qa4+ Nd7 14.Qxb4 Bc5
15.Qe4+, demonstrated by Karpov a couple of months later in Karpov-Van der Wiel,
Brussels 1986. Interestingly, Van der Wiel managed to hold a draw, but the variation was closed after this) 12...O-O 13.Bf3 Bf5 14.Bg5 Re8 15.Qd2 b5. After a tense fight Kasparov won brilliantly, and I strongly recommend the reader to analyse that game.
9.Be2 O-O 10.O-O b6 11.Be3 Bb7
In some opening manuals you will find 11...Ne5 given as the most precise for Black so as after 12.Qb3 to be able to play 12...Ned7. This set-up with Nf6 & Nd7 is very flexible and was played by quite a number of top players. For many years I also thought that 11...Ne5 was the most precise; however, recent developments (this Abdusattorov game included) shed new light on this.
12.Qb3
12...Nd7
A) In his first outing vs Karpov in this line, Kasparov went for a novelty that worked out badly, but actually the idea is much better than its reputation: 12...Na5!?. Garry's idea found no followers due to the game outcome, but it looks playable for Black: 13.Qxb6 Nxe4 14.Nxe4 Bxe4 15.Qxd8 Bxd8 16.Rad1. And here Black can improve on Kasparov's play with 16...Bf6! (in the game Black probably miscalculated something; after 16...d5? 17.f3 Bf5 18.cxd5 exd5 19.Rxd5 Be6 20.Rd6 Bxa2 21.Rxa6 White was a sound pawn up and soon won in Karpov- Kasparov, Moscow 1984) 17.b3 (17.Bd4 Be7 is about equal) 17...Nc6 18.f3 (White can sacrifice an exchange with 18.Rxd6 Be7 19.c5 Bxd6 20.cxd6 when he has compensation but not more) and now comes the important move 18...Bb2! 19.Nb1 Bxb1 20.Rxb1 (only Black can be better in case of 20.Rxd6 Bxa2 21.Rxc6 Rfb8)
20...Ba3 21.f4 a5. White's queenside majority cannot advance, Black has a stable
position and can play ...Bc5 at any time he chooses, trading the bishops. The position is about equal; B) If Black is willing to adopt the above-mentioned set-up with Nd7/Nf6, he can still reach it by playing 12...Ne5 (this possibility was completely omitted by old theory!) as after 13.Bxb6 (13.f3 Ned7 and we have the standard set-up) 13...Qd7 Black has great compensation for the sacrificed pawn - White's pieces are poorly coordinated here; C) Black also has interesting option in a move that has not been played yet at the time of writing, so strange enough it would be a novelty: 12...Rb8 analysis diagram followed by ...Ba8, with complicated play. The pawn grab is risky and probably not advisable: 13.Bxb6 (13.Qxb6 is not even a pawn grab; after 13...Qxb6 14.Bxb6 Ba8 White has to give his pawn back and Black has excellent play, e.g. 15.c5 dxc5
16.Nc4 Nd4) 13...Qc8 14.Be3 Ba8 15.Qc2. White has lost time on the pawn win.
Black is harmoniously developed, White's Na3 is badly placed. Black opens up the centre and has sufficient compensation: 15...Nb4 16.Qb1 Qb7 (16...d5 also comes into consideration) 17.f3 (17.Bf3? is wrong due to 17...Nc6! 18.b3 Ne5 and Black has a clear advantage) 17...d5 and for the price of only one pawn Black has excellent play. Now, it is logical for White is to develop one of his rooks. Question: which rook to which file?
13.Rfd1
A number of strong players have opted for this move. I would personally prefer
13.Rad1 followed by Nab1, aiming to quickly solve the Na3 problem.
White can also postpone the decision about his rooks and improve his queen first:
13.Qc2 Nc5 14.Qd2 and after a few more moves, 14...Bf6 15.f3 Be5 16.Rf2,
the situation is in a way similar to our game. Black did not choose the right plan here with 16...Qh4. Black's planned queen activity will only give White the time to coordinate his forces (the correct decision was similar to Abdusattorov's in our game: 16...f5! and Black is ready to open up the position while White still needs to redeploy his Na3. After 17.exf5, interestingly Black gets good play with either recapture: 17...exf5 / Rxf5 18.Nd5 Ne7) 17.g3 Qf6
18.Rd1 Qg6
19.Nab1!. White has coordinated his forces and is now ready for action. 19...Rad8
20.f4! Bxc3 21.Nxc3. White has well-coordinated pieces, the bishop pair, and more
space, while Black's queen is 'overdeveloped'. 21...Ne7 22.f5! exf5. White has a winning advantage, but, as usual, precision is needed: 23.exf5! (the game Lupulescu-Nisipeanu, Khanty-Mansiysk 2009, went 23.Bxc5 bxc5 24.exf5 Qf6 and was later drawn) 23...Nxf5 24.Bxc5. Now Black is forced into a piece sacrifice that doesn't work: 24...Nxg3 (24...bxc5?? loses a piece for nothing after the simple 25.Bd3 Bc8 26.Qf4) 25.hxg3 Qxg3+
26.Kf1 Qh3+ 27.Ke1 bxc5.
analysis diagram White probably evaluated this position wrongly and therefore went for 23.Bxc5. Yes, at the moment Black does have three pawns for the piece, but White's pieces are better coordinated and his king is actually quite safe. After 28.Qf4 White has a winning advantage as Black's king will soon become a target.
13...Nc5 14.Qc2 Bf6
15.f3?
White is making seemingly standard Hedgehog moves, but probably he was not aware of Black's plan. I do not know whether Nodirbek knew Mamedyarov's game from 16 years earlier, but Shakhriyar went for exactly the same plan! 15.Rac1 Be5
16.Nab1.
Black is ready to open up the position: 16...f5! 17.f4! (White defends in the best way under the circumstances; after 17.exf5 his king would quickly become a target and Black has a pleasant choice between 17...Rxf5 and the more inventive 17...Ne7)
17...Bxc3 18.Nxc3 fxe4 19.a3 (White will get his pawn back, but Black has excellent
play) 19...e5 20.Nd5
20...Nd4 (this allows a relative simplification into equality. A good alternative was

a4!) 21.Bxd4 exd4 22.Rxd4 Ne6 23.Rxe4 Bxd5 24.cxd5 Nxf4 (Black does not have enough pieces for a real attack on White's king) 25.Bf3 (better was 25.Bg4 Qg5
26.Be6+ with approximate equality) 25...Qg5 26.Ree1 Rf6 27.Kh1 Raf8 28.Be4
Rh6. Black's situation has improved in the last couple of moves, but White is far from getting mated. White now made a terrible blunder, bringing the game to an abrupt end: 29.Rf1?? (he had to go for 29.Qc3) 29...Rxh2+! 0-1, Naiditsch-Mamedyarov, Fügen 2006. White first loses his queen and then more: 30.Kxh2 Qh5+ 31.Kg1 Ne2+
32.Qxe2 Qxe2 33.Bxh7+ Kxh7 34.Rxf8 Qe3+.
15...Be5 16.Bf1
16...f5!
White's knight is still stranded on a3 and Mamedyarov's plan now works much better! Black is clearly better here. The position opens up, and White is in essence a piece down (the Na3). It is difficult when a player has to choose between different bad possibilities. Salem takes a reasonable practical decision:
17.f4
An attempt at some flashy defence like 17.exf5 Rxf5 18.Nd5 fails as the knight is not doing much on d5 and Black has several good ways to achieve a winning advantage:
18...Qh4 (the simple 18...Ne7 should work well too) 19.f4 Raf8 20.fxe5 exd5
21.cxd5 Nxe5 and Black has a winning attack.
17...Bxc3 18.Qxc3 Nxe4 19.Qb3
White has the bishop pair, will get his pawn back, and if he doesn't get mated he will probably survive. White's biggest problem? Playing a complicated position essentially a piece down (the Na3)!
19...Rb8
The immediate attack 19...g5 was probably even stronger.
20.Bxb6
Stockfish suggests trying to bring the knight into play by 20.Nb1 as a better chance to survive. Well, Black has two good options here: 20...Nc5, keeping his extra pawn, or going for an attack with 20...g5.
20...Qh4 21.Qe3
21.g3 is similar to the game situation after 21...Qe7 22.Qe3 and Black opens the
white king's position with either 22...g5 or 22...e5 - a pleasant choice.
21...e5!
As said, 21...g5! was also good.
22.g3 Qf6 23.Bg2 exf4 24.gxf4
24.Qxf4? loses tons of material after 24...Qxb2 25.Qe3 Nc3.
24...Qg6
Now White attemps to finally bring his knight back into play:
25.Nc2
25...Ne5!
Direct and strong. Stockfish prefers 25...Ba8 26.Ne1 Rfe8 and either White loses his bishop (on b6) or he gets mated: 27.Bd4 Nxd4 28.Qxd4 (28.Rxd4 Nc3) 28...Nc3
29.Qxc3 Rxe1+ 30.Rxe1 Qxg2#.
26.Qe2
26.fxe5 loses to 26...f4 followed by ...f3.
26...Ng4 27.Rd3 Nef6
White simply has too many hanging pieces.
28.Bd4 Rfe8 29.Qf1 Nh5 30.Rd2
Black chooses a nice finish:
30...Re4!
30...Qh6 also wins.
31.Re1 Rxf4 32.Qd3 Nf2!
And White resigned. The 'Kan Hedgehog' Diagrammed is the Hedgehog position arising from the Kan Sicilian (this type of Hedgehog may also arise from the English Opening). White's knight is now placed on d4 and does not need to be improved. Black has just opted for one of the typical counterplay plans by pushing ...h5. I have selected three games here and hope they will give the reader a 'good grip' on the right timing of the h-pawn push for Black and how to deal with it as White. This diagram shows the dilemma White often faces. Black has just pushed ...h3 and White must decide how to react. In this position he should take gxh3!. The silicon brain also tends to take the pawn in such situations. Black's h-pawn push Game 15 Veselin Topalov 2737 Rustam Kasimdzhanov 2652 Tripoli FIDE-Wch KO 2004 We have here a top-level example of Black's h-pawn push and a poor reaction to it on White's part.
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 e6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 a6 5.c4 Nf6 6.Nc3 Qc7 7.a3 b6 8.Be3 Bb7 9.f3
d6 10.Be2 Be7 11.O-O O-O 12.Qd2 Nbd7 13.Rac1 Rac8 14.Rfd1 Qb8 15.Bf1 Rfe8
16.Kh1 Bd8
Both sides have been playing typical Hedgehog moves. Black has not shown his hand yet.
