He can still choose between all three standard plans: the h-pawn push, the plan with ...Bc7, ...Kh8, ...Rg8, ...g5, or staying flexible and aiming for a timely ...d5 pawn break. Topalov plays another regular Hedgehog move:
17.Bg1
Now Kasim shows his hand:
17...h5!?
How should White react? As we will also see in the comments to the next game, Dominguez-Andreikin, most white players prefer not to touch the h-pawn. Topalov takes the same 'human' approach as Dominguez did: 'do not touch the h-pawn, do not risk creating a weakness'.
18.Qf2
White's best reaction was 18.h3 when after the logical 18...h4 19.Bh2 Nh5 analysis diagram
20.Nde2! covers the g3-square and makes sure White keeps the dark- squared
bishop: 20...Ne5 21.b3 Be7. As mentioned in a similar situation in the game Dominguez-Andreikin, e3 is a good square for the white queen here: 22.Qe3! and White is ready to push f4. The silicon brain prefers White. In human play I would say the game is unclear.
18...Ne5
Black's pawn could have continued on its journey: 18...h4. Most probably, Rustam did not play this because he was not sure about the position resulting after the following pawn sacrifice: 19.h3 (Black is at least okay after the pawn structure change with 19.Qxh4 Nxe4) 19...Nh5. Now White has to part with his bishop:
20.Bh2 (20.Nce2? is not good due to 20...Ne5 21.b3 b5!)
analysis diagram
20...Ng3+! 21.Bxg3 hxg3 22.Qxg3 Nf6. The bishop pair is important here as Black
will open up the position with the ...d5 break at some stage; Black has sufficient compensation.
19.b3 Ng6 20.b4?
The right approach was to touch the h-pawn: 20.h3! h4 21.Bh2 Nh5 22.Nde2.
20...h4 21.Nb3
Black now accomplishes his plan:
21...h3!
Should White take the black pawn or should he ignore it? The decision is difficult as White is clearly worse in both cases. Veselin decides to ignore the pawn.
22.g3
In an ideal world (it will not happen in this position) White hopes to preserve his pawn structure while at some stage collecting the pawn with Bxh3. Unfortunately for White, he does not get the opportunity to do this. In answer to 22.gxh3 Black has different good options. The most straightforward central pawn break is a good one: 22...d5 (22...Ne5 and later breaking with ...d5 is also good) 23.exd5 exd5. analysis diagram A) White gets devastated if he plays 24.cxd5 Rxc3 (the simple 24...Qa8 followed by ...Nxd5 is also not bad) 25.Rxc3 Nxd5 (White's pieces have poor coordination; Black has a winning advantage) 26.Rcd3 Bh4 27.Qd4 Ngf4 etc.; B) 24.c5 Nd7 (also better for Black are the positions resulting from the thematic pawn sac 24...d4 25.Rxd4 Bc7) 25.Nxd5 Nde5 26.Bg2 Qa8 - White cannot defend on the a8-h1 diagonal and this will prove decisive here.
22...Ne5 23.Rd4
Here Kasimdzhanov made the only mistake in this game, agreeing to a draw by move repetition:
23...Nc6?
Black has a large, close to winning advantage. Best would have been to open up the position further with 23...b5! and White does not have a good way to keep his stuff together. Black's bishop coming to b6 is very unpleasant for White: 24.c5 dxc5
25.Nxc5 Bb6.
analysis diagram White would love to solve the problem of the pin on the a7-g1 diagonal and also finally collect the h3-pawn... but he cannot get it done: 26.Rdd1 (26.Bxh3? loses to
26...Nfd7 27.Rdd1 Nxc5 28.bxc5 Bxc5 29.Qe2 Bxa3) 26...Qa8!. The pressure along
the a8-h1 diagonal has been increased while Black's queen no longer hangs once Black plays ...Nd7 and White takes. White's best option here is to finally grab the annoying pawn: 27.Bxh3 Nfd7. ...Nxc5 is coming, and White is in a bad shape here, while 28.Qd4 only delays the inevitable. Black has a large advantage after 28...f6 or
28...Rcd8 29.Qe3 Nxc5 30.bxc5 Ba5 31.Bg2 Nc4.
23...Qa8, though less forcing, leads to Black's advantage too.
24.Rdd1 Ne5 25.Rd4 Nc6?
1⁄2-1⁄2 White pushes a4, Black responds ...a5 Game 16 Leinier Dominguez Perez 2726 Dmitry Andreikin 2687 Havana 2011
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 e6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 a6 5.c4 Nf6 6.Nc3 Bb4 7.Bd2 Qc7 8.a3 Be7
9.Be3 d6 10.Be2 O-O 11.O-O b6 12.Qd2 Bb7 13.f3 Nbd7 14.Rfc1!?
We do not often see the rooks positioned on a1 and c1 in such positions. White has a specific plan in mind (similar to Agrest-Nisipeanu below).
14...Rac8 15.a4!?
The standard queenside pawn push 15.b4 is certainly an option here. Like in Agrest-Nisipeanu, White wants to push a5 and Black stops this in an unusual way!
15...a5!?
I have rarely seen such a pawn structure - actually I cannot recall any other top game with it! Black stops White's pawn advance at the expense of giving White the b5- square. How important is it? Is White better here? It doesn't look that way. White has the b5-square for his knight, but Black's knight has obtained a very stable post on c5. Black seems to have no problems defending his d6-pawn, so the position is probably balanced. What alternative plans did Black have? Advancing the h-pawn is a standard plan, so let's see how it works here: 15...h5 16.b4. A) If 16.a5 bxa5 17.Nb3, White does accomplish his plan of bringing his knight to a5, however after 17...h4 18.Nxa5 Ba8 analysis diagram Black has adequate counterplay, White does not have a free hand to push his queenside pawns. One way for White to deal with Black's h-pawn advance is to bring his knight to f2:
16.Nd1. Now, if Black wants to, he can also push 16...a5 and have a reasonably good
version of the game; B) 16.b4 h4 (trying to use the fact that White's b4-pawn is not protected and going for a central break with 16...d5 may backfire as White has the strong piece sacrifice
17.Nxd5! exd5 18.cxd5 Qb8 19.Nf5; 16...Ne5 does not worry White as he has
17.Nd1) 17.a5 bxa5 18.Rxa5.
analysis diagram After 18...Ne5! (the immediate 18...h3 allows the nice rook swing 19.Rg5! and it is White who will attack on the kingside!) the position is unclear. Initially, the engine slightly prefers White, however there are many dynamic/tactical ideas: 19.Nd1 (if
19.h3 - White wants to play 20. f4 - Black has his trumps: 19...Nh5! (the pawn grab
19...Nxc4?? blunders the queen: 20.Bxc4 Qxc4 21.Nce2) 20.f4 Ng3! 21.fxe5 dxe5
the whole board is hanging; Stockfish gives zeros) 19...h3 20.g3 (20.gxh3 is possible but quite risky here). analysis diagram In an ideal world, White wants to keep his kingside pawn structure intact and collect Black's pawn with Nd1-f2-h3. 20...Qd7 (Black also has piece sacrifice ideas:
20...Bxe4!? 21.fxe4 Nxe4 22.Qe1 Qb7) 21.Nf2 and now is the time for the central
pawn break 21...d5 with an unclear game. Was 15...h5 an option? Yes, it certainly was. Another possibility was 15...Qb8 and in case of 16.a5 bxa5 17.Rxa5 (White cannot bring his knight to a5 as 17.Nb3? blunders a pawn to 17...Bxe4) 17...Ne5 it is not easy for White to advance further on the kingside; the position is balanced.
16.Ndb5 Qb8
17.Bf4
White wants to take advantage of his knight on b5 and put pressure on the d6-pawn. Anther way to do this was 17.Re1 Rcd8 18.Rad1 Nc5 19.Bf4. analysis diagram The pawn win appears not to bring White any advantage here: 19...Nh5! 20.Bxd6 Bxd6 21.Nxd6 e5 and the Nc5 is well placed while the Be2 locked up by its own pawns; Black is definitely not worse, e.g. 22.Qg5 (or 22.Ncb5 Bc6) 22...Rxd6
23.Qxh5 Rxd1 24.Rxd1 Bc6.
Dominguez most probably rejected 17.Rd1 because matters don't look clear after
17...Nc5.
17...Ne5 18.b3 Rfd8 19.Kh1
Andreikin now decides in favour of one of Black's standard Hedgehog plans: pushing the h-pawn:
19...h5
The alternative was to bring Nf6 to the nice c5 outpost with 19...Nfd7 20.Rd1 Nc5
21.Rab1 when the position is probably about equal.
How should White react to Black's pawn push? What I noticed is that most white players prefer not to touch their h-pawn, and here this is also the case.
20.Bf1
In this and most similar positions, Stockfish has much less trouble 'creating weaknesses' and happily plays 20.h3!. Now, after the most logical follow-up 20...h4
21.Bh2, Black seems to be doing well optically - one knight goes to h5, another to
g6, dark-square control... However, in practice it does not work that well here, for example 21...Ng6 (21...Nh5?? blunders a piece after the simple 22.f4) 22.Qe3! (the white queen is well placed on e3; it prepares the f4-push while also subsequently covering the g3-square) 22...Bc6 23.Rd1. White has a relatively simple plan here: double the rooks to keep Black busy defending the d6-pawn, and then at a convenient moment push f4. Black does not have an easy life here, as counterplay attempts like 23...Nh5 lose a pawn for no compensation after 24.f4 Ng3+ 25.Bxg3 hxg3 26.Bh5 Nf8 27.Qxg3.
20...h4
Now Black is fine. The position is about equal but probably easier to play with Black.
21.Qf2
21.h3 now does not bring the same result as after 21...Nh5 22.Bh2 Ng6
analysis diagram
23.Qf2 (in reply to 23.Ne2, Black has a nice idea in 23...Rc5! and White can easily
get in trouble, e.g. 24.Qe3? Bg5! 25.f4 Bh6 with the threat 26...e5; White's pawns are shaky and the evaluation is minus two!) 23...Ng3+! 24.Bxg3 hxg3 25.Qxg3 Bf6 Black has excellent compensation for his sacrificed pawn.
21...Rc5
Black is getting ready for the typical Hedgehog break ...d5. Could Black immediately go for 21...d5 ? Yes, he could. It does require some good calculation though: 22.cxd5 Nd3! 23.Bxd3 Qxf4 24.Qxb6. analysis diagram At moments like these, a human player often stops calculating and (instead of
21...d5!?) looks for some other moves (like 21...Rc5 in the game - so that b6 will not
be hanging). Black is actually doing pretty well here: 24...Bxd5! 25.exd5 (25.Ne2?? blunders into the simple 25...Rxc1+ 26.Rxc1 Qd2) 25...Nxd5. Black gets his sacrificed piece back, and White is the one who needs to be careful here: 26.Qg1! Nxc3 27.Nxc3 Rxd3 and White bails out into a drawn rook endgame with 28.Nd5! Rxc1 29.Nxe7+ Kf8 30.Qxc1 Qxc1+ 31.Rxc1 Kxe7 32.h3. A draw will be the outcome of this tactical tussle: 32...Kd6 33.Rc8 Rxb3 34.Ra8 etc.
22.Be3 d5!
Black judges well that the central break works and goes for it. For a better understanding of this position it is good to notice that in the event of
22...Ng6!? 23.Bxc5 dxc5, Black has full compensation for the sacrificed exchange.
analysis diagram For example, 24.Rd1 Rf8! followed by ...Nh5 and ...f5.
23.exd5 exd5 24.cxd5
Andreikin ignores the threat to his rook and decides to weaken White's king position.
24...h3! 25.Rd1
Black is fine in the event of 25.Bxc5 Bxc5 26.Qg3 as White has to give his extra exchange back: 26...hxg2+ 27.Bxg2 Nd3 28.Rc2 Nf2+ 29.Rxf2 Qxg3 30.hxg3 Bxf2
31.Kh2 Bc5. Black has suffient compensation for the pawn and is not worse here.
(Of course not 31...Nxd5?? which blunders into a pin and material loss) after
32.Rd1.)
25...Nxd5 26.Bxc5 Bxc5 27.Qg3 Nxc3
The position is sharp, but still approximately equal.
28.Rxd8+?
Most probably a time-pressure blunder.
28.Nxc3 led to a forced sequence of moves with a drawish outcome: 28...hxg2+
(28...Bd4 transposes after 29.f4! hxg2+ 30.Bxg2 Bxg2+ 31.Qxg2 Bxc3= etc.)
29.Bxg2 Bd4 30.f4!.
analysis diagram And we get a peaceful outcome after 30...Bxg2+ 31.Qxg2 Bxc3 32.Rxd8+ Qxd8
33.Rc1 and now a few more pieces leave the board: 33...Bb2 (or 33...Ng6 34.Rxc3
Qd1+ 35.Qg1 Qd5+=) 34.Qxb2 Nd3 35.Qd2 Qa8+ 36.Kg1 Nxc1 37.Qxc1 with a dead drawn queen ending.
28...Qxd8!
Black's threats are obvious, White either suffers decisive material loss or gets mated.
29.Qxe5
Or 29.Nxc3 Qd4.
29...Nd1 30.Ra2 Ne3 31.Nc3 Nxf1 32.Re2 Nd2 33.Qg3 hxg2+ 34.Rxg2 g6 0-1
White pushes the a-pawn, Black counters with his h-pawn Game 17 Evgenij Agrest 2554 Liviu-Dieter Nisipeanu 2584 Batumi Ech-tt 1999
1.c4 e6 2.Nc3 b6 3.e4 Bb7 4.Nf3 c5 5.d4 cxd4 6.Nxd4 d6 7.Be2 Nf6 8.f3 a6 9.Be3
Nbd7 This position can also arise from the Kan Sicilian. If Black is worried about White's a-pawn push, he can delay developing the Nb8 with 9...Be7 10.O-O O-O when 11.a4 doesn't achieve its aim due to 11...Nc6.
10.O-O Be7
And now White goes for a plan similar to Dominguez-Andreikin.
11.a4 O-O 12.a5 bxa5 13.Nb3 Rb8 14.Nxa5 Ba8 15.Qd2
White has achieved his aim: his knight is well placed on a5, his queenside pawns are ready to move. White's plan is simple: push the b-pawn. Black cannot really stop this, so he urgently needs to find something. What is Black's best counterplan here? Answer: pushing the h-pawn. Nisipeanu ultimately understands this, but he loses precious time in the process!
15...Re8?!
On this move or the next, Black had to start with the immediate 15...h5! with sufficient counterplay.
16.Kh1 Qc7?! 17.Rfc1
With his last two moves, Black has not really improved his position, while White is ready to push his b-pawn.
17...Ne5 18.Bd4
Black now decides on the right counterplay; however, precious time has been lost. Black is in trouble here.
18...h5! 19.b4! h4
19...Rxb4?? was bad due to the simple 20.Nb5 axb5 21.Qxb4 and White is an
exchange up with a better position.
20.b5!
Black cannot stop White on the queenside, so for better or worse, he needs to keep pushing his pawn:
20...h3
The critical moment. How should White react?
21.g3?
Agrest reacts in a way similar to Topalov and Dominguez: preserve the structure, collect the pawn on h3 later. Correct was 21.gxh3! when Black has many tactical ideas, but they don't work. White has a large advantage. The pawn will stay on h3 until the end of the game and due to White's king weakness Nisipeanu eventually saves the game.
21...axb5 22.cxb5
Putting the queen on c7 was not a good idea in the first place.
22...Qd8!
Now White creates a strong passer on c6, probably thinking he had a winning advantage.
23.Bxe5 dxe5 24.Qxd8 Rexd8 25.Nc6 Bxc6 26.bxc6
Black saves the game due to White's weak king; the pawn on h3 is doing an excellent job.
26...Rd2 27.Ba6
Or 27.Bf1 Rbb2 28.Ne2 Bd8! 29.c7 Bxc7 30.Rxc7 Rxe2 31.Bxe2 Rxe2 with a draw like in the game.
27...Rbb2
The silicon brain will explain that 27...Ne8 was good as well, but the text is more human.
28.Ne2 Bd6 29.c7 Bxc7 30.Rxc7 Rxe2 31.Bxe2 Rxe2
White's king is 'inside the box' and the extra exchange doesn't matter here.
32.Rf1 g5 33.g4 Kg7 34.Kg1 Rg2+ 35.Kh1 Re2 36.Rc3 Nd7 37.Rfc1 Rf2 38.Kg1
Rg2+ 39.Kh1 Rf2 1⁄2-1⁄2 Fresh ideas for White The diagrammed position comes from the Kan Sicilian. Here we see a relatively new concept that will be more deeply explored! White has just pushed g4 and is starting a Scheveningen-type pawn storm. Do NOT confuse this with the first two introductory diagrams in this chapter as here the situation is VERY different. White's knight is well-placed on d4, so he does not run the risk of 'missing a piece'. White's attack can develop fast here! Game 18 Yasser Seirawan Nick de Firmian Estes Park 1986
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 e6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 a6 5.c4 Nf6 6.Nc3 Qc7
7.Be2!?
The text move is considerably less often played than 7.a3, but to me it is far from clear which move is the most precise. Black has two logical options: a) continue Hedgehog development with 7...b6 or b) try to exploit the fact that White hasn't played 7.a3 and play 7...Bb4. Let's have a look at both.
7...b6
A) 7...Bc5, played by Bronstein, does not look inspiring: 8.Be3 d6 9.O-O Bd7 10.a3 O-O 11.b4 Ba7 12.Rc1 White has a typical Hedgehog position while Black looks passive. White is going to trade the dark- squared bishops (a good trade for White here!): 12...Rc8 13.Qd2 Be8
14.Rfd1 Nbd7 15.Nb3 Bxe3 16.Qxe3 Ne5 17.c5 with an easy, risk-free advantage.
(17.h3 was played in Honfi-Bronstein, Miskolc 1963. White later spoiled his position and even lost.) B) 7...Bb4
8.O-O (8.Qd3 can of course be played - probably transposing to 9.Qd3 below - but
White should not really object to Black grabbing a pawn here) and now: B1) 8...Bxc3 9.bxc3 Nxe4 is asking for trouble after 10.Bf3 Nc5 11.Nb3
11...d6 (11...O-O? leads more or less to a lost position after 12.Nxc5! - 12.Qd4 d6
13.Rd1 was played in Shirov-Fedorov, Riga 2014, where Black eventually survived
- 12...Qxc5 13.Qa4 and Black simply cannot develop). Now White can reach a better ending with a relatively forced sequence: 12.Bf4 e5 13.Nxc5 Qxc5 14.Be3 Qc7
15.Qd5 O-O 16.Rfd1 Nc6 17.Qxd6 Qxd6 18.Rxd6 with play for two results: maybe
Black makes a draw, maybe not; B2) 8...O-O 9.Qd3 d6 10.Bd2. Similar to Bronstein's 7...Bc5, we have a Hedgehog position with the black bishop 'out'. To me, the bishop looks more natural on e7 in these positions. 10...Nbd7 (to
10...Nc6, probably White's best reaction is 11.Nxc6! bxc6 and now he can take
space 12.a3 Bc5 13.b4 Ba7 14.Bg5 with an easy advantage) 11.Nb3 Ne5 12.Qd4 Nc6 13.Qe3 Bd7 14.Rac1 Rac8. White now took the bishop pair with 15.a3 and had an advantage in Naiditsch-Ivanchuk, Danzhou 2017. White has two dangerous 'Sicilian pawn storm plans' here (both a little unusual for the Kan Hedgehog). In both cases Black holds a dynamic balance... if... a computer is playing.
8.Be3
The other option is 8.O-O Bb7 9.Qd3
9...d6 (9...Nc6 leads to a safe advantage for White after 10.Nxc6! Bxc6 11.Be3 Bc5
12.Bxc5 bxc5 13.f4 with d-file pressure and more space, Shomoev-Khismatullin,
Khanty-Mansiysk 2015). A) Now White can start a standard Sicilian pawn storm with 10.f4 Nbd7 11.b4 Be7
12.Be3 O-O 13.Rac1
13...Rac8 (13...Rad8? runs into a textbook tactic: 14.Nd5! exd5 15.cxd5 and White
got his piece back - 15...Qb8 is refuted by 16.Nc6 - and had a clear advantage in Khalifman-Akopian, Minsk 2014). Now we have an interesting situation: White starts a 'Scheveningen Sicilian pawn storm'! 14.g4!. White has five pawns on the fourth rank and yet he isn't overstretched! A1) 14...Qd8 15.g5. Now White has a winning advantage. This is a Hedgehog where the Scheveningen pawn storm works perfectly for White: 15...Ne8 16.Qd2
16...Nc7 (breaks of the 16...d5 type sometimes save Black in similar situations... but
not here! 17.cxd5 Bxb4 18.dxe6 Nc5. Now White first wins an exchange and later probably the game with the tactic 19.Nc6! Qxd2 20.Bxd2 Rxc6 21.e7) 17.a3 Re8
18.Nb3 (Stronger was just continuing the Scheveningen pawn storm with 18.h4!.
White has advanced so many of his pawns and yet Black does not have a good pawn break!) 18...f6 19.h4! Rf8. White understands he needs his Nb3 as an attacking piece: 20.Nd4! fxg5 21.hxg5 and White had a winning attack in Brkic-Gajewski, Jerusalem 2015 (1-0, 43); A2) Black is perhaps not worse if... a computer is playing, after 14...h6! 15.h4 analysis diagram
15...h5! 16.g5 Ng4 17.Bxg4 hxg4 18.Nce2. It looks like 19. Ng3 next will be
slaughter but Black has defensive resources: 18...g6! 19.Ng3 e5!. analysis diagram Now White has several beautiful ideas, all leading to a 'zeros' evaluation: 20.Ndf5! (or 20.h5 exd4 21.Bxd4 Ne5!) 20...gxf5 21.Nxf5. To a human player this looks terribly dangerous for Black but apparently the position is dynamically balanced:
21...Rce8! 22.Nh6+ Kh7 23.f5 and it all ends peacefully with a move repetition:
23...Qc6 24.Qd5 Qc7 25.Qd3 Qc6.
B) An interesting novel idea is 10.Qg3!? Nbd7 (the pawn grab 10...Nxe4?! 11.Nxe4 Bxe4 is at least risky: 12.Re1 Nc6 13.Bf3! Bxf3 14.Qxf3 Rc8. analysis diagram Black's pawn defences need to be destroyed: 15.Nxe6! fxe6 16.Rxe6+ Be7 17.Bg5 and Black is in bad shape; 17...Nd4? loses to 18.Qh5+ g6 19.Rxg6) 11.f3. analysis diagram It looks logical to fianchetto the bishop here: 11...g6 12.Be3 Bg7 13.Rac1 O-O. At first glance, this looks like a normal Hedgehog, but my Stockfish is very enthusiastic for White, evaluating plus one!
8...Bb7 9.f3 d6
It is perhaps useful to mention that 9...Bd6? is a tempo loss as after 10.Qd2 White threatens 11. Ndb5 and Black has nothing better than 10...Be7 (10...Bxh2 loses to
11.O-O-O with many threats). White is simply a tempo up compared to regular lines
and here went for the most aggressive continuation 11.O-O-O Nc6 12.Kb1 Ne5
13.Rc1 and White went on to win in Ivanchuk-Kamsky, lichess.org blitz 2020.
10.O-O Be7 11.Rc1 Nbd7
We have our standard Kan Sicilian Hedgehog with the difference that White has not played a3. If White wants a queenside a3, b4 build up he can still play 12.a3, transposing. However, White can also opt for a much more aggressive set-up!
12.Qe1!? O-O
And now White goes for a Sicilian pawn storm!
13.g4!
White is combining Hedgehog central control with a Sicilian pawn roll. Please note that White can also apply similar plans in lines with 7.a3. It just looks to me that if White is willing to go for such a set-up, this is the best move-order and the most efficient way to do it. The resulting positions are very sharp and easy to get wrong. It started as a standard Hedgehog but now the only thing making this a Hedgehog is White's pawn on c4, otherwise we have an open Sicilian where White applies a bayonet pawn roll on the kingside! Interestingly, there are almost no games here! We have one top-level game from almost forty years ago, then one game between top engines, and not much else - definitely an area to explore further! The first tough decision for Black is whether or not to play ...h6 - never an easy decision in such positions. In our human top-level game and also in the engines' top- level game, Black opted for 13...h6.
13...h6
Allowing White to roll his pawns undisturbed is not a good idea; after 13...Ne8 14.g5 g6 15.h4 Ng7 16.f4 Rfe8 17.f5 exf5 18.Nd5 Qd8 19.exf5 White was clearly better and easily won in Wagner-Sachdev, Biel 2018.
14.h4 Nh7
White gets ready to bring his rook to the h-file.
15.Kg2
White could also roll his pawns differently with 15.Qg3. If Black wants to follow Komodo's type of play with 15...Qd8 (see the next comment) White can continue with 16.Rcd1 Re8 (16...Bxh4 17.Qxd6 is probably better for White) and now: A) Pawn sacrifice ideas like 17.g5 hxg5 18.hxg5 Bxg5 19.f4 are not easy to evaluate: 19...Bf6 20.Rd2 and White's rook will swing to the kingside; my Stockfish evaluates around zeros... not easy to tell; B) 17.Rd2, preparing the g5 push (to sacrifice a pawn under better circumstances) is also an option; C) 17.Bf2 Rc8 18.f4 with interesting play. I would personally prefer to be White, but is a matter of taste. Yasser Seirawan took a drastic step to stop White's pawn roll:
15...g5!?
In a high-level computer game, Komodo went for 15...Qd8. Obviously, these engines are much stronger than me, while to use my weaker engine to analyse top engine games looks stupid. So I will just give some moves of that game: 16.Rh1 Re8
17.Qg3 Ndf8 18.f4 (18.Rcd1 is an alternative)
It looks as if Black will fall victim to White's bayonet pawn roll, but Black has resources: 18...Bf6! 19.g5 hxg5 20.fxg5 (Black can counter 20.hxg5 with 20...Bxd4
21.Bxd4 e5 22.fxe5 Qxg5 with sharp play, my 'weak Stockfish' giving me zeros)
20...Be5 21.Qg4 f5! 22.Qh3 Ng6 with a sharp game ending in a draw, Stoofvlees-
Komodo, TCEC 2020.
16.hxg5 hxg5
Black did stop White's pawn and has also secured himself an outpost on e5; however, it's the h-file that matters! White has an advantage.
17.Rh1 Rfe8 18.Qg3 Ndf8 19.Rh6 Bf6 20.Rch1
Black is not exactly getting mated, but the pressure on the h-file is unpleasant for him here.
20...Be5 21.Qf2 Rab8
Now White improves his knight:
22.Nc2! Bc8 23.Nb4 f6 24.Nd3
Black has to part with his bishop (though doubling White's pawns).
24...Bxc3 25.bxc3 Re7
White improves his knight again:
26.Nb4! Rg7 27.Bd3 a5 28.Nc2
White's knight on d4 will annoying, while playing 28...e5 allows White to bring his knight to e3, targeting the d5- and f5-squares. White definitely has an advantage here, and Black faces unpleasant dilemmas. Yasser's problems were solved by probably the only mistake Nick made in this game - a draw offer! It was gladly accepted. Typical piece sacrifices for White The diagrammed position shows a typical piece sacrifice idea for White.Black is a piece up, there no deadly threats, and yet Black is in deep trouble! Though we have relatively little games featuring this sacrifice concept, it is dangerous for Black, and computer engines confirm this. Game 19 Pascal Charbonneau 2510 Viswanathan Anand 2803 Turin ol 2006
30.a4
This looks sharp, but 30.Nd5! leads to a winning advantage as after 30...exd5
31.cxd5 Black's bishops are killed (meaning 'no attack') and, apart from dominating,
White will also start collecting some material, e.g. 31...Ne8 (31...Bd8 loses a piece after 32.Rc8 Bb7 33.Rb8; 31...Qd8 32.Bxg5 (32.Ng3 with Nf5 to follow is also strong) 32...Nxe4 33.fxe4 Rxg5 34.Rxc7; 31...Bb8 32.Bxa6+-) 32.Bxa6 with Ng3- f5 to follow and Black's position will collapse, e.g. 32...Rh4 33.Bb5+- Bd8 34.Rc8. If 30.g4 Rh3 31.Kg2 h5! 32.Kxh3 hxg4+ 33.Kg2 Qh7 is unclear.
30...Bb7
30...Rh4, preparing the ...g4 pawn push, was logical, however White would again
have the killer 31.Nd5!! exd5 32.cxd5 Ne8 33.Bxa6 Ng3. Nf5 will follow, while Black cannot create real counterplay. Still, from a practical standpoint 30...Rh4 was perhaps the best move guided by the logic 'if White did not sac on d5 last move, it is not very likely that he will do it next move'. This is an interesting psychological moment! We are playing against an opponent but also playing against ourselves, and being a strong player (seeing your opponent's best options) does not always help!
31.c5
Could White still sacrifice a piece? The answer is: Yes! After 31.Nd5 exd5 32.cxd5 Bd8 33.Ng3 White has (similar to Hübner-Ligterink below) only one pawn for the piece but has domination and full compensation (as a minimum - and Stockfish agrees!). The game is now tense and probably dynamically balanced. This game has been analysed in many publications (including my own book Sacrifice and Initiative). Interestingly, Vishy was the one to err and the game became one of the biggest 2006 Olympiad upsets.
31...dxc5 32.bxc5 bxc5 33.Rb1 Bc6 34.Bxc5 g4! 35.f4
35...g3?
It's not easy to tell what Anand blundered, but this mistake proves crucial. 35...Nf3! was the way to go, with complicating lines resulting in equality.
36.fxe5! Nxe4 37.Nxe4 Bxe4
Most likely Black overestimated his threats. White has an easy way to deal with the problem:
38.Bd3! Bxg2+ 39.Qxg2 gxh2 40.Bxg6 Rxg6
Black has no attack here because he simply has no pieces left. White chooses an elegant finish:
41.Ng3 Rxg3 42.Qe4 Rg4 43.Be7! 1-0
43...Rxe4 44.Bf6 Qg7 45.Rb8 Bxb8 46.Rd8#.
Game 20 Robert Hübner 2620 Gert Ligterink 2445 Wijk aan Zee 1984 Another good example of a typical sacrifice idea for White. White does not need to sacrifice a piece here, but Robert Hübner's idea is instructive! If Black's bishops are tamed, White will have full domination and this can be achieved by means of a positional piece sacrifice:
29.Nd5!
White also had a reasonably promising pawn sacrifice at his disposal: 29.Ng3. A) If 29...Nxe4 30.Bxe4 Bxe4 White should not be greedy for material but should play for the initiative with 31.Re1! (Black has reasonable compensation for the exchange in the event of 31.Nh5 Kh7 32.Nxg7 Rxg7); B) After 29...Bxe4 White has an unpleasant pawn break: 30.c5! bxc5 31.bxc5 Bxb1
32.c6! and many black pieces are hanging. Black's position looks lost but he can
continue fighting: 32...Ne4 33.Nxe4 Bxe4 34.cxd7 g3! (the key move, creating mate threats) 35.h3 Qd8 36.Rd4! (36.Qxc7?? blunders into a mate after 36...Qh4) 36...Ba5
37.Qa3 Bb7 and White should be better but the game remains complicated, e.g. 38.f5
(or 38.Rb1 Ba8 39.f5) 38...Bb6 39.Rxd6 Qh4 40.Rcc6.
29...exd5 30.cxd5
From a practical perspective (compared to the 29.Ng3 lines) Hübner's choice was an excellent one! Black is now doomed to passivity and cannot think of those flashy lines shown under 29.Ng3. This is an instructive example because most of the players, when they sacrificing a piece and get only one pawn in return, will be looking for a forced justification of the sacrifice. White does not have a 'forced justification' here, but he does have domination! Black's bishops are killed and White's knight will travel to the f5-outpost.
30...Bd8
30...Rc8 31.Nd4 also looks good for White.
31.Bd4
31.Ng3! with Nf5 to follow was more precise.
31...Nh5 32.Bxg7+ Rxg7
33.Nd4
It was probably better to keep the f4-pawn (so as not to allow the blockade on e5) with 33.Rf1.
33...Nxf4 34.Nf5 Ne5 35.Nxd6 f6 36.Qe3 Nfg6 37.Rf1 Rh7
The best defence was 37...Qb8! when the situation is not clear because White has given Black an excellent blockading outpost on e5!
38.Rf5 Qb8 39.Ne8 Bc8 40.Nxf6 Bxf6
The only way to struggle on was 40...Bxf5! 41.Nxh7 Kxh7 42.exf5 Bg5! 43.fxg6+ Kg7 44.Qe1 Bxc1 45.Qxc1 Qd6.
41.Rxf6 b5 42.Rxg6 1-0
A dream position for White The diagrammed position, which occurred in a well-known top-level game, shows a dream situation for White! He has regrouped excellently and is ready to push his pawns on the queenside. Black has a badly placed queen and no counterplay. In a higher sense this position is close to lost for Black! The game is very instructive in the way White (Kasparov) regrouped his pieces and Black (Kramnik) got a terrible position due to just shuffling his pieces around and not coming with a clear plan. To have and have not a plan Can a world champion in a classical game get into a lost position by playing random moves, never achieving any counterplay? Yes, he can! So don't kick yourself if sometimes it happens to you. Game 21 Garry Kasparov 2838 Vladimir Kramnik 2802 Moscow 2001
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 e6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 a6 5.c4 Nf6 6.Nc3 Qc7 7.a3 d6 8.Be3 b6 9.Rc1
Nbd7 We will get a typical Kan Hedgehog position.
10.Be2 Bb7 11.f3 Be7 12.O-O O-O 13.Kh1 Rac8 14.b4 Qb8 15.Qd2
White plans Rc2, Rb1, Na4, Nb2, Nd3, Qc1 and then simply start pushing his queenside pawns. Black obviously needs to plan some counterplay. In such positions, when it comes to planning, computer engines are less helpful than human knowledge/experience. The engine will give many options for Black evaluating at approximately 0.40. In my opinion, the plan Kramnik had to go for was pushing the h-pawn. Vladimir does not decide on a plan and keeps randomly shuffling his pieces around while Garry is executing his plan. As a result, once White's regrouping has been accomplished, he has a close to winning advantage!
15...Bd8
Black could consider 15...Rfe8 16.Rc2 h5.
16.Rc2 Re8
16...h5 again came into consideration. White continues with his plan.
17.Na4 Bc7
It was high time to start counterplay with 17...h5!.
18.Bg1
18...Rcd8?
This shows a lack of any clear plan as the ...d5 push is not really on the cards here. This was the last moment to play 18...h5! and if 19.Qe1, 19...Bd8 followed by ...h4.
19.Rb1
White is now clearly better and it's difficult to give Black clever advice.
19...Ba8
19...h5? is now wrong as there is no follow-up after the simple 20.Qe1.
20.Qc1
The next few random moves do not improve Kramnik's situation; however, without any counterplay his prospects are already bleak here.
20...h6
Kasparov takes the human decision and continues with his plan.
21.Nb2
My Stockfish is also enthusiastic about immediate action with 21.c5.
21...Nf8 22.Nd3
Regrouping accomplished! Now is the time for White to push his queenside pawns and crush Black!
22...Ng6 23.a4
Black's queen is terribly placed.
23...Qc8
White pieces are perfectly positioned for the final onslaught! Time to open the queenside. Which pawn would you push?
24.b5?
Surprising enough, Kasparov chooses for the least strong push.
24.a5 bxa5 25.b5 led to a winning advantage as Black cannot stabilize the queenside
situation:
24.c5 was perhaps strongest. At first glance Black seems to get counterplay after
24...bxc5 25.bxc5 d5.
analysis diagram However, the 'tension' lasts only a few moves: 26.e5! Nxe5 27.Nxe5 Bxe5 28.c6 and Black's queen is horribly placed. White has a winning advantage, e.g. 28...Bc7
29.Qf1!; 28...Qc7 29.Bxa6; or 28...Re7 29.Qf1.
24...a5
Black now stabilizes the queenside.
25.Nc6
It is possible that Kasparov, when deciding on 24.b5, thought that one of the pawn breaks 25.e5 or 25.c5 would work but then figured out that they didn't.
25...Bxc6
25...Rd7? is a mistake due to 26.Nxa5! bxa5 27.c5 and White crushes Black -
probably in the way he was hoping for.
26.bxc6
The lion's share of White's advantage has evaporated. Black stabilizes his situation and equalizes:
26...e5! 27.Bxb6 Ne7
28.c5
Keeping the bishop on was probably better: 28.Bg1! Nxc6 29.Rcb2.
28...d5 29.Bxc7 Qxc7 30.Rb7 Qxc6 31.Rb5 Ng6
Black has active play and the position is now balanced.
32.exd5 Nxd5 33.Nf2 Ngf4 34.Bf1 Ne6 35.Rxa5 Qc7 36.Rb5 Nd4 37.Rc4 Nxb5
38.axb5
We have a material imbalance resulting in equality.
38...Rb8 39.b6 Nxb6 40.cxb6 Qxb6 41.Qe1
Kramnik correctly decides to trade a pair of rooks.
41...Rec8 42.Rxc8+ Rxc8
The position is equal. One would expect White to give his king some air and play h3 or h4 now or on the next move, and then a draw agreement. However we are very far from the last miracle in this game!
43.Ng4 Re8 44.Bd3?!
Played with a wrong idea in mind.
44...Qd4
45.Be4?
This blunders a piece!
45...f5! 46.Bxf5 Ra8
Luckily for White, the loss of the piece is not fatal and he escapes into a drawn ending:
47.Bb1 Qb2 48.h4 Ra1 49.Kh2 Qxb1 50.Qxe5 Qh1+ 51.Kg3 Qe1+ 52.Qxe1 Rxe1
We have reached a theoretically drawn ending. But many mistakes will still be made and there is a lot to learn from this ending! This is, however, not an endgame book, so I will only point out a few important moments.
53.Kf4 Re2 54.Ne3 g6 55.g4 Kf7 56.h5 g5+
After 56...gxh5 57.gxh5 Rh2 58.Kg4 Black's h6-pawn is a target and White has an easy draw.
57.Ke4 Ke6
58.f4
This active defence complicates White's life. 58.Kd4 would have built a fortress, e.g.
58...Rd2+ 59.Ke4 Rb2 60.Nf5! Rb4+ 61.Ke3 Ke5 62.Kf2 Kf4 63.Ng3! Rb3 64.Ne2+
Ke5 65.Ng3 etc.
58...Rf2
As a result of his active defence, White now has to take an important decision.
59.Nd5?
This desicion should have cost him the game.
59.f5+! Kd6 60.Nc4+ still made a draw. Long analyses prove that the king and
knight coordinate well enough to hold.
59.fxg5 also held the draw, however after 59...hxg5 60.Nd5 Rf7 White has to
sacrifice a pawn and understand that he has a drawing fortress: 61.h6! Rh7 62.Nb4 Rxh6 63.Nd3.
59...gxf4 60.Nxf4+ Kf6 61.Ke3 Ra2 62.Nd3 Ra3 63.Ke4
63...Ra1
For some reason, until the end of the game Kramnik refuses to enter with his king:
63...Kg5 when White will run out of moves at some stage.
64.Kf4 Ra4+ 65.Kf3 Ra3 66.Ke4 Rb3 67.Nc5 Rb4+ 68.Kf3 Rb5?
It was still not to late to change his mind and enter with the king on g5: 68...Rc4!
69.Nd7+ Kg5!, winning.
69.Ne4+ Ke5
Now White grabs his chance:
70.g5! Rb4
Black is also in time to draw after 70...Rb3+ 71.Kg4 Kxe4 72.gxh6.
71.Nf2 Rf4+ 72.Kg3 1⁄2-1⁄2
Chapter 4 The Sveshnikov Variation The Sveshnikov Sicilian is rich with ideas, and half this book could have been devoted to it. With our limited space, my choice is personal and I have opted for two ideas for White that I like very much. Also, I think they can be useful for the reader in practical play. One idea is a positional exchange sacrifice; the other is an attacking idea related to a rook swing along the second rank. In both cases, we have quite a number of top-level examples. The exchange sacrifice Diagrammed is the typical exchange sacrifice idea seen in a number of top-level games. We have different versions of this idea, but positionally/strategically, the concept is the same. In return for the exchgange White gets light-square control, a dominant knight on the d5-outpost, and a passed b-pawn. Black is to move in the diagrammed position and has to decide on a plan to create counterplay. Black's best counterplay options here are related to the f-pawn push (...f5). In Kasparov-Shirov, which we will analyse below, White got an even better version of the diagrammed position with an extra pair of knights on the board. The second-rank rook swing The second diagram shows a nice attacking idea for White seen in a couple of top- level games. White has first sacrificed the h-pawn to open the h-file and now has just played b3, clearing the second rank for a rook transfer. White will push f4, then swing his Ra2 to the kingside and obtain a powerful attack. To my knowledge, this idea was invented by Ruslan Ponomariov and it has later been replicated in some top-level games. Exchange sacrifice/light-square domination For a good understanding of the positional exchange sacrifice with which White tries to achieves light-square domination, this game by Kasparov is an important cornerstone, and the next game Anand-Van Wely is another important building block. When the first game was played, it was (to my knowledge) a novel idea. Having to solve new strategic problems in over-the-board play, Shirov quickly got into trouble and Kasparov celebrated a smooth victory. As we will see, things are actually rather complex. Game 22 Garry Kasparov 2805 Alexei Shirov 2740 Horgen 1994
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 e6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 Nf6 5.Nc3 Nc6 6.Ndb5 d6 7.Bf4 e5 8.Bg5 a6
9.Na3 b5 10.Nd5 Be7 11.Bxf6 Bxf6 12.c3
12...Bb7
Predominantly due to this game, this continution has slowly disappeared from practice, perhaps unjustifiably so as Black's play can be improved.
12...Bg5 is nowadays considered to be the main move.
13.Nc2
Black's knight on c6 needs to be improved. Usually Black either opts for the game plan or plays 13...Ne7 which after 14.Nxf6 gxf6 leads to a different pawn structure.
13...Nb8 14.a4
In general, the standard plan for White here. In this particular case, Kasparov already had the ensuing exchange sacrifice in mind.
14...bxa4 15.Rxa4 Nd7
16.Rb4!
Preparing a positional exchange sacrifice. In this game, everything went perfect for White. Either Alexei was not aware of White's plan, or he underestimated it, and White's strategic idea worked to a full extent. There is however a risk associated with White's idea: if the exchange is not sacrificed on b7, the rook will be a bit awkward on b4.
16...Nc5?!
Definitely helping White with the execution of his planned exchange sacrifice; the knight will be out of play on b7. The correct decision was 16...Ra7 when matters are far from clear as White's exchange sac now works considerably less well. A) The exchange sacrifice now leads to approximate (dynamic) equality, e.g.
17.Rxb7 Rxb7 18.Ncb4 (18.b4?, trying to control c5 (similar to Kasparov) now does
not work with Black's knight on d7 as Black is actually better after 18...a5) 18...Nc5 (Black may also opt for 18...a5 when best for White is to gain back the exchange with 19.Ba6 Qa8 20.Bxb7 Qxb7 and it is doubtful if White has any advantage here) analysis diagram
19.Bxa6! Nxa6 20.Nxa6 O-O (Black may decide to collect the b2-pawn: 20...Rxb2,
and after 21.Nab4 (the threat is Qa1) 21...Qa5 22.Qc1 Qa3 23.Nxf6+ gxf6 24.O-O, to me the position looks promising for White but my Stockfish gives around equality)
21.Nab4 and compared to our main game (see below), we have a different situation.
White has compensation for the exchange, but not more; B) White can play for light-square domination with 17.Bc4!?, however after 17...O-O
18.O-O a5...
analysis diagram ... the rook is awkwardly placed on b4; best is to sacrifice a pawn: 19.Ra4 Nc5
20.Ra3 Nxe4 21.Qe2 Ng5 (21...Nc5 leads to White's advantage after 22.b4 Ne6
23.Rfa1) 22.Nce3 Ne6 23.b4 and White definitely has compensation but probably
not more; C) 24 years later, Alexei tried Kasparov's idea as White, but even though he won that game, he didn't get an opening advantage: 17.Nce3 O-O 18.h4!? (this typical idea to limit Black's bishop does not work that well here because Black can improve the bishop in another way) 18...a5 19.Rc4. The critical moment; here Black has a possibility to improve on the game by executing a typical Sveshnikov structure plan! 19...Nc5 (better was 19...Qb8! with ...Bd8 and ...Nb6 to follow. White's rook on c4 is clumsy and Black has a comfortable, perhaps even somewhat better position) 20.b4. Shirov's rook will now be liberated but Black gets the d4-square for his knight: 20...axb4 21.cxb4 Ne6 (White may have had regrets about pushing h4, as now he needs to spend a move protecting it and it can also can be a target for a piece sacrifice in the future) 22.g3 Nd4 (the position can be evaluated as dynamically balanced) 23.Bd3 Ra3 24.O-O
24...g6 (a standard Sveshnikov move, but Black had an interesting tactical option at
his disposal: the piece sacrifice 24...Bxh4!? 25.gxh4 Bxd5 26.exd5 Qxh4. Black already has two pawns for the piece and White's king is not secure, White needs time to coordinate his pieces. Your engine will give you zeros, but in practice it might be easier to play this position as Black) 25.Nc2. Now we get a position with opposite-coloured bishops. 25...Nxc2 26.Qxc2 Bxd5 27.exd5. White has a passed b-pawn, but Black can easily deal with it and is not worse.
27...Qa8? (a step in the wrong direction; correct was 27...Qb8 followed by ...Bd8,
...Bb6, ...f5, with sufficient counterplay) 28.Rc6! Rd8 29.b5 Qa5 30.Rb1. Now White is in the driving seat and his b-pawn is powerful. White soon won in Shirov- Lindh, Sweden tt 2018. After the text move, Kasparov gets what he was hoping for!
17.Rxb7! Nxb7 18.b4
Black's knight on b7 will be passive for quite some time. White has total light- square domination. Compared to Anand-Van Wely (below) White does not have a passed b-pawn, but the presence of an extra pair of knights (Black having a passive one on b7) definitely favours White. Shirov tries to create counterplay by opening the a-file (also giving White a passed b-pawn).
18...Bg5 19.Na3!
The right place for the Nc2 is the c4-square, so first improving the knight is the most precise.
19...O-O 20.Nc4 a5
Kasparov chooses the most human move, completing his development.
21.Bd3
Probably the most precise was 21.h4!. The bishop on h6 controls the (not exactly important) h6-c1 diagonal, but does not control e7, allowing White some tactical motifs: 22.Ncb6! A) After 22...Ra7 we see the effects of Black's bishop being chased to h6 and not controlling the e7-square: 23.Nc8! Ra8 24.Nce7+ Kh8 25.Bb5 and White has total domination; B) 22...Rb8 23.g3 and Black is very passive, Stockfish 15 even gives White a winning advantage (plus two); C) 22...axb4 23.cxb4! (playing for positional domination is the most practical here; on 23.Nxa8 Black can sacrifice a piece with 23...bxc3! (23...Qxa8? is a strategic blunder as after 24.cxb4 White has a huge advantage with his passed b-pawn and total domination): 24.Nab6 Bd2+ 25.Ke2 Nc5 and in a game between humans, this position does not look clear) 23...Ra3 24.Bb5. analysis diagram White has full domination and definitely plenty of compensation. Play may continue
24...f5 25.Nc4 Ra7 26.exf5 Rxf5 27.g3 followed by castling, with light-square
domination while Black doesn't have any counterplay. Such positions are what White aims for with his exchange sacrifice.
21...axb4 22.cxb4
22...Qb8?!
My Stockfish 15 (at a decent depth) advises Black to be patient, to 'sit on his hands' and go for something like 22...Ra7 23.O-O (23.h4?! Bxh4 23.Ncb6 Qe8=) 23...Kh8
24.g3 g6, claiming that it will be difficult for White to improve his position. I have
tried to play this position against my Stockfish, and of course failed to improve White's position; Black has defensive resources. For a human it's not easy to employ a 'rope-a-dope' strategy here, so it's easy to understand that Shirov wants to create counterplay.
23.h4!
This typical idea (send black bishop to h6) improves White's situation here (as tactics, later on, related to Ne7+ will appears).
23...Bh6 24.Ncb6 Ra2 25.O-O Rd2
Black does not really have counterplay as his Rd2 does not have any support.
26.Qf3 Qa7
27.Nd7
The most direct, but not the most precise. The point is that the Bd3 will be hanging in lines where White wants to capture Qxf7. Black's pieces are paralysed and there was no need for White to be in a hurry. Best was 27.Bb5! so as to play Nd7 and, when the Rf8 moves, Ne7+ followed by Qxf7. Black will first lose his extra exchange (since after White's Nd7 his Rf8 cannot move) and then White's b-pawn will decide. Black's Bh8 continues to control plenty of empty squares.
27...Nd8?
This loses quickly. Best was 27...Ra8! when White has a clear advantage but Black can still fight. White's best is to hope for a move repetition (and then play Bb5): A) 28.Ne7+ does not achieve the aim as after 28...Kh8 29.Qxf7, the Bd3 is hanging (therefore 27.Bb5! was needed) and Black can simply take 29...Rxd3 and after
30.Nf8 play 30...Qa2! when White has to force perpetual check with 31.Neg6+ hxg6
32.Nxg6+ Kh7 33.Nf8+;
B) 28.N7b6! Qa3 and now White has nice tactics due to Black's bad king position and bad piece coordination, but not a clear win (28...Rf8 allows the precise 29.Bb5!, with a winning advantage): 29.Nxa8! Rxd3 analysis diagram B1) 30.Nf6+ looks flashy but brings not more than a draw after 30...Kf8! (30...gxf6? loses to 31.Qg4+ Bg7 32.Qc8+ Bf8 though (as usual) White still has to be precise:
33.Nc7! and Black does not get the time to coordinate his pieces: 33...Qxb4 34.Ne8!
d5 35.Nxf6+ Kg7 36.Nh5+ Kg8 37.Qg4+ Kh8 38.Qf5 and White wins in a few moves) 31.Nxh7+ Kg8 32.Nf6+ Kf8 33.Qf5 Qxa8 34.Nd5 Kg8! and White has nothing better than a draw by move repetition 35.Ne7+ Kf8 36.Nd5; B2) 30.Qe2 Rxd5 (30...Rd2 loses to 31.Qb5 Qxa8 32.Nc7! Qb8 33.Ra1) 31.Nb6! (White's knight is needed on b6 as in some lines the move Nd7 is needed; 31.Nc7? is not precise as after 31...Rd4 32.Qg4 g6 33.Qc8+ Bf8 White needs to be able to play Nd7) 31...Rd3 32.Qg4 Rc3 33.Qd7. analysis diagram Here the only way for Black not to get mated immediately is 33...g5 (33...g6? leaves Black's king boxed in after 34.Qe8+ Kg7 35.Nd7 g5 36.h5) 34.Qxb7 - White has a large advantage and should win.
28.Nxf8 Kxf8 29.b5
Now White's life is easy. Black's pieces are still not coordinated. White has a passed b-pawn plus an attack, and Kasparov executes with energetic play right until the end.
29...Qa3 30.Qf5! Ke8 31.Bc4 Rc2 32.Qxh7 Rxc4 33.Qg8+ Kd7 34.Nb6+ Ke7
35.Nxc4 Qc5
White has many ways to finish the game off here; Garry characteristically chooses the most hard-hitting option.
36.Ra1! Qd4 37.Ra3 Bc1
38.Ne3!
A nice concluding move. White had an even nicer move at his disposal with a similar idea: 38.Nxd6!. 1-0 Game 23 Viswanathan Anand 2792 Loek Van Wely 2647 Wijk aan Zee 2006
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 Nf6 5.Nc3 e5 6.Ndb5 d6 7.Bg5 a6 8.Na3 b5
9.Nd5 Be7 10.Bxf6 Bxf6 11.c3 Bg5
This is not a theory book, but some opening info never hurts. The text 11...Bg5 has established itself as the main line nowadays because Black is not in a hurry to show his hand by quickly castling. The immediate 11...O-O has been played by a number of very strong players; however, after 12.Nc2 Rb8, 13.h4! ... ... is a clever and useful move - stopping ...Bg5, enabling g3 followed by Bh3, and getting ready to push the h-pawn further (definitely if Black plays ...g6), while still not showing his hand regarding his king position. Black would like to play ...Ne7, trading a pair of knights, but that allows Nxf6+, damaging his kingside pawn structure. A) With Black's king open after 13...Ne7 14.Nxf6+ gxf6 we have two games with Kasparov (as White) castling queenside: 15.Bd3! (in his first outing, Garry went for
15.Qd2 Bb7 16.Bd3 d5 17.exd5 Qxd5 18.O-O-O here, Kasparov-Kramnik, Novgorod
1994. Garry celebrated victory, however Black's play could have been improved and
in the same position a couple of months later he opted for the more precise text move). In Kasparov-Lautier, Moscow 1994, Black followed Kramnik's plan, hoping for counterplay: 15...d5 16.exd5 Qxd5. Now we see the advantage of the 15.Bd3! move-order: 17.Ne3! Qe6 18.Qh5. Just like in the game vs Kramnik, White will castle queenside but here he has a better version: 18...e4 19.Bc2 b4 20.c4 Kh8 21.O-O-O! White's king is safe on the queenside, and he has a better pawn structure plus attacking chances on the kingside. White is clearly better. The game did not last long; I will give it until the end because of the beautiful last move: 21...f5 22.Qg5 Rb6 23.h5 Rc6 (Black does not have any counterplay; if 23...Rg8 24.Qf4 Qh6 the bad coordination of Black's pieces is obvious after 25.Qc7) 24.Kb1 Rc5 (now on 24...Rg8 25.Qf4 Qh6 White has the unpleasant 26.Qb8!) 25.h6! (weaving a mating net!) 25...Qe5 26.Rh5! Rg8. White to play and Black to resign! 27.Ng4!! and mate or decisive material loss cannot be avoided: 27...Rxg5 28.Nxe5 or 27...Qe6 28.Rd8. B) Carlsen, playing Black, took the logical decision to place his bishop on g7 first and then play ...Ne7: 13...g6 14.g3 Bg7 15.h5. Now Black tries to get rid of White's dominant knight: 15...Ne7 16.Nce3 Nxd5 17.Nxd5 Be6. Now Wesley So played a good strategic move that almost always works in such positions: 18.Bh3!. Black is forced to take a decision, as ignoring this move is not easy: 18...Bxd5 (if Black ignore the move anyway and plays 18...Qd7, White has a clear advantage after 19.Bxe6 (my engine also likes 19.h6 Bh8 20.Bxe6 fxe6
21.Ne3±). Black has to allow his pawn structure to be damaged, otherwise the Nd5 is
a monster: 19...fxe6 20.hxg6 hxg6 21.Ne3±) 19.Qxd5 (Black hopes to stabilize the queenside) 19...b4 20.c4 Qc7 21.O-O Rb6 22.b3. We get this type of position more often in the Sveshnikov (which is the reason I selected this game) and it would be a mistake to evaluate this as drawish due to the opposite-coloured bishops. White has a clear advantage while Black has no counterplay: 22...Rd8. Wesley So is an excellent technical player but here he committed an unusual (for him) mistake. White had to continue to slowly improve his position when Black would be under long-term pressure struggling for a draw. For example, 23.Rad1. White has many plans here: triple on the d-file or think about pushing the f4-pawn. I am not sure if White's advantage is as large as your engine will evaluate (around +1) but Black definitely faces a long-term struggle for a draw. In a hurry to improve his position, So played 23.a4? bxa3 and now Black had a target (the b3-pawn) and the position was equal! 24.hxg6 hxg6 25.Rxa3 Qc5 (the queen trade leads to a drawish endgame) 26.Rfa1 Qxd5 27.cxd5 Bh6 and a draw was soon agreed in So-Carlsen, Stavanger 2019.
12.Nc2 Rb8
Anand goes for the main move (today as well as at the time). Vishy probably had the ensuing exchange sacrifice in mind already. Was he inspired by the Kasparov-Shirov game that he obviously knew? It's not easy to say, as the exchange sacrifice - based on light-square domination - Vishy is about to execute had already been tried in a lesser-known game from grandmaster practice.
13.a4
In another high-level game thirteen years later, White was definitely inspired by this game from Anand! Let's take a look at the critical moment: 13.Be2 O-O 14.O-O Kh8
15.Bg4 Bb7 16.Ncb4 Nxb4 17.cxb4 g6 18.a4 bxa4 19.Rxa4 Bc6 and now White sort
of copied Anand's idea with 20.Be2 (if White does it in a slightly different way with
20.Rxa6 Bb5 21.Be2 Bxa6 22.Bxa6, Black also, just like in the game, gets good
counterplay after 22...f5) 20...Bxa4 21.Qxa4. If we compare this position to our main game (see comment after the move 19...Qe8 - Black plays 19...g6 instead) we see that Giri is two tempi down compared to Anand (actually better compared to the stem game Barua-Lalic) as Black's pawn is already on g6, Black is to move and the pawn on a6 has not yet been taken! Black has comfortable play here with 21...f5! 22.exf5 Rxf5. Probably, Magnus had already calculated the coming tactical motif. It should be mentioned that a draw would have been enough for Carlsen to win the tournament, so he was not ambitious (Black could have shown ambition by playing 22...gxf5!?): 23.Bd3 Rf8 24.Qxa6
24...Bd2! 25.Qc4 (25.b5?! Ba5 leads to Black's advantage) 25...Qc8 26.Qe4 and
now comes the tactical solution Magnus had probably calculated when playing
22...Rxf5: 26...Bxb4 27.Nxb4 Rf4 28.Qc6 end it all ended peacefully in a drawn
endgame: 28...Rfxb4 29.Qxd6 Qf8 30.Qxe5+ 1⁄2-1⁄2 Giri-Carlsen, Wijk aan Zee 2019.
13...bxa4 14.Ncb4
14...Bd7
Main theory nowadays is 14...Nxb4, the starting point of the theoretical discussion being 15.cxb4 O-O 16.Rxa4 a5! when we have many top-level games; the current assessment seems to be 'a dynamic balance'.
15.Bxa6 Nxb4 16.cxb4 O-O 17.O-O Bc6
Black could have stopped White's exchange sac by playing 17...Qe8 but then White has an advantage after the simple 18.b3 axb3 19.Qxb3. White has light-square domination, a passed b-pawn and control of the a-file; Black has no real counterplay. Now Vishy goes for a positional exchange sac that had already been seen in grandmaster practice some years earlier, so it's quite possible that Anand knew that game:
18.Rxa4! Bxa4 19.Qxa4
Compare this position to (the same sacrifice idea in) Giri-Carlsen, in the comment after 13.a4 (White playing 13.Be2). Well, it cannot really be compared, because in that game Black had played three more moves: ...Kh8, ...g6, ...f5, and also his pawn on a6 is still alive! Probably guided by the logic 'I am material up - a queen trade makes sense' Loek went for that:
19...Qe8
Black struggles to create counterplay here anyway, so keeping the queens on did not equalize either: 19...Kh8 20.Bb5! (20.b5 Qd7 21.Qc4 f5 was about equal in Ragger- Markos, Austria tt 2007) 20...f5 (20...g6 does not bring Black the counterplay he is hoping for after 21.Bc6 f5 22.b5 fxe4 23.Qxe4) 21.exf5 Rxf5 22.Bc6. Now, best for Black is probably to go for a pawn sacrifice: 22...e4 23.b5 e3 24.fxe3 ... ... assuming he finds the brilliant 24...Qf6!, but White still has some advantage after
25.Rxf5 Qxf5 26.h4.
The stem game went 19...g6 20.Qc6. Now Black went for the typical bishop transfer to b6: 20...Bd2 21.b5 Ba5 22.Bb7 f6 (including the Rf8 in the game) 23.b4 Bb6
24.Nxb6 Rf7.
The game Barua-Lalic, Ubeda 1998, saw a 'clean-up' into a drawn endgame after
25.Rd1 Rfxb7 26.Rxd6 Qxd6 27.Qxd6 Rxb6=. White's play can be improved with
25.Qc4! and after 25...Rbxb7 26.Nd5 Black struggles for a draw.
20.Qxe8 Rfxe8 21.b5
We have a type of position White is happy to get. Yes, the queens are off, but the light-squares domination from the middlegame still persists. White's plan is relatively easy: push the pawn to b7, then Nd5-b4-c6 (get your exchange back) and win a superior endgame. Black urgently needs counterplay, which is difficult to find. Van Wely goes for the most logical - and typically Sveshnikov - counterplay idea:
21...f5 22.b6
Vishy continues on his path and keeps the position closed. It is doubtful if Black gets enough counterplay after 22.exf5 e4 23.b6.
22...fxe4
White stands better, but how much better isn't easy to tell. Vishy comes up with an interesting pawn sac:
23.h4!?
With a limited number of pieces, White sacrifices a pawn to penetrate the c-file. The most logical alternative for White looks to be 23.Re1 when Black needs to improve his king: 23...Kf7 24.Rxe4. analysis diagram Black's position is difficult to defend, but the engine always has ideas: 24...Rf8! (the arguably more logical 24...Bd8 25.Rb4 Ke6 loses to 26.Bc4 Rf8 27.b7. White wants to play simply Rb5, Nb4, Na6 and Black cannot stop this: 27...Kd7 28.Bb5+ Ke6
29.Bc6 followed by Rb5, Nb4, Na6). Now White needs to spend a move to drive
Black's bishop away: 25.h4 (25.Rb4 Bd2) 25...Bd8 26.Rb4 Ke6 and things are not the same as in the comment to 24...Bd8: 27.Bc4 Kd7 28.Bb5+. analysis diagram Here Black has the defensive resource 28...Kc8!! 29.Rc4+ (it's never too late to do something stupid: 29.Bc6?? Bxb6) 29...Kb7 30.Bc6+ Kc8. analysis diagram Strangely, White does not have a killer check here and has to go for 31.b7+ Rxb7
32.Bxb7+ Kxb7 with (still) some advantage, but Black has reasonable chances to
survive.
23...Bd2?
For better or worse, the pawn had to be taken! 23...Bxh4!. The c-file penetration is less decisive than it looks: 24.Rc1 Rf8 25.g3 Bg5 26.Rc6 analysis diagram
26...Kf7! with approximate equality after 27.Bc4 Kg6 (or 27...h5) 28.Rxd6+ Kf5.
24.b7 Kf7 25.Rd1 Bh6 26.Nb4 Ke7 27.Nd5+ Kf7 28.g4 Bf4
29.Re1?
Logical but not precise. Apparently, it was better not to force matters as after 29.Kg2 Black runs out of useful moves while 29...Rf8 loses to 30.Bc4 Ke8 (30...Rxb7 31.Nxf4+ Ke8 32.Ne6)
31.Bb5+ Kd8.
analysis diagram Now White reminds Black of his weak king with 32.Ra1! and gets a winning advantage, e.g. 32...Rf7 33.Ra6
29...g5!
A strong move, keeping Black in the game! He could also have chosen a different move-order: 29...Red8 30.Rxe4 Rd7 31.Rb4 g5! (the immediate 31...Ke6 does not work that well after 32.Rb5 g5 33.Nb4) 32.h5 Ke6 analysis diagram
33.Rb5 (White has no deadly discovered check after 33.Bc4 Bd2) and we see why it
was good to protect the bishop on f4: 33...e4".
30.Re2
White renews the idea of the c-file penetration. After the (perhaps most logical)
30.h5 Black holds with 30...Red8! 31.Rd1 (31.Nb4?? Bd2; 31.Rxe4 Ke6) 31...Rd7=;
if 30.Rxe4, Black again eliminates the b-pawn with 30...Red8! 31.Rc4 Rd7=.
30...Red8?
Van Wely (again) refrains from pawn-grabbing and this proves to be the decisive mistake. Normally Loek loves to take material!
30...gxh4! had to be played. The advanced h-pawn helps Black to create counterplay
and probably this will save the game! A logical run of play can be 31.Rc2 h3 32.Kh1 h2 33.Nb4. analysis diagram Black's situation looks hopeless but thanks to his advanced h-pawn he can save the game: 33...h5! 34.Nc6 and now another hidden resource: 34...Ra8! 35.bxa8Q Rxa8
36.Nb4 hxg4 and it will probably end in a draw; Black has enough pawns for the
piece.
31.Nb4
Now White is easily winning and Anand brings it home with a steady hand.
31...d5 32.Nc6
The simple threat is 33.Nxd8 Rxd8 34.Rc2 with Rc8 to follow, so 30...Red8? proves to be a loss of two tempi.
32...Rg8 33.Nxb8 Rxb8
34.h5!
In essence, creating another passed pawn, as Black's h-pawn is going to fall.
34...Ke7 35.Kf1 d4 36.Rc2 e3 37.fxe3
Sadly for Black, he has to take with the pawn:
37...dxe3 38.Rc7+ Kf6
39.Rxh7
White's passed pawns are impossible to stop.
39...e4 40.Bc4 Rd8 41.Rf7+ Ke5 42.Rd7
And Black resigned. The second rank rook lift Game 24 Ruslan Ponomariov 2700 Vladimir Kramnik 2754 Wijk aan Zee 2005
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 Nf6 5.Nc3 e5 6.Ndb5 d6 7.Bg5 a6 8.Na3 b5
9.Nd5 Be7 10.Bxf6 Bxf6 11.c3 Bg5 12.Nc2
12...O-O
Magnus entered this line twice as Black. First he lost to Peter Leko, but a year later (also at Linares) he repeated it vs Grischuk: 12...Ne7 13.h4 Bh6 14.a4 bxa4 15.Ncb4 O-O with a nice positional advantage for White: 16.Qxa4 Nxd5 17.Nxd5 a5 (after
17...Kh8, most probably Magnus did not like 18.Qa5) 18.Bb5.
White's advantage is small, but normally we have play for two results here. White will create a passed b-pawn, Black's Bh6 is controling many 'empty squares' while White dominates the light squares. 18...Be6 19.Bc6 Rb8. It's time for White to create a passed pawn: 20.b4 Bxd5 21.Bxd5 axb4 22.cxb4 (practically, White is a pawn up as Black's d6-pawn is not worth much, but Black should be able to hold) 22...Qb6
23.Rb1 (one year later, interestingly enough Grischuk went for a pawn sacrifice here
with 23.O-O Qxb4 24.Qd7. White does not have enough pieces to build on his light- square pressure and the game Grischuk-Carlsen, Linares 2009, was soon drawn)
23...Kh8 (the coffeehouse-type sacrifice 23...Bd2+? does not work for Black after
the simple 24.Kxd2 Qxf2+ 25.Kd3 Qxg2 26.Rhe1) 24.O-O. After losing against Leko, Magnus was ready to play this position again (vs Grischuk), and it makes me wonder what Magnus had in mind. 24...f5 (perhaps here Magnus intended to improve his bishop with the typical Sveshnikov maneuvre
24...Bd2 25.b5 Bc3 with ...Bd4 to follow, when it looks difficult for White to
improve his position) 25.Qa5 (keeping the queens on with 25.b5 offers White good prospects.) 25...fxe4 26.Qxb6 Rxb6 27.Rb3 and thanks to his b-pawn White has an advantage in this ending. Black is probably closer to a draw then White is to a win, nevertheless White won in Leko-Carlsen, Morelia/Linares 2008.
13.a4 bxa4 14.Rxa4 a5 15.Bc4 Rb8
This is the position of our interest. Ponomariov comes up with an interesting plan here! White has many pawns on his second rank, nevertheless Pono plans to swing his Ra4 to the kingside and obtain a deadly attack! Later, white players improved the move-order, however to my knowledge the plan belongs to Pono and this is the stem game!
16.Ra2!?
Nine years later Anish Giri copied Ponomariov's plan and crushed Alexei Shirov in their friendly match (it pays off to do some research!). Let's see this game. This is also the best move-order for White: 16.b3! Kh8 17.Nce3 g6 (If Black goes for 17...Bxe3 18.Nxe3 Ne7, White has an advantage after 19.O-O analysis diagram as Black does not have the convenient counterplay idea 19...f5 here, e.g. 20.exf5 Nxf5 21.Nxf5 (Black will lose his a5-pawn for no compensation) 21...Rxf5 /(21...Bxf5) 22.Qd5±.) Now Anish goes for the plan Pono devised in our stem game: 18.h4!. White sacrifices his h-pawn so as to open the h-file and gain time to push g3, followed by f4, clearing the second rank for the rook transfer Ra4-a2-h2! 18...Bxh4 (if Black does not accept the sacrifice, White has an easy advantage) 19.g3 Bg5 (19...Bf6
20.Ra2 transposes to our main game Ponomariov-Kramnik). Anish now copies
Pono's plan: 20.f4! exf4 21.gxf4 Bh4+ (this check is logical; Black hopes to gain time. After 21...Bf6 White gets a deadly attack with 22.Ra2! Re8 23.Rah2!). Where to go with the white king? A) It took white players some time to understand the necessity of keeping the second rank free for the Ra4-a2-h2 rook swing! After 22.Kd2, the king stands in the way of the rook transfer and Black has a good game after 22...Ne7! as for example in Topalov-Leko, Linares 2005; B) 22.Kf1!. Now Black wants to open the f-file to take advantage of White's king being placed on f1: 22...f5 (22...Ne7 now loses to the rook swing 23.Ra2! with Rah2 to follow and a crushing attack) 23.Ra2! (consistent and strong) 23...fxe4 (23...g5
24.Rah2 fxe4 25.Qh5 Rb7 26.Ke2 transposes to the game) 24.Rah2 g5. White will
need to move his king away from the f-file pin in order to threaten fxg5, but he needs to activate his queen first. I do not know how far Giri's opening preparation went, but I do know Anish played a perfect game: 25.Qh5! B1) 25...Ne5 does not help Black, as after 26.Ke2 Nxc4 27.fxg5! he does not have real counterplay, e.g. 27...Rxb3 28.Rxh4 and White's king is perfectly safe in the centre: 28...Rb2+ 29.Ke1 Rb1+ 30.Nd1 Bf5 31.g6 (Black cannot avoid material loss)
31...Rb7 32.Rf4 Bxg6 (32...Qd7 loses to the prosaic 33.Rhf1 and Bf5 falls, while
32...Be6 lands Black in a lost endgame after 33.Rxf8+ Qxf8 34.Qxh7+! Rxh7
35.Rxh7+ Kg8 36.Ne7++-) 33.Rxf8+ Qxf8 34.Qxg6 White was a sound piece up
and soon won in Korneev-Huerga Leache, Linares 2015; B2) 25...Rb7 26.Ke2. The triumph of White's strategy! His king is perfectly safe in the centre while Black needs to start worrying about fxg5, forcing the Bh4 to move and opening the h-file: 26...Be6 27.Qh6 Bg8 (more stubborn was 27...Bxd5, however after 28.Nxd5 - 28.Bxd5 leads to unclear consequences after 28...Rxf4! 29.Bxc6 Rxb3 as White is reminded that his king is now 'always safe' - 28...Rff7 29.Qe6 White has light-square domination plus an attack along the h-file, and should win)
28.Rg2! (White is playing with great accuracy; 28.Ng2??, hoping to clear the h-file,
is a blunder and loses to 28...Ne7; and moving the other rook was not precise as after
28.Rg1 Bxd5 29.Nxd5 Ne7 30.Rxg5 Nxd5 31.Rxd5 Black has the unpleasant tempo
move 31...Bg3!) 28...Rbf7 (arguably most stubborn was 28...Bxd5 29.Nxd5 Ne7, but ultimately it would probably not change the outcome as after 30.Rxg5 Nxd5 31.Rxd5 Bf6 - 31...Bg3? does not gain a tempo now and loses to 32.Rdh5 Qe7 33.Qg6 Bxf4
34.Rf5! - 32.Rdh5, White has a strong attack with the opposite-coloured bishops.
The attacking side usually wins in such situations!). Anish now demonstrates accuracy in the execution: 29.Rxh4! gxh4 30.Nf5 h3
31.Nh4!. Mate in one is always a powerful threat! Black is forced to part with his
queen: 31...Qxh4 32.Rxg8+ Rxg8 33.Qxh4 Rg2+ 34.Kf1. White has a sizeable material advantage, while his attack is far from over. Black will suffer further material losses: 34...Rh2 35.Ne3 Rg7 36.Qf6 Rh1+ 37.Kf2 Rh2+ 38.Ke1 (if Black keeps checking, White's king goes all the way to a3) 38...Rh1+ 39.Kd2 Rh2+
40.Kc1 Ne7
41.Nf5!, concluding the game. Out of inertia Black played a few more moves:
41...Rhg2 42.Nxg7 Rxg7 43.Qf8+ Ng8 44.Bxg8 Rxg8 45.Qf6+ Rg7 46.Qh4 1-0
Giri-Shirov, Hoogeveen 2014.
16...Kh8 17.Nce3
17...g6
The reason why 16.b3! is a better move-order than 16.Ra2 is the following line:
17...Bxe3! 18.Nxe3 Ne7 and now after 19.b3 white king is still on e1 and Black
equalizes with 19...f5, as for example in Karjakin-Topalov, Wijk aan Zee 2006. Now Ruslan goes for his master plan!
18.h4!
Interestingly, a couple of months later Anand did not follow White's bold plan and went for the relatively timid 18.O-O, giving Black a good game after 18...f5 in Anand-Kasparov, Linares 2005.
18...Bxh4
Again Black is not forced to accept the sacrifice but White has an easy advantage' after 18...Bxe3 19.Nxe3.
19.g3
19...Bf6
Facing a novel plan, Kramnik understood the danger and did not want to give White another tempo. A couple of months later, Vladimir utilizes Ruslan's idea on the White side and scored an easy win! Van Wely went for 19...Bg5 and Vladimir followed up on Pono's idea with 20.f4 exf4 21.gxf4 Bh4+ 22.Kf1 f5. With his pawn still on b2, White wants to take the maximum out of the position:
23.b4! (23.b3 transposes to Giri-Shirov). Now Black also needed to worry about the
move b5, harassing his knight at an unfortunate moment: 23...fxe4 24.Rah2 g5. It being a rapid game, White does not play in the most precise way. The most accurate was to immediately remove White's king from the pin on the f-file:
25.Ke2!+- (the game itself went 25.b5 Ne5 and now Black had some counterplay.
White eventually won in Kramnik-Van Wely, Monaco 2005). At a desirable moment White will take fxg5, unleashing the power of his doubled rooks. Black does not have a suitable defense, e.g. 25...Rf7 (protecting the h7-pwn with 25...Rb7 loses to
26.b5 and Black has no good answer; if 25...axb4, White comes first with 26.fxg5)
26.b5! and again Black does not have a good response.
20.b3
The second rank is almost cleared. White only has to push the f-pawn and the Ra2 will swing to the kingside with devastating power!
20...Bg7
Kramnik hopes his bishop will offer his king Dragon-like protection while creating counterplay on the e-file. Your computer engine will suggest 20...Kg7 21.f4 h5 - this looks passive and will probably not save Black.
21.f4 exf4 22.gxf4 Re8
Here Ponomariov missed an opportunity to create a masterpiece.
23.Qf3?
The best move was the bold and direct 23.Rah2!. Probably, Ruslan found it difficult to calculate the consequences of 23...Rxe4 (after 23...h6 24.Qf3 White has a crushing attack without any risk) 24.Rxh7+ Kg8 25.Qf3 Rxc4!. analysis diagram The position indeed looks complicated, and it's easy to understand that White did not go for this. He would have needed to see a beautiful mating net woven with 26.f5!! and Black does not have a good response as 26...Ne5 runs into 27.Rxg7+! Kxg7
28.f6+ Kg8 29.Rh8+! Kxh8 30.Qh1+ Kg8 31.Qh6 Qf8 32.Ne7+ Qxe7 33.Qg7#.
23...Kg8
A logical prophylactic move. Black does not want his h-pawn to be taken with check. The position is very complicated. Black's Bg7 is now protecting its king in 'Dragon fashion' and the silicon brain flashes with (some) zero-point-zero lines. To be honest, I am surprised that neither of the players cracked here and this crazy game ended in a draw!
24.Rah2 h5
Black's best defense was 24...Ne7! when the game may continue 25.Rxh7 Nxd5
26.Nxd5 (26.Bxd5 Qf6 looks fine for Black; again, similar to the Dragon Variation,
the dark-squared bishop is protecting its king and there is no mate) 26...Bf5. analysis diagram The white queen is urgently needed on the h-file: 27.Qf2! Rxe4+ 28.Kf1 Rxc4
29.Qh2 and most probably this mess now comes to a peaceful conclusion: 29...f6
30.Rh8+ Bxh8 31.Qxh8+ Kf7 32.Qh7+! Ke6 33.bxc4 Rb1+ 34.Kf2 Rxh1 35.Nc7+
Qxc7 36.Qxc7 Rh4 with equality.
25.Rxh5!?
Logical and principled! It is actually surprising that Black's defensive resources are sufficient. Stockfish thinks White needed to be a little more patient and would have had excellent attacking chances after 25.O-O or 25.Qg2.
25...gxh5 26.Qxh5
With his pawn defences removed, Black's king needs more defenders:
26...Re6! 27.Qh7+ Kf8 28.Rg1 Rg6 29.Rxg6 fxg6
The bishop is a strong defender; White cannot force mate by using his light-square domination only.
30.f5
If 30.Nf6, 30...Ne7! defends.
30...Qg5!
The active queen provides Black with counterplay.
31.Nf4! Ne7 32.Nxg6+ Ke8
White is a full rook down and needs to keep his attack going.
33.Nd5! Qg1+ 34.Kd2 Qg5+ 35.Ke1
Understanding there is no mate (and being a rook up), Vlad gets ambitious!
35...Bf6! 36.Ngxe7! Bxe7
37.Qh8+
Being a rook down, Ponomariov (very understandably and humanly!) looks for the most direct moves. I was very surprised that my engine considered White's domination enough compensation for the rook deficit and gave zeros after a 'pass move' like 37.Ke2.
37...Kd7 38.f6 Qg3+ 39.Ke2 Qg2+ 40.Ke3 Qg3+ 41.Ke2
Black still had winning hopes:
41...Qg4+ 42.Kf2
42.Ke3 should also have led to equality.
42...Bd8
Being a rook down, it is never easy to find quiet moves:
43.Bf1! Kc6
We could have had a spectacular finish with 43...Qxe4 44.Bg2 Qf5+ 45.Kg1 Bb7 (pinning the bishop with 45...Qg5?? leads to a disaster after 46.f7) 46.Bh3 and it peters out into a draw: 46...Bb6+ 47.Nxb6+ Kc6 48.Qxb8 Qxh3 49.Nc4 Qg3+=.
44.Qxd8
And Black decided to call it a day:
44...Qh4+ 45.Kg1 Qg3+ 1⁄2-1⁄2
I also played in this tournament, but it escaped my attention what an amazing game this was!! Chapter 5 French Defence: Winawer Variation The French is a strategically complex opening, with different (often unrelated) pawn structures, rich with ideas - enough material for a separate book. With limited space, I had to make a choice and you will notice that all my examples feature pawn structures from the Winawer Variation. Why did I choose these? Well, when I was in my 'formative years' (a junior player), I struggled to understand these Winawer type positions. I was approximately 2400 Elo, and I had trouble understanding that the positions that looked so great for White to me, were not so clear at all. Needless to say, computer programs did not exist, making my job harder. Per definition, White has more space in the centre and on the kingside, the pair of bishops, nice development, while Black has less space and a 'stupid' light-squared bishop locked in behind its own pawns. The doubled c-pawns White allows to grab the bishop pair look more like an 'academic weakness'. I assume that many of the readers will struggle to understand these complex positions just like I did forty years ago! That's why the Winawer pawn structures were my 'French choice' for this book. I have selected twelve games that, I think, illustrate well the strategic ideas for both sides in these complex positions. The weakness on e6 In the diagram, we have a typical position that is favourable for White. Black has traded his 'stupid French bishop' and even has potential c-file pressure, but he has one problem: the weakness on e6! White's knight is great on f4, keeping Black busy defending weak the e6-pawn, while White also has great prospects of a kingside attack. The first game I have selected is an excellent instructive example, Kasparov's play is logical, and his plans are relatively easy to replicate. Game 25 Garry Kasparov 2795 Nigel Short 2690 Novgorod 1997
1.e4 e6 2.d4 d5 3.Nc3 Bb4 4.e5 c5 5.a3 Bxc3+ 6.bxc3 Qc7 7.Qg4 f5
A quite consequential decision (though taken by many top players, World Champion Botvinnik included). Black creates a permanent weakness on e6 for himself.
8.Qg3
8...cxd4
Fixing the central pawn structure. The more dynamic 8...Ne7 9.Qxg7 Rg8 10.Qxh7 cxd4 was tried in several Tal-Botvinnik games in the 1960 and 1961 World Championship matches. The basic problem for Black is that the modern treatment
11.Ne2!, as in Anand-Hansen, Middelfart 2003, gives White a strong initiative.
9.cxd4 Ne7
The greedy 9...Qxc2? is not a good idea here, as White can develop quickly and harmoniously while Black has to play some awkward moves: 10.Bd2 Qc7 11.Rc1 Nc6 12.Bb5. Now, in order to develop, Black has to go for 12...Kf7 and after 13.Ne2 Nge7 14.h4 Bd7 15.h5 h6 White has an initiative worth considerably more than the sacrificed pawn. Though White doesn't have an 'instant killer', Black cannot coordinate his pieces and in computer language this position is close to plus two.
10.Bd2 O-O
11.Bd3
Black is likely to play ...b6 followed by ...Ba6, trading off his bad bishop, while White (in this game and a number of others on top level) keeps his bishop on d2. It looks to me that White can win a tempo on Kasparov's play if he takes Bxa6 in 'one go' by playing 11.c3!? here. Black still has to continue his basic plan with 11...b6 and now White seeems to have a better version of the game after 12.h4 Ba6 13.Bxa6 (or 13.Nh3 Bxf1 14.Kxf1; or 13.h5) 13...Nxa6 14.Nh3.
11...b6 12.Ne2 Ba6
The typical French trade. Black solves the problem of his bad bishop; however, he will need one piece to defend the e6-weakness.
13.Nf4
White now gets long-term strategic pressure. 13.Bb4 looks active (and has worked well for White in practice), but it is not as good as it seems: 13...Bxd3 14.cxd3 Nbc6
15.Bd6 Qd7 16.O-O.
analysis diagram A) Black now unpinned his knight while also placing his rook on an open file with
16...Rfc8?!. This looks logical but is not best: 17.h4 Ng6 18.h5 Nh8 19.h6 g6.
Black's king is weak and White senses an opportunity: 20.Nf4! Nxd4 21.Ra2 Qb7. Now White would like to attack the Nd4 but first wants to prevent the retreat to b5:
22.a4! (22.Qe3 leads to unclear play: 22...Nb5 23.Nxe6 Re8 24.a4 d4! 25.Qe1 Nxd6
26.exd6 Qd7 27.Re2 Qxd6 with complications) 22...Rc6 23.Qe3! Nc2 24.Rxc2!
Rxc2 25.Nxe6. Black's king is weak and White has a winning attack. 25...Nf7
26.Nd4 Ra2
27.Nxf5! Nxd6 28.exd6! gxf5 29.Qg5+ Kf8. The pawn defences in front of Black's
king have been removed and White's rook joins the attack, putting game to an end:
30.Re1 Qf7 31.Re7 1-0 Diez del Corral-Portisch, Buenos Aires 1978;
B) Black could have exploited the fact that White's d4-pawn is shaky and taken the initiative with 16...f4! and Black is better, e.g. 17.Qg4 (or 17.Qf3 Rf7) 17...f3
18.gxf3 Rf7.
13...Qd7 14.h4
White can (again) place his bishop on the a3-f8 diagonal with 14.Bb4, however the outcome is not clear after 14...Rc8 followed by ...Bxd3 and ...Nbc6. It is perhaps good to mention that a number of strong players blundered here with
14...Bxd3? when White gets a large advantage after 15.Nh5!. Now, Black already
struggles to find moves to stay in the game: 15...Ng6 16.Bxf8 Ba6 17.Bxg7 Qa4! (after 17...Qb5 18.O-O-O Black had no compensation and White easily won in Brkic- Nikolic, Croatia tt 2008) 18.O-O-O Be2 19.Nf4 Bxd1 20.Rxd1 Nxf4 21.Qxf4 Qxa3+
22.Kd2 Nd7!. Black has prevented immediate annihilation, but White is clearly
better after 23.Bf6.
14...Bxd3 15.Qxd3
This is the position of our interest, and a good moment to take stock to trying to understand this type of position. Quite a number of top players (former World Champion Botvinnik included) have repeatedly picked the Black side here. Black has traded the 'right bishops' and has also fixed the pawn structure for the time being, as White has no immediate attack and no immediate pawn breaks. Black also has potential pressure on the c-file and an outpost on c4. What can be Black's problem? The pawn on e6 is a permanent weakness, and it is targeted by White's Nf4. Black cannot trade off this knight unless White voluntarily agrees to it. White will push his h-pawn, at least to h5 or maybe all the way to h6. He also has the potential g-pawn push (f3 followed by g4) and has an attack along the g-file, as happened in this game. Black's c4-outpost and c-file 'pressure' are of academic value. White has long-term pressure here, and potentially a swift attack as it is easy for Black to go wrong!
15...Nbc6
Some years later, Nigel Short tried to improve by first bringing his rook to the c4- outpost: 15...Rc8. His opponent of course knew the current game and copy-catted Kasparov's play: 16.Rh3! Rc4 (Black's 'active' rook on c4 doesn't have any value)
17.Rg3 Nbc6 18.c3 Rf8. Just like Garry, White now first took the time to improve
his king's position: 19.Kf1 Rf7 20.Kg1 Na5 21.a4 Qc6 22.Ra3 Kf8. Now is the time to bring the queen to the kingside (just like Garry): 23.Qb1! Nb7. Here, Black 'equalized' with a draw offer and the draw was agreed in Teran Alvarez-Short, Lanzarote 2003. In reality, if he had not been impressed by his opponent's high Elo rating, White could have continued like Kasparov with 24.Qd1 and built up a risk-free kingside attack. Stockfish 15 at a decent depth evaluates it as more than plus two! Kasparov goes for the immediate rook lift:
16.Rh3
16.h5 followed by Rh3 was an alternative.
16...Rac8
16...Ng6 most probably transposes into the same type position as in the game as
White retreats with 17.Ne2 followed by 18.h5.
17.Rg3 Rf7
A classic game by Botvinnik also mirrors the strategic decisions players have to take in this type of position, so let's have a look: 17...Kh8 18.h5 A) If Black is not in the mood to play ...h6, he can choose to transfer his knight to f7 with 18...Nd8 19.Kf1 (in case of 19.h6 g6 20.Kf1 Nf7 Black controls the entry points) 19...Nf7. analysis diagram White is better here as Black does not have any active counterplay. White will have to combine kingside threats with opening up the queenside to penetrate there; B) Reshevsky chose to determine the kingside pawn structure: 18...Rf7 19.h6 g6
20.Rc1 Rff8 21.Ne2! (preparing Bd2-g5-f6) 21...Nb8 22.Kf1 Rc4 23.Kg1 Nbc6
24.Bg5! Ng8 25.Re1 Qf7 26.c3 Na5 27.Nf4 Rc6.
B1) Probably in time pressure (which Reshevsky was known for), White gets impatient: 28.Bf6+? Nxf6 29.exf6 Nc4 (White can no longer play Rge3 and simply loses a pawn for nothing) 30.Qb1 (30.Qe2 Qxf6 31.Nxe6?? drops a piece to the simple 31...Re8) 30...Qxf6. Black is now a pawn up with an excellent position and won easily in Reshevsky-Botvinnik, The Hague/Moscow 1948; B2) The idea of Bf6+ followed by Nf4-h3-g5 needed to be prepared: 28.Rge3! Nc4
29.R3e2 Ne7 (29...Nxa3?? loses to 30.Bf6+! Nxf6 31.exf6 Re8 32.Nxd5) 30.Bf6+
Kg8. Now White improves his knight with 31.Nh3! Rfc8 32.Ng5 Qe8 33.a4 and Black has no active play. White will combine opening up the queenside with f3 and g4 (opening up the kingside). However, bear in mind that though your computer engine will give a clear advantage to White (considerably more than plus one!), White will need a precise execution to gain the full point.
18.h5
In the next couple of moves, Black has to decide whether to push ...h6 or not and White whether to push h6 himself (as Black will give him a couple of opportunities). It seems like Kasparov and Short mutually established ...h6 to be weakening for Black. Such pawn structure decisions are hard to take as there are no direct variations supporting them. Still, I think Black had to play 18...h6 here.
18...Nd8 19.c3 Rf8
Again, 19...h6 came into consideration.
20.Kf1 Rc4 21.Kg1 Nf7 22.a4 Rfc8
And again, 22...h6 was to be considered. Kasparov now skilfully improves his queen, generating different ideas:
23.Qb1!
Black has a passive position; however, the idea of holding on by 'sitting on his hands' will not work:
23...Nc6?
23...h6 was needed, though now White combines kingside pressure with the opening
of the queenside: 24.Qb5! (please note that a trade of the type 24.Ng6? Nxg6
25.Rxg6 is a terrible strategic mistake here, as White loses the crucial pressure on e6:
25...Nh8 26.Rg3 Nf7 and Black is doing well) 24...Qc6 25.Ra3 Kh7.
analysis diagram How can White improve here? Well, by opening up and penetrating the queenside:
26.Nd3! Qe8 (Black cannot say 'pass' as after for example 26...Kh8 White forces the
