decision with 27.Nb2) 27.Qxe8 Rxe8 28.a5! b5 29.a6!. White's Rg3 now transfers to the queenside - Rg3-e3-e1-b1 - and Black has serious difficulties here.
24.Qd1
White could have considered 24.h6 g6 25.Nh5 Kh8 26.Nf6 as Black cannot remove the Nf6 with ...Ne7 and ...Ng8 due to 26...Qd8 27.Qb5! Ne7 28.a5!.
24...Ne7
Black now pays the price for not pushing ...h6. We are no longer just talking domination here, White gets a mating attack!
25.h6! g6
25...Nxh6?? loses a piece for nothing after the simple 26.Nh5 Ng4 27.f3 or 26...Ng6?
27.Bxh6+-.
26.Qh5!
Unable to stop White's threats, Black, understanding his position is lost anyway, grabs a pawn:
26...Rxa4
26...Kf8 runs into 27.Qh4 with 28. Qf6 to follow, and if 27...Ng8?, White wins with
28.Rxg6 hxg6 29.Nxg6+ Ke8 30.h7.
27.Rxa4
White had a pleasant alternative in 27.Re1 with the same winning attack as in the previous comment: 27...Kf8 28.Qh4 etc.
27...Qxa4
Probably feeling the job has been done, Kasparov now relaxes and does not execute in the best way:
28.Nxe6
A) The straightforward 28.Nxg6? hxg6 29.Rxg6+ Nxg6 30.Qxg6+ Kf8 31.h7 does not win as after 31...Qd1+ 32.Kh2 Qxd2 33.Qf6 Black has perpetual check with
33...Qf4+ 34.Kg1 Qc1+;
B) The most precise execution was the 'prophylactic attacking king move' 28.Kh2!. White now threatens 29. Nxg6 while, compared to 28. Nxg6 line, Black can no longer save himself by perpetual check, e.g.: B1) 28...Qd7 29.Nxg6 hxg6 30.Rxg6+ Nxg6 31.Qxg6+ Kf8 32.h7 Qd8 33.Bg5; B2) 28...Qc2 only delays the inevitable after 29.Be3 or the immediate 29.Qh4; B3) 28...Rc6 29.Nxg6 hxg6 30.Rxg6+ Nxg6 31.Qxg6+ Kf8 32.h7 (now Black does not have the saving check on d1) 32...Qd1 33.Bh6+ Ke7 34.Bg5+.
28...Qc2?
This loses on the spot. Interestingly, Black could have continued fighting with 28...Rc6! when White's next two moves are more or less forced: 29.Nf4 Kf8 30.Qe2 analysis diagram and now after 30...g5! yes, White does stand better but the issue is far from decided.
29.Qh4! f4 30.Bxf4 Nf5 31.Rxg6+
Black resigned as after 31...hxg6 White has various ways to win, the following line being convincing enough: 32.h7+ Kh8 33.Qf6+ Kxh7 (the only thing White needs to pay attention to is not to blunder into a perpetual check by ...Qd1+ and ...Qh5+)
34.Qxf7+ Kh8 and now White can go for mate: 35.Qf6+ (the simple 35.Qxg6 wins
as well) 35...Kh7 36.Ng5+ Kh6 37.Ne4+ Kh7 38.Qf7+ Kh8 39.Nf6 Ng7 40.Bh6. Kingside attack White is going to play for a kingside attack, while Black will push his pawns on the queenside, trying to create counterplay. This particular position is favourable for White because his knight on f4 is excellently placed, supporting a quick kingside attack. The same (pawn structure) position with a white knight on f3 would have had been rather unclear (as it will take White more time to start a kingside attack. I have selected two games emphasizing this point. The fixed central pawn chain Game 26 Sergey Karjakin 2778 Francisco Vallejo Pons 2697 Sao Paulo/Bilbao 2012
1.e4 e6 2.d4 d5 3.Nc3 Bb4 4.e5 c5 5.a3 Bxc3+ 6.bxc3 Ne7 7.Qg4
7...O-O
Another main, more theoretical line here is 7...cxd4 8.Qxg7 Rg8 9.Qxh7 Qc7. I will not analyse it in this book because there are too many theoretical ('computer prep') lines here. The sideline 7...Kf8 avoids direct threats; however, Black will need to lose time solving his king problem, as for example in Anand-Xiong, Wijk aan Zee 2020. The sideline 7...Nf5 is no longer played at top level, and probably for a good reason, see Stein-Petrosian, Moscow 1961 further on in this French chapter.
8.Bd3
Modern theory regards this as the main line here. 8.Nf3 used to be played at top level - see Mecking-Korchnoi, Wijk aan Zee 1978. Black is now at a crossroads; he has to decide on the type of pawn structure we are going to get.
8...Nbc6
This leads to a closed structure. The alternative 8...f5 9.exf6 Rxf6, resulting in a quite different pawn structure, is another often played line here - see Anand- Timman, Dortmund 1999.
9.Qh5!
Modern theory considers this the most precise move here.
9.Nf3 can still transpose into Mecking-Korchnoi (the move-order with 8.Nf3 Nbc6
9.Bd3).
9...Ng6
Seeing this position, I recall playing my first Dortmund super-tournament 34 years ago, walking along the tables and seeing a former Soviet champion, the famous grandmaster Lev Psakhis, getting massacred. Engines did not exist at the time... opening preparation was at a considerably lower level, and poor Lev played 9...h6?? when thunder struck at once with 10.Bxh6!. Black's position is already lost!
10...gxh6 11.Qxh6 Nf5 12.Bxf5 exf5 13.O-O-O f4 (or 13...c4 14.Nh3 Qe7 15.Nf4+-)
14.Nh3 (even more effective/brutal was 14.Rd3 Ne7
analysis diagram
15.Rg3+!! Ng6 16.Ne2) 14...Bf5 15.Nxf4 with a winning attack; White won after a
few more moves in Kindermann-Psakhis, Dortmund 1989. The kingside pawn structure is likely to change soon. White will soon capture Bxg6 when Black recaptures ...fxg6 so White positions himself for this change of structure.
10.Nf3
Modern theory considers 10.Nh3! to be the most precise, see Caruana-Shankland, Saint Louis 2016.
10...Qc7 11.Be3
Vallejo decides to force the mentioned pawn structure change.
11...c4
Black can opt for a different pawn structure with 11...Nce7!? 12.h4 Nf5 when the pawns are not fixed and the game is rather 'fluid'. We have an interesting game of Anand here. White's chances are related to a kingside attack, so the knight on f5 needs to be harassed: 13.g4! Nxe3 14.fxe3 cxd4. A critical moment! A) Vishy opts for the most logical continuation, but it's not the best: 15.cxd4 Qc3+!
16.Ke2 Bd7. White's exposed king should provide Black with enough counterplay
for a draw: 17.Rab1! (White eliminates Black's ...Bb5 while penetrating the b-file; if

With material equal, White has an attack plus domination, and he went on to win, Anand-Lputian, Moscow 2004; B) Precise was 15.O-O!. With his king in safety, White can focus on the attack:
15...f6 (Black gets mated in case of 15...dxe3 16.Ng5 h6 17.Bxg6 fxg6 18.Qxg6

White wants to double his rooks or take on g6 and collect a pawn for free. 17...Be8? does not help as White simply builds up with 18.Raf1 and has a winning attack.
12.Bxg6 fxg6 13.Qg4
White's central pawn chain makes it difficult for Black to obtain immediate counterplay here, while White wants to regroup and start a pawn storm on the kingside. Vallejo now regroups well, slowing down White's pawn storm:
13...Qf7!

The 'active' 14...Qf5? traps his own queen after 15.Qe2 with a close to winning advantage for White in Kasparov-Short, Munich 1994.

22.Bc5 Rc6 23.Bd6 Rf7
A razor-sharp position, and both players were probably under severe time pressure. The objective assessment is a 'dynamic balance'... well... yes, if computer engines are playing. In a game between humans, (serious) mistakes are bound to happen. I strongly recommend to test your calculation skills here (and in the ensuing positions) and compare the results with the silicon brain lines. 38.Qc8+ 1-0
The closed centre: White gets a 'perfect' attack Game 27 Fabiano Caruana 2796 Samuel Shankland 2656 Saint Louis ch-USA 2016 Perhaps it would be going too far to say that this game 'closed' the variation seen in Karjakin-Vallejo Pons but actually, in essence it did! Caruana regroups his pieces better and thereby gets a better/quicker attack. The mistakes Shankland made... well, it's not easy to say... could Sam have had played it differently? Yes. Could he at any point in time got something like equality? No. After this game (up to the moment of this writing) we no longer have top level games in this line. So, let's see.
1.e4 e6 2.d4 d5 3.Nc3 Bb4 4.e5 Ne7 5.a3 Bxc3+ 6.bxc3 c5 7.Qg4 O-O 8.Bd3 Nbc6
9.Qh5 Ng6
So far we are following Karjakin-Vallejo. White's chances are on the kingside. Caruana readies his attack much better than Karjakin:
10.Nh3!
What is point behind this knight development? Caruana (correctly) anticipates that soon, just like in Karjakin-Vallejo Pons, the transaction ...c4; Bxg6 fxg6 is going to happen and he wants to be able to place his knight on f4 and immediately start pushing his kingside pawns. White will win considerable time compared to Karjakin- Vallejo Pons. Karjakin took three moves to place his knight on h3 while Caruana needed only one, while the pawn structure and the situation were more or less the same. For 10.Nf3 see Karjakin-Vallejo Pons, Sao Paulo/Bilbao 2012 (previous game).
10...Qc7
If Black wants to deviate, he should now or on the next move analyse 10...Nce7 though it is not clear if this brings any advantages to Black. For example, now White a slightly unusual tactical motif created by Black's last move: 11.Bh6 and after
11...Nf5 (11...gxh6?? 12.Qxh6 leads to a disaster for Black) 12.Bd2 it is not clear
what Black has achieved with his knight pirouette, while the pawn grab 12...cxd4
13.cxd4
analysis diagram
13...Nxd4?? is a suicide after the simple 14.Ng5 h6 15.Nxf7.
11.Be3 c4
Here Black can also decide to deviate with 11...Nce7 and here we have a top-level game with Judit Polgar playing White. Black hopes to be 'more ready' for White's kingside attack. True to her style, Judit takes immediate action, and doesn't mind sacrificing a pawn in the process: 12.f4 (12.g4!? with a similar plan was another interesting option) 12...Qa5 13.Bd2 Qa4
14.g4! (direct and principled! In case of 14.dxc5 Black gets some compensation for
the pawn after 14...b6). Black will be under attack anyway, so he'd better grab a pawn: 14...c4! 15.Be2 Qxc2 16.Rc1 Qb2. Now White needs to provoke ...h6 in order to create a target: 17.Ng5 h6 18.Nh3 f6 19.g5 hxg5. A critical moment! Judit may have underestimated Black's defensive resources:
20.exf6? (she had to move her bishop to the b1-h7 diagonal: 20.Bd1! followed by
Bc2, with good compensation) 20...Rxf6! (prepares ...Nf8) 21.Nxg5 Nf8 and suddenly Black was better, though later the game ended in a draw, J. Polgar-Hillarp Persson, Malmö 2000.
12.Bxg6 fxg6 13.Qg4 Qf7 14.Nf4!
The point behind 10.Nh3.

We have a situation similar to the Karjakin-Vallejo game, White having more or less two extra tempi.

Forcing Black into absolute passivity.

36.Bc7 Ne7 37.Bd6 Nc6 38.Kg2 Kh7 39.Kh3 Kg8 40.Kg3 Kh7
Obviously White has total domination, but how to win? Caruana's plan is instructive: fix the kingside when Black will have no moves, bring the Nf4 to the queenside and Black's defence, already pushed to the limit, will eventually break.
41.Nh3 Kg8 42.g5! h5
Black cannot say 'pass' as 42...Kh7? loses even faster after 43.gxh6 gxh6 44.Nf2. Now the knight starts on its journey.
43.Nf4! Kh7 44.Ng2 Bg8 45.Ne3 Bf7 46.Nc2 Bg8 47.Na3 Bf7
Not willing to risk blundering, Caruana brings his king to 'maximum safety' first.
48.Kf2 Bg8 49.Ke3 Bf7

50.Kd2 Bg8 51.Kc2 Bf7 52.Kc1
White's king is safe; now the knight will enter, forcing resignation.
52...Kg8
52...Bg8 is a similar story: 53.Nb5 and let's say Black hopes for some miracle
perpetual check: 53...Ne7 54.Na7! (54.Bxe7 is not a piece win after 54...Rb8!)
54...Qf7 55.Nxc8 Qf4+ 56.Kb1 Qd2 57.Nxe7. Black has lost all his pieces and only
has a few checks to give before resigning.
53.Nb5! Ne7
54.Na7! 1-0
Not 54.Bxe7?? when Black gets his piece back with 54...Rb8! and has good chances to save himself. An impressive and instructive game by Caruana. A dream situation for Black! Black has pushed his f-pawn, and the transaction exf6 Rxf6 has taken place. Black has timed his ...c4-pawn push well, gaining space and removing White's Bd3 from its active post. Black has also managed the difficult to execute improvement of his queen's bishop (...Bd7-e8-g6). White's bishops are passive (hitting their own pawns), while his damaged queenside structure is no longer an 'academic weakness'. This was a great game by Korchnoi, as we will see below. A true masterclass! Black plays ...f6 exf6 Rxf6 Game 28 Henrique Mecking 2630 Viktor Korchnoi 2665 Wijk aan Zee 1978 This game is very instructive for a good understanding of this type of positions and is another great example of how a game can be lost while playing only 'logical' moves.
1.e4 e6 2.d4 d5 3.Nc3 Bb4 4.e5 c5 5.a3 Bxc3+ 6.bxc3 Ne7 7.Qg4 O-O 8.Nf3
As mentioned in this chapter, modern theory regards 8.Bd3 as best and in this particular line, 8.Bd3 offers White extra possibilities - see Anand-Timman, Game 29.
8...Nbc6 9.Bd3
Korchnoi now goes for another typical Winawer pawn structure:
9...f5 10.exf6 Rxf6
We have a theoretical position, seen in many top-level games. Let's try to take stock. Black has seemingly weakened his kingside (pawn structure). White, with his active queen, bishop pair and knight, is ready to attack (he definitely has enough attackers) and can force Black to play either ...h6 or ...g6, weakening his king position further. Based on these arguments, logically White should be doing very well here. However, in reality things are rather complex. White's attack is not easy to develop, and Black's king is safer than it looks. White's active queen can be easily targeted (the ...e5 break, the ...Nf5 jump), the seemingly well placed Bd3 can be easily removed from its post by the ...c4 push. Your computer engine will tell you that the position is complex and that Black is not worse. However, I remember analysing these positions about 35-40 years back - in pre- computer days, when I was already a strong junior player. Firstly, I did not exactly understand why a player like Korchnoi kept playing such 'stupid positions' as Black. Next, I was not able to find a convincing way for White to develop his attack, and ultimately I came to the conclusion that these positions were unclear and Black may easily get the upper hand.
11.Qh5
Mecking decides to provoke ...h6. White could also have decided to provoke ...g6 with 11.Bg5 Rf7: A) Parting with his bishop in order to force Black's rook to e7 does not bring White an advantage: 12.Bxe7 Rxe7 13.Qh4 g6 and Black's king is less weak than it may look: 14.O-O. Now we see an important part of Black's strategy in this type of position. White's bishop is removed from its post, while Black takes space! 14...c4! 15.Be2 Qf8
16.Rfe1 Bd7 17.Bf1 Rae8 (Black is harmoniously developed and soon it will be the
second player who explores his attacking options on the kingside) 18.Qg5 Rf7
19.Qd2 Qd6 (White has to start watching out for the ...Rxf3 exchange sacrifice)
20.Ne5 Nxe5 21.Rxe5 Ref8. Black's pressure on the f-file fully compensates for the
weakness of his e6-pawn and - very important to notice - White's weak a3-pawn needs to be defended(!). Black had a comfortable position and went on to win in Ljubojevic-Beliavsky, Tilburg 1984; B) 12.Qh5 g6 13.Qh4. At first glance, White has a great attacking position. The reality, however, is quite different. First, White's bishops will be removed from their attacking posts and then his queen suddenly becomes a target. Probably, the objective assessment of the position should be 'dynamic, complex play with mutual chances'. 13...c4! 14.Be2 Qa5! 15.Bd2 Nf5 16.Qg5 (readers interested in opening theory should explore
16.Qh3!? here as White is now more ready for his g4 pawn push, while 16...e5? does
not seem to work as after 17.Nxe5! Nxe5 18.dxe5 Black does not have a good jump with his Nf5) 16...Bd7. Now, aiming to seize the initiative, Ljubojevic makes a risky decision: 17.g4!? (the alternative was 17.O-O with unclear play after 17...Nd6 or
17...Raf8 followed by ...Nf5-d6-e4) 17...Nd6 18.h4 Ne4 19.Qe3.
A critical moment! Black can seize the initiative but, as usual in such sharp positions, precision is needed. B1) Korchnoi went for 19...Raf8. After 20.h5 gxh5 21.Rxh5 Rg7 the game was unclear and White eventually emerged victorious in Ljubojevic-Korchnoi, Linares 1985; B2) The immediate central pawn break is Black's best option here: 19...e5! and White is in trouble. His best option is to try to create attacking chances of his own with 20.h5 exd4
21.Nxd4. An important moment! Black should keep the h-file closed! 21...g5!
(21...Re8 22.hxg6 hxg6 23.Rh6 with unclear play and a later draw was seen in Ernst- Hansen, Ostersund 1992). Now White has to disturb Black's knight: 22.f3 (22.Nxc6? Bxc6 23.f3 is wrong as after 23...d4! 24.cxd4 Nxd2 25.Qxd2 c3 26.Qd3 Qc7 White's king is weak) 22...Nxd4 and Black gets a better endgame after 23.cxd4 (23.Qxd4? is asking for trouble in view of 23...Re8!) 23...Qxd2+ 24.Qxd2 Nxd2 25.Kxd2 Re8.
11...h6 12.O-O
Now Black takes an important and typical strategic decision in such positions which we already mentioned in the above comments:
12...c4!
White Bd3 is removed from its post while Black gains space! It is important to note that Black should not postpone this decision for too long as at some stage White will take dxc5.
13.Be2 Qa5 14.Bd2
A fancy move like 14.Bg5? does not work for White here: 14...hxg5 15.Nxg5 Ng6 and White will soon regret his decision to sacrifice.
14...Bd7
An important moment! White has to be careful not to become worse and needs to jump with his knight to e5 here.
15.Rfb1?!
15.Ne5! was needed and now:
A) 15...Be8! 16.Qh4 (White does not get enough compensation for his pawn after
16.Qh3 Nxd4 17.Bg4 Ndf5)
16...Nf5 (an interesting strategic decision here was to enter a 2 Ns vs 2Bs fight with
16...Bg6!? 17.Nxg6 Nxg6 18.Qg4 Raf8 where Black is definitely not worse)
17.Nxc6 (17.Qg4? Nfxd4) 17...Bxc6 18.Qf4 Raf8 with equality in the game Jaracz-
Khusnutdinov, chess.com 2021; B) 15...Nxe5 16.Qxe5 Nc6 is less convincing for Black as after 17.Qe3 Be8 18.Bg4 Bg6 19.Bxe6+ Kh7 20.Bd7 Bxc2 21.Rfe1 Raf8 22.f3 Qc7
23.Bxc6! though your engine will give you around zeros, this opposite-coloured
bishops position is, due to his safer king and more active bishop, easier to play for White who ended up victorious in the game Zelcic-Nisipeanu, Zagreb 2019.
15...Qc7 16.Nh4?
White wants to push f4 in order to stop Black's ...e5 push. The consequence of this strategic decision (a mistake that will cost White the game) is that White's Bd2 will be passive, surrounded by its own pawns, whilc Black's bishop will become a monster once it is transferred to g6. This was White's last chance to go for 16.Ne5! and after 16...Be8 17.Qg4 Nxe5... analysis diagram ... White needs to decide on the dynamic 18.Qg3! (18.dxe5 is better for Black after
18...Qxe5 19.Rxb7 Qd6). Now the position looks better for Black to me, however
Stockfish finds dynamic ways for White to keep the balance.
16...Raf8 17.f4 Be8! 18.Qg4
18...Ng6!
The right trade!
19.Nxg6 Bxg6
White is strategically lost! Korchnoi's execution is instructive and done with a steady hand.
20.Ra2
Black's Nc6 needs to be improved.
20...Ne7! 21.Rab2 b6 22.Qh3 Nf5 23.Re1 Qe7 24.Ra2
Note the poor Ra2 - out of play and defending the weak a3- and c2-pawns. Black's N+B totally dominate White's two Bs here.
24...Nd6 25.Bf3 Ne4 26.Bxe4
A sad neccessity. White has to enter a strategically lost opposite-coloured bishops position.
26...Bxe4 27.g3 Qf7 28.Qg4
Time for Black to open up the kingside and launch a direct attack:
28...g5!
Now White has to try to bring his rook into play, so Black can collect a pawn for free.
29.Raa1 Rf5 30.Rf1 Bxc2 31.Rf2 Qg6 32.Re1 Be4
White could have safely resigned here. Black threatens 33...h5 and White's position will collapse. The rest of the game does not need any comment: White gives an exchange and, probably driven by inertia, plays some more unnecessary moves.
33.Rxe4 dxe4 34.Kf1 Rb5 35.Qe2 Rb1+ 36.Kg2 Rb2 37.Qxc4 e3 38.Bxe3 Qe4+
39.Kh3 Rxf2 40.Bxf2 gxf4 41.gxf4 Rxf4 42.Bg3 Rf5 43.Qc8+ Rf8 44.Qc4 h5
45.Qb5 Qg4+ 46.Kg2 h4
And White resigned. A powerful instructive strategic lecture by Korchnoi! A happy scenario for White The pawn structure is almost the same as in the previous diagram, but with some small differences! Here, Black's bishop is passive on d7 while White's bishop is excellently placed on e5, and Black's king is potentially vulnerable. White is happy here. This was a great game by Anand. The advantage of White's knight still being on g1 Game 29 Viswanathan Anand 2771 Jan Timman 2650 Dortmund 1999
1.e4 e6 2.d4 d5 3.Nc3 Bb4 4.e5 Ne7 5.a3 Bxc3+ 6.bxc3 c5 7.Qg4 O-O 8.Bd3
As we will see, this move-order gives White an important extra option compared to the game Mecking-Korchnoi we just saw.
8...f5 9.exf6 Rxf6
Now White first provokes ...g6, weakening the dark squares:
10.Bg5 Rf7 11.Qh5 g6
Now White uses the fact that he played 8.Bd3 instead of 8.Nf3:
12.Qd1!
Black now has a vulnerable kingside while, compared to Ljubojevic-Korchnoi (in the comments to the game Mecking-Korchnoi), White's queen is not exposed.
12...Nbc6
Black will place his queen on f8 in order to push ...h6. Another possible plan for Black was 12...Qa5 13.Bd2 and now: A) In case Black opts for 13...Nbc6 14.Nf3 c4 (opening the centre with 14...Qc7
15.O-O e5 plays into White's hands here as after 16.dxe5 Nxe5 17.Be2 Bd7 18.Re1
the open position suits the side with a bishop pair, and also Black has problems defending his central d5-pawn, e.g. 18...Nxf3+ 19.Bxf3 Bc6 20.Bg5 (20...Rd8, moving under the pin, is not really an option here) 20...Raf8 21.Bxe7 Rxe7
22.Bxd5+ Bxd5 23.Qxd5+ and White was a sound pawn up and went on to win in
Eljanov-Naiditsch, Douglas 2016) 15.Be2 Bd7. Yes, Black did close the centre, but compared to Ljubojevic-Korchnoi, White's queen cannot be targeted and the dark-square weaknesses in Black's camp become important. Black struggles to create counterplay: 16.O-O Kg7 17.Qc1! Ng8 18.Bf4. In this difficult position, Black sacrifices an exchange in the hope to get counterplay:
18...Raf8 19.Bd6 Qxc3. White is not in a hurry to take the Rf8 and will do so at a
moment of his choosing: 20.a4! Nf6 21.Rd1 Ne4 22.Ra3 Qa5 23.Bxf8+ Rxf8 24.c3 and White had a material advantage and went on to win in Topalov-Jussupow, Dortmund 1997; B) 13...Nd7 14.Nf3. Now Black follows a standard plan in this type position: 14...c4
15.Be2 Nc6.
White decides, similar to Mecking vs Korchnoi, to gain space and to disallow Black's ...e5 pawn push: 16.Ng5 (instead, White could have started an unpleasant immediate attack with 16.h4! followed by h5, Ng5, and Black's situation would not have been easy) 16...Re7 17.f4 Nf6 18.O-O. If we compared this to Mecking-Korchnoi (as we have the same central pawn structure), White is in considerably better shape as Black's bishop cannot be placed on g6. However White has also limited his own dark-squared bishop and game is balanced: 18...Bd7 19.a4 Rf8 20.Bf3.
20...Nd8! (Black wants to trade off the Ng5 and then jump with his own knight to the
central e4-square) 21.Qc1 (on 21.Bc1, Black has a promising exchange sacrifice:
21...Qxc3! 22.Ba3 Rfe8 with ...Nc6 to follow and a great game) 21...Nf7 22.Qa3
Ree8 (the position is about equal) 23.Nxf7 Rxf7 24.Rfe1 Bc6. Not seeing a better way to proceed, White simplifies into a drawn endgame: 25.Qb4 Qxb4 26.cxb4 Ne4
27.Bxe4 dxe4 28.c3 Bd5 and a draw was soon agreed in Karjakin-Carlsen, Sao
Paulo/Bilbao 2012.
13.Nf3 c4
We have one top-level game with an instructive strategic mistake. Let's see it:
13...Qf8 14.O-O c4 15.Be2 h6 16.Bc1! Bd7 17.Ne1 (there was no reason to hurry
with this knight retreat; 17.a4!, keeping all options open, looked promising) 17...g5
18.g3 Nf5 19.Ng2 Qg7. Now White would like to open the position for his dark
squared bishop: 20.f4 Nd6 And now comes a mutual strategic/ positional blunder:
21.Qe1? (good, and in the spirit of 20.f4, was 21.fxg5! Rxf1+ (or 21...hxg5 22.Rxf7
with 23.Ne3 to follow) 22.Bxf1 hxg5 23.Ne3 and White has a promising position: his bishop pair will start working, and the black king can become vulnerable)
21...b5? (Black misses a great opportunity to close the position and play for
domination on the light squares with 21...g4! with the simple plan ...h5, ...Ne4, ...Ne7, ...Nf5 and White's Bc1 may easily be reduced to a 'big pawn'). Now White did not miss his second chance and played 22.fxg5! Rxf1+ 23.Bxf1 hxg5 24.Ne3 Rf8
25.Bg2 and White had a better game and went on to win in Ponomariov-Ivanchuk,
Linares 2002. Vishy is going to use the power of his bishops combined with Black's weak king position and will win this game with great ease. It's really not clear where Timman might have made a mistake from here on.
14.Be2 Qf8
Now Anand cleverly delays castling and places his bishop on the dominant central e5-square:
15.Qc1!
Black has to push through ...h6 now.
15...Nf5 16.h4! h6 17.Bf4 Bd7
White makes sure his dark-squared bishop remains the dominant piece for the rest of the game:
18.Ne5! Nxe5 19.Bxe5 Ne7
Black needs to chase away White's bishop from e5.
20.Qe3 Nc6 21.Bg3 Re8
22.h5! g5
Now White will soon come with the f4 push; the kingside will be opened and Black's king will be vulnerable.
23.O-O Ne7 24.Be5! Nc6 25.Bh2 Rg7
Black has no counterplay, while White easily improves his pieces further.
26.Bg4! Qf6 27.Rae1 Qe7 28.a4 b6 29.Qh3!
Black's only chance here is to grab a pawn and hope...
29...Nd8 30.Re2 Bxa4
31.f4! Bd7 32.f5! Rf7 33.Rfe1
Black's position is collapsing so Timman seizes his best practical chance: he gives an exchange and hopes to reach a drawish ending.
33...Rf6 34.Be5 Rxf5 35.Bxf5 exf5 36.Bc7 Ne6 37.Qxf5
Black has to trade queens in order not to get mated:
37...Qf7 38.Qxf7+ Kxf7 39.Bd6 a5
Unfortunately for Black, his queenside pawns do not offer any real hopes of counterplay. Anand wraps up efficiently.
40.Rf1+ Kg7 41.Re5! Nf4 42.Rxe8 Bxe8
Now, an important check:
43.Be5+! Kg8 44.Bxf4 gxf4 45.Rxf4
46.Rf6 needs to be stopped, so Black has no time to take White's h5-pawn.
45...Kg7 46.g4 a4 47.Kh2 1-0
An instructive victory by Anand! A double-edged position This is a typical modern double-edged position. We have many recent top games here (Ruslan Ponomariov and Richard Rapport are currently the main protagonists for the Black side). This is a very popular type of pawn structure - take your time to study the next three games thoroughly! White doesn't need to worry yet about the a4-pawn or doubled c-pawns, but at some stage, he may. White has a considerable kingside space advantage, but the tricky bit is: at a convenient moment, Black wants to open the position with ...f6 or ...f5... so both sides are actually playing on the kingside! The ensuing positions are very complex and easy to misplay. Closed position, typical plans, exposed white queen Game 30 Zoltan Almasi 2650 Alexander Beliavsky 2615 Hungary tt 1996/97 This game is another great example of how strategically sensitive these positions are and how easy it is, even for a world class player, to get into trouble. In a similar way as in Mecking-Korchnoi, Almasi makes 'logical moves' and still finds himself in terrible trouble in no time!
1.e4 e6 2.d4 d5 3.Nc3 Bb4 4.e5 c5 5.a3 Bxc3+ 6.bxc3 Ne7 7.Qg4 Qc7
This move usually leads to very sharp lines where theoretical knowledge and memory are of crucial importance. If White wants to avoid long, computer-prepared lines here, sometimes he opts for:
8.Bd3
Mainstream theory is 8.Qxg7 when after 8...Rg8 9.Qxh7 cxd4 White has an equally interesting choice between 10.Ne2, 10.Kd1!? or 10.Qd3. The resulting positions are sharp, heavily (engine-)analysed, and I have not included them in this book because (cloud) computer engines preparation, good memory etc. are more important elements here than understanding Winawer fundamentals.
8.Bd2 is another attempt to avoid theory; however, it is little played as White fails to
find an advantage in the lines after 8...cxd4 9.cxd4 Qxc2.
8.Bd3 is a tricky sideline where Black needs to know his stuff! Best here is to use the
standard way to immediately close the position in the centre:
8...c4!
8...h5?, trying to force White into sharp Qxg7-type lines, can lead to a disaster for
Black: 9.Qxg7 Rg8 10.Qh6 cxd4 11.Ne2. With White's bishop already developed to d3, Black has a terrible version of the 8. Qxg7 lines. After 11...Nbc6 12.cxd4 Bd7
13.Be3 O-O-O 14.Qxh5 White had a massive advantage and soon won in Velimirovic-
Uhlmann, Vrsac 1973. The pawn grab 8...cxd4 is not really a good idea either: 9.Ne2 analysis diagram
9...dxc3 (or 9...Qxe5 10.cxd4 h5 - (of course, not blundering a queen with
10...Qf6?? 11.Bg5) - 11.Qh4 Qc7 12.O-O when Black has problems finishing his
development and with his king in the centre; White has more than sufficient compensation for the pawn) 10.Qxg7 Rg8 11.Qxh7 Qxe5 12.h4. Black is a pawn up, but the h-pawn is fast. White has an excellent version of the 8.Qxg7 lines here.
9.Be2 Qa5
It would be a serious strategic mistake to compare this position to Mecking- Korchnoi, thinking 'White will play 10.Nf3 next' and castling kingside here. Beliavsky made this mistake and was swiftly punished for it two years prior to this game: 9...O-O?. Unfortunately for Black, White's Ng1 stays on its initial square for the time being:
10.h4! f6. Black hopes to open the centre and get counterplay - sorry, this will not
happen! The centre stays closed: 11.f4!. White now simply rolls his kingside pawns while Black does not have any meaningful counterplay. White has a massive advantage already: 11...Nbc6 12.Nf3
12...b5 (one of the earlier examples went 12...Qa5 13.Bd2 Qa4 14.Ra2 and now
White took action on the kingside and quickly won in Velimirovic-Lengyel, Vrnjacka Banja 1973) 13.h5 a5 (Black has futile hopes of counterplay; in case of
13...h6, White builds up a kingside attack with the simple 14.Qh3 a5 15.g4) 14.Bd2
(the immediate 14.h6 was also good) 14...Rb8. Given the absence of counterplay for Black, White has different ways to proceed here. Polgar is not in a hurry: 15.O-O. Now Black does not have much of a choice but to push his b-pawn: 15...b4 16.axb4 axb4 17.cxb4 c3 (in case of 17...Nxb4, Black lands in a strategically lost position after 18.h6 g6 19.exf6 Rxf6 20.Bxb4 Rxb4 21.Ne5) 18.Bxc3 Nxe5 19.fxe5 Qxc3
20.Bd3 (20.Ra7 wins as well) 20...h6 (or 20...Rxb4 21.Ra7) 21.b5 and White is up a
pawn and has the better position on top of it. White quickly won in Polgar- Beliavsky, Linares 1994. The immediate 9...Nf5 was also possible, leading to a type of play similar to the text.
10.Bd2 Nf5
11.Nf3
In a higher sense, this natural developing move is the start of White's troubles as Almasi is not aware of the exposure his queen may be subjected to. The position is dynamically balanced. Black is going to castle queenside and prepare the opening of the kingside (most probably with the ...f6 push) while White also needs to watch out a bit on the queenside, given his pawn structure weaknesses there. Computer engines respect space in such positions, so your engine will give you a 'White's advantage' assessment but things are far from the way they initially seem and 'dynamic balance' is a better assessment here. I will now give a recent example of another very strong grandmaster getting into trouble with White in this line: 11.h4 (Ivan Saric, in a game against Furlan, has a reasonable idea: he pushes pawns and gains space on the kingside) 11...Qa4 12.Bd1 Nc6 13.h5 h6 14.Qf4 Bd7 15.g4 Nfe7. It's time for White to develop the Ng1. Compared to Almasi's queen in our game, White's queen is far from exposed here, so Saric is in good shape: 16.Nf3 Rg8 (preparing queenside castling) 17.Nh4 O-O-O (Black wants to play ...Rdf8 and ...f6 so White makes this standard rook lift to stop it) 18.Rh3 Rdf8 19.Rf3
19...b6 (Black has to be patient as White has an advantage in case of 19...f6 20.exf6
Rxf6 21.Qd6) 20.Qg3. The position is strategically balanced, and both sides find it not easy to improve. Black, playing an opponent who is more than 300 Elo points higher rated, is happy with the status quo. Note that Black could have immediately opted for the game plan of bringing his knight to d8, or for ...a5 followed by ...Na7, ...Nb5, which is another standard plan here. 20...Qb5 21.Bc1 Qa4 22.Kf1 Kb7. A logical run of play in such positions. The queenside is blocked while White, despite his space advantage, has problems improving on the kingside. 23.Rf4 (the 'active' 23.g5 is not well timed as Black has excellent play after 23...hxg5 24.Bxg5 Be8 or 24...f6) 23...Be8 24.Qf3 Nd8! (this move enables some tactical ideas based on the fact that Black is the one who will be opening the kingside!) 25.Rb1. A) Here Black had an interesting aggressive option, the motifs behind which are useful to remember, in 25...g5!? 26.hxg6 f5! (the kingside opens up and Black is ready for it!) 27.gxf5 (after 27.exf6 Black, with his well-coordinated pieces, gets excellent compensation with 27...Nxg6) 27...exf5. analysis diagram Now we see all the advantages of placing the black knight on d8! 28.Qh3 Ne6
29.Rf3 Bxg6 30.Nxg6 (in case of 30.Bxh6? Rf7 31.Nxg6 Rxg6 32.Bc1 f4! we have
another excellent example of White playing all the logical moves and everything going wrong! Black has a close to winning advantage here as the two knights are clearly superior to the two bishops) 30...Rxg6 31.Rg3 with complicated play in a dynamically balanced position; B) Black continues with his wait-and-see strategy: 25...a5. And now, out of frustration due to a combination of factors: not being able to make progress while playing a considerably lower-rated opponent with White, does its job and Ivan Saric takes an irresponsible decision: 26.Ng6? (a strategic mistake which should have been losing. White helps Black to obtain excellent play on the kingside) 26...Nxg6
27.hxg6 Qd7. Most probably by now, Saric understood what he had done and now
he hopes to maintain the position by playing neutral moves: 28.Be2 (28.gxf7 Nxf7 plays further into Black's hands) 28...Ka7 29.Ra1
29...Qe7!. No more jokes! White is stuck between a rock and a hard place and has to
decide between taking on f7, opening the f-file to Black's advantage, and losing a pawn for nothing. Saric opted for the latter: 30.a4 fxg6 and Black was a pawn up for nothing though White ultimately saved a draw in Saric-Furlan, Slovenia tt 2022.
30.gxf7 leads to a bad position for White after 30...Nxf7 31.Qg3 Ba4! 32.Ra2 Ng5
(White cannot surrender the f-file here, while 33...Ne4 is a threat) 33.f3 Rb8 and White is in terrible shape as he will need to give material in order to save his Rf4.
11...Nc6 12.Qh5?!
This certainly does not help White.
12.h4 Bd7 13.h5 h6 14.Qf4 with 15.g4 to follow was needed, leading to play similar
to Saric-Furlan from the previous comment. Again... do not draw any easy conclusions based on your computer engine giving White a slight advantage here!
12...h6
Now it's high time for White to be careful!
13.O-O?
13.g4 was needed, keeping the king on e1.
13...Bd7 14.Rfb1 O-O-O!
By now Zoltan most probably understood the trouble he'd got himself into! Black is clearly better. White queen is terribly placed. One of Black's ideas is ...Rdg8, ...g7- g5-g4, ...Be8 and ...f6. Almasi now continues according to the logic, 'if I have to suffer, I'd better grab a pawn':
15.Qxf7 g5
16.Be1?
This loses decisive material. The only way for White to stay in the game was with the drastic measure 16.g4! and after 16...Rdf8 17.Qh5 Be8 18.Qh3 Nfe7 White is still in the game. However, with ideas like ...Ne7-g6-f4 with ...h5 to follow Black has plenty of compensation: White's position can collapse at any moment.
16...g4
Now it's over. White's queen cannot be saved.
17.Nd2 Rhg8 18.h3
Or 18.a4 Rdf8 19.Qh7 Nce7 with ...Rh8 or ...Rg7 to follow.
18...Rdf8 19.Qxf8+ Rxf8 20.hxg4 Nfe7 21.Nf1
Black is up a queen for a rook and has the better position too.
21...Be8! 22.Bd2 Bg6 23.Bxh6 Rh8 24.Bd2 Bxc2 25.Rb2 Bg6 26.Ng3 Qc7 27.Bd1
Many moves win here. Beliavsky finishes with a simple tactic:
27...Nxd4! 0-1
Black plays ...c4, fixing the pawn chain Game 31 Ivan Saric 2668 Mateusz Bartel 2630 Germany Bundesliga 2022/23 The pawn structure in this game can be compared to that in Almasi-Beliavsky and Karjakin-Grischuk. This game is another example of how easy it is, even for a very strong player, to go wrong in these strategically complex positions.
1.e4 e6 2.d4 d5 3.Nc3 Bb4 4.e5 c5 5.a3 Bxc3+ 6.bxc3 Nc6!?
A relative sideline. We will, however, not focus on the opening here but will try to understand the strategic compexities of the ensuing positions.
7.Qg4
Arguably White's main line here - in any case, the most direct line. White is asking a direct question to Black, who now has to either weaken his kingside or lose his right to castle. At a high level, White players have tried different moves here: 7.Nf3, or 7.a4, or my personal favourite 7.h4.
7...g6
The future of Black's king will probably be on the queenside. At some stage (soon), Black will close the centre with the standard ...c4 push. We have many top-level games here. I have chosen a few examples that I thought to be instructive, outlining possible plans and objectives.
8.h4
One high-level game saw an interesting struggle here: 8.Bd2 Bd7 9.h4 (for the time being, Black has to keep the kingside closed) 9...h6 10.Bd3 Qe7!? (Black places his queen on e7, hoping to be better prepared to create counterplay by pushing ...f6 or ...f5) 11.Nh3 O-O-O 12.O-O. Black needs to be careful: if he doesn't close the centre on time, White might take dxc5: 12...c4! 13.Be2 Be8 (Black is getting ready to open up the kingside and create counterplay) 14.Nf4 Kb8 15.a4 Ka8 (getting all set to push his f-pawn) 16.Bc1!? Anand is asking Ponomariov a tough question! If Pono pushes his f-pawn, it will be a difficult-to- calculate pawn sacrifice, while if not, Vishy will play the unpleasant
17. Ba3.
Most probably White's best option was the prophylactic 16.Qh3! because 16...f5?? now blunders a pawn for nothing since after 17.exf6 Nxf6 White's queen is not attacked and he can simply take 18.Nxe6, with a winning advantage. Ponomariov makes the right call: 16...f5! (for the sacrificed pawn, Black gets a kingside attack)
17.exf6 Nxf6 18.Qxe6 (the zwischenzug 18.Ba3!? was worth considering) 18...Qg7
(Black will soon push ...g5 and start attack on the white king; he definitely has sufficient compensation here) 19.Qh3 Bd7 20.Qg3. An important moment. Ruslan underestimates White's strategic threat of trading the kinghts: 20...Bf5 21.Nh5! and with the knights off the board Black's situation is not nearly as good. Suddenly White is better, though Black managed to save a draw in Anand-Ponomariov, Leon 2007. More precise was 20...Rhg8!, preparing ...g5 while not allowing the knight trade:
21.Bf3 Qf7! and Pono's pawn sac idea would have worked to perfection - White
would have been in serious trouble here! A logical continuation might be 22.Nh3 g5
23.hxg5 Bxh3 24.Qxh3 hxg5 and White has only one move to avoid immediate
annihilation: 25.g4; however after 25...Qg6 he is in serious trouble. The power of his bishop pair is contained, while his king is weak and prone to immediate attack. Black now has to opt for the standard reaction to White's h-pawn push:
8...h6 9.a4

14.Bh3
This is a typical kind of position we can get in this line. In the game, White finds it difficult to make progress, while Black gets ready to push the f-pawn and create kingside activity. Again, I advise you: do NOT draw any 'easy conclusions' based on your computer engine giving White some plus here. The reality in human play is very different - we have a complex, unclear game here! In human play position may in some sense be easier to play with Black. Objectively, White probably has some advantage, but Black has a relatively clear plan: to push ...f6 or ...f5 and take action on the kingside, favouring him. At the same time, White needs to be clever and has to improve his position while anticipating Black's actions. Ivan Saric did not manage to find a clever way to regroup, and in the game Black's plan worked to perfection.

Closed centre, the plan with ...Nf5 and ...h5 Game 33 Leonid Stein Tigran Petrosian Moscow ch-URS Final & Zonal 1961
1.e4 e6 2.d4 d5 3.Nc3 Bb4 4.e5 c5 5.a3 Bxc3+ 6.bxc3 Ne7 7.Qg4
In the 1960s, the following Black set-up was popular at top level:
7...Nf5 8.Bd3 h5
Big names like Petrosian and Korchnoi had it in their regular repertoire. Black's idea is to build a stable kingside with his Nf5 supported by the pawn on h5 (aimed against White's g4 pawn push). As it will transpire, however, Black's problem is that pawns can't move back, and his kingside will be vulnerable in many cases due to his pawn already being on the h5-square. Leonid Stein slowly improves his position, underlining the deficiencies of Black's set-up. As we will see, Petrosian could have played better but was never close to equality and never had any meaningful counterplay. After this game, Black's set-up disappeared from top-level play.
9.Qf4!
Mikhail Tal, true to his style, applied a more direct approach (albeit a bit chaotic) with 9.Qh3. White wants to push g4, harassing the Nf5 as soon as possible: 9...cxd4
10.Nf3 (10.Bxf5 exf5 11.cxd4 does not bring White any advantage as Black is okay
after 11...h4!) A) 10...Nc6 11.g4!? (Tal loved this approach here; the alternative was 11.cxd4 Ncxd4 12.Nxd4 Nxd4 13.O-O and White has good compensation, among other things because Black's pawn is on h5, making the evacuation of his king more difficult)
11...Nfe7 12.gxh5 with a complicated game, won by White in Tal-Petrosian,
Moscow 1957; B) 10...Qc7 11.Rb1 (Tal insists on the same idea as in his game vs Petrosian (see the comment above); better was the typical sacrifice 11.O-O! dxc3 12.a4 Nc6 13.Ba3 with excellent compensation for White) 11...dxc3 and Tal went for his favourite idea: 12.g4 Ne7 13.gxh5. Now, best for Black was to disturb the Bd3 with 13...b6! (the game Tal-Korchnoi, Riga ch-URS 1958, went 13...Nbc6 and after quite a number of ups and downs was eventually won by Black). 14... Ba6 is an often-seen unpleasant positional threat, and White does not have a good way to stop it as after 14.Qg4 Black gains the upper hand with 14...Nd7 15.Bf4 Nc5.
9...Nc6
A) After 9...Qc7... A1) One game by young Magnus Carlsen went 10.Ne2. Black (Yannick Pelletier) understood that White's positional threat was 11. Bxf5 and correctly responded with
10...Ne7!. White now has little else but to to change the central pawn structure
(similar to Shirov-Polgar in this chapter) and go for 11.dxc5. Black was fine after
11...Nd7 12.O-O Qxe5 the game Carlsen-Pelletier, Biel 2005, later ended in a draw;
A2) White should go for a change in the pawn structure and gets a clear advantage with 10.Bxf5! exf5 11.Ne2 (or immediately 11.Qg3) as Black now has a problem stabilizing his kingside (here it would have been helpful if he had his pawn on h6!)
11...Be6 12.Qg3.
B) Ideas like 9...Ne7? do not make sense here as the situation is not comparable to Carlsen-Pelletier above - White's knight is not on e2. White has different ways to get a clear advantage; for example, 10.dxc5 now works much better than in Carlsen's case above; 10.Nh3 looks very promising as well.
10.Ne2
Stein plans to open the f-file. A good alternative is 10.Nf3, combining two ideas: either, in the right circumstances, taking on dxc5 or on Bxf5. Black has a hard life here: 10...c4 (or 10...Nce7 11.dxc5!)
11.Bxf5 exf5 12.Qg3 and White has different plans here. Black is doomed to passive
defence, hoping to salvage a draw, e.g. 12...Rg8 13.Bg5 Qa5 14.Kd2 Be6 15.a4 Kd7
16.Rhb1.

Well-known lines killed by technology Game 34 Viswanathan Anand 2753 Curt Hansen 2610 Middelfart 2003 Modern technologies (engines, the cloud...) are going to 'close' some of the sharp tactical lines in the Winawer. Here is an example of a variation played by Botvinnik (as Black) in two different World Championship matches (meaning that he and his team analysed it a lot!) against Tal in 1960 and 1961. In the next game, Anand- Nikolic, we will see some more examples of technological advances probably 'killing' well-known lines played by very strong players.
1.e4 e6 2.d4 d5 3.Nc3 Bb4 4.e5 c5 5.a3 Bxc3+ 6.bxc3 Qc7 7.Qg4 f5 8.Qg3 Ne7
9.Qxg7 Rg8 10.Qxh7 cxd4
This variation became well known after the Tal-Botvinnik matches (1960 and 1961). White won both games; however, Black's play could have been improved). Anand's dynamic play in this game is the product of 'modern support'. Computer engines (see also Anand-Nikolic) on behalf of White here, as the position has been opened and White's bishop pair is a great force.

11.Kd1 was Tal's choice in both matches.

13.cxd4 Nf7 (Black has a drawing threat with ...Rh8, ...Rg8) 14.Qh5! Qxc2 (material
is equal, however White's h-passer now becomes very annoying) 15.Qf3 Bd7 16.h5 Bb5 17.h6 and the position is sharp but better for White.

13...Qxe5?!

14.f4! Qg7 15.h4!
This move is almost always good for White in such positions. Rh3 is enabled, Bg5 is extra protected, and pushing the h-pawn further can throw Black off balance.
15...dxc3 16.Rd1

Minor sacrifices - pawn sacrifice for development Game 35 Viswanathan Anand 2765 Predrag Nikolic 2630 Groningen FIDE Wch KO 1997 This game is a good example of the influence AI will have on certain variations and concepts. Predrag Nikolic is a strong GM who has played risky lines in the French Winawer over the years against the very world top, and took some rather 'poisoned' pawns. Twenty-five years ago, the verdict was that White had compensation and Black is taking a considerable risk snatching such a pawn. Today's verdict will be: 'Black is playing a losing variation'. Any player with a good feeling for the initiative, armed with a strong 'second' (read Stockfish or any other top engine) has a good chance to simply 'close' most of these lines. In most cases, a pawn sacrifice for the initiative in the type of lines we will see in this game, is worth considerably more than just 'compensation'. Let's study a couple of examples.
1.e4 e6 2.d4 d5 3.Nc3 Bb4 4.e5 c5 5.a3 Bxc3+ 6.bxc3
Now Black attempts to avoid mainstream theory:
6...Qa5
Another attempt to avoid theory is 6...Ne7 7.Qg4 Kf8?! (the main moves are 7...O-O and the nowadays most often played 7...Qc7). Playing this move, depriving himself from his castling rights, Black banks on the closed nature of the position. The problem is that in case the position opens up, his weak king and poor development will play a major role while any extra pawn Black may collect in the process will have merely psychological importance: 8.h4 and now: A) In case of 8...Qa5 we have a relatively recent top game in which the White player was well prepared: 9.Bd2 Qa4
10.Rh3! - the standard way for White to develop an initiative in such positions
10...Nbc6 (the pawn snatch 10...Qxc2? is bad due to the simple 11.Bd3 Qa4 12.Rg3
g6 13.Nh3 and Black comes under a crushing attack) 11.h5 h6 12.Qf4 Ke8 (White is certainly happy if Black plays 12...cxd4 13.Rf3). White now changes the character of the position and forces a favourable ending with
13.dxc5! (in this type of position Black should watch out for such changes in the
pawn structure!) 13...Qxf4 14.Bxf4. White has tripled c-pawns, however one of them is a plus pawn. White is clearly better. Black hopes to create counterplay with 14...f6
15.Nf3 g5 16.hxg6 Nxg6 17.Rxh6 Rxh6 18.Bxh6 fxe5 19.Rb1 and White had an
advantage and went on to win in Nisipeanu-Suleymanli, Reykjavik Ech 2021. Perhaps even stronger was 17.Bh2 Ngxe5 18.Nxe5 Nxe5 19.Bxe5 fxe5 20.c4! and Black has problems: his pawn centre is shaky while his rooks are not coordinated:
20...Bd7 (or 20...Ke7 21.O-O-O) 21.cxd5 exd5 22.Rh5;
B) 8...Qc7. Now we have a standard pawn sac: 9.Rh3!. This is a strong move, almost a novelty, that probably closes the variation. White sacrifices a pawn under very good circumstances. An old pawn sacrifice that was once considered the 'main line' (in this sideline) is
9.Qd1 cxd4 10.cxd4 Qc3+ 11.Bd2 Qxd4 12.Nf3 Qe4+ 13.Be2 b6. White's sacrificial
idea is similar to Anand's in our main game (considerably less convincing here, however, as Black's pawn is still on g7!). White had compensation at this stage of the game but probably not more, and went on to win in Kasparov-Nikolic, Horgen 1994. B1) One of the very few games we have with 9.Rh3! went 9...b6 10.h5 h6 11.Rf3 cxd4 12.cxd4 Nf5 13.Qf4! (the obvious threat is 14.g4; the game we have went 13.c3 Ba6 14.Ne2. White is clearly better here as well, but later he messed it up and lost in Plazuelo Pascual-Moskalenko, Barcelona 2021) 13...Qxc2 14.g4 and now forcing an ending does not save Black: analysis diagram
14...Qe4+ 15.Qxe4 dxe4 16.Rc3 Ne7 17.a4 with Ba3 to follow, with a large
advantage for White as Black cannot coordinate his pieces; B2) If Black is to suffer, he might as well explore the pawn grab: 9...cxd4 10.cxd4 Qxc2 when a logical further continuation might be 11.h5 h6 12.Ne2 Nbc6 13.Nc3 (Black's only counterplay here is the attack on the d4-pawn) 13...Nf5 14.a4 Nfxd4 (14...Ncxd4 runs into 15.Ba3+ Kg8 16.Rc1 Qb3 17.Ne4 and White wins) 15.Ba3+ Kg8 16.Rg3 and now: analysis diagram B21) 16...Rh7 17.Rd1 Nf5 18.Rd2 Qb3 19.Nxd5 wins for White; B22) 16...Qh7 looks terribly ugly and loses in various ways: 17.Bd3 Nf5 18.Qf4 (18.Qe2 should win as well) 18...Bd7 19.Rb1! (provoking ...b6 proves advantageous for White here) 19...b6 20.Rf3. So as not to lose a piece to 21.g4, Black has to play
20...g5 but now he loses for a different reason: 21.hxg6 fxg6 22.Bxf5! gxf5 23.Rg3+
Kf7 24.Qh4 Rag8 25.Qf6+ Ke8 26.Nb5 and Black gets mated, e.g. 26...Bc8 27.Rc1 Kd7 28.Rgc3!.
7.Bd2 Qa4 8.Qg4
White's main move here. Black is now forced to either weaken his kingside by playing 8...g6 or give up castling by playing 8...Kf8:
8...g6
This move is less often played, because the weakened dark squares on the kingside prove difficult for Black to deal with. 8...Kf8 is arguably the better of two unpleasant choices.
9.Qd1
Anand takes a typical decision, judging that the weakening of Black's kingside is worth two tempi. The text also 'invites' Black to accept the pawn sacrifice.
9.h4 is a typical alternative, played in a number of top games.
In case of 9.Nf3, Black closes the centre with 9...c4, hoping to get something similar to the game Saric-Bartel (in case of 9...Nc6 10.Qf4 h6, like Nisipeanu in the comments above, White can orchestrate a favourable change in the central pawn structure and obtain an advantage with 11.dxc5 as in Karjakin-Zhang, Khanty- Mansiysk 2007). But now we have 'AI at work' more or less closing the line: 10.Kd1! (10.Be2 was played, for example, in Grandelius-Merry, Douglas 2015). This idea, which was novel at the moment of writing, has an interesting tactical motif behind it: A) 10...Nc6 11.h4 h6 12.h5 g5 analysis diagram and now comes tactical idea behind 10.Kd1!: 13.Bxg5! hxg5 14.Qxg5. The position looks complicated?... hmmm... not exactly if you were helped in your preparation by a silicon friend. White has a large advantage, e.g. 14...Qa5 15.Qg7 Qxc3 16.Ra2 or
14...Nge7 15.Qf6 Rf8 16.h6 Qa5 17.Kd2;
B) If Black suspects White's tactical idea and goes for 10...Qa5, White gets a clear advantage with 11.h4 h6 12.Nh2! Bd7 13.h5 g5 14.Qf3 (White has to watch out not to fall for the typical motif 14.f4? f5!) 14...Nc6 15.Ng4 and White has a 'free roll'; C) 10...Bd7 fails to the same tactical motif as 10...Nc6 in line A: 11.h4 h6 12.h5 g5
13.Bxg5! hxg5 14.Qxg5 with Qg7 to follow and White wins (note the importance of
10.Kd1 - the pawn on c2 is protected!)
9...cxd4?
Risky and wrong. White gets much more than just compensation for his sacrificed pawn... not even taking into account that Vishy Anand is one of the worst possible opponents on the planet to face in such a position! Closing the center with 9...c4? does not help Black here as White has 10.h4 h6
11.h5! g5 12.f4!. Had White's queen been placed on g4, then Black would have
12...f5 here, but as it is, Black is in terrible trouble.
Best was 9...b6, though White has an advantage after 10.h4.
10.Rb1
How to assess this pawn sacrifice? Well, at the time game was played, it would have been something like: 'Black has made a very risky choice, White has sufficient compensation at least, and most probably more'. The assessment of today: 'Black is more or less lost, White is close to plus two'.
10...d3
10...a6 11.cxd4 Qxd4 12.Nf3 does not change much.
11.Bxd3 Qxa3 12.Nf3
For his sacrificed pawn, White has a massive development advantage , and the dark squares on Black's kingside are weakened. It may sound a bit over the top, but in a higher sense White has a winning advantage here. Anand goes on to play a nice attacking game crowned by an elegant execution. Yes, there are moments when White could have played better - more accurate perhaps, but Black never really gets a chance to escape.
12...Qc5 13.h4 h6 14.O-O Nd7 15.Re1 a6
Time to open up the position.
16.c4! dxc4 17.Be4
Black wants to stop 18.Bb4.
17...c3 18.Be3 Qc4 19.Bd4 Nc5
Vishy decides for arguably the most logical human move:
20.Re3
My Stockfish has an interesting idea: 20.Bxc5 Qxc5 21.Re3 Ne7 22.h5! g5 and now the Ne7 proves to be badly placed after 23.Qd6! Qxd6 24.exd6 and White has a winning advantage, e.g. 24...Nc6 25.Rxc3.
20...Nxe4 21.Rxe4 Bd7 22.Re3 Ne7
22...Bc6 does not help as White gets a winning advantage with 23.Rxc3 Qd5 24.Rd3
Qd8 (or 24...Bb5 25.Rd2) 25.Bb6 Qe7 26.Rd6.
23.Rxc3 Qd5 24.Rc5! Qe4 25.Rc7 b5 26.Bc5
A logical, human decision, not letting anything go. Your computer engine will tell you that the alternatives 26.Rb4 and 26.Nh2 are even stronger.
26...Nd5?
This allows an elegant finish. 26...Qd5 was more stubborn, but it does not save the game: 27.Qe1 Rc8. analysis diagram A) Now the most human solution is quite efficient: 28.Rxc8+ Bxc8 29.Bxe7 Kxe7
30.Qb4+ Ke8 31.Rb3 and Black simply cannot coordinate his pieces - the Rh8 is
doomed; B) The silicon brain suggests as the most precise 28.Bxe7 Rxc7 (or 28...Kxe7
29.Rd1 Rxc7 30.Rxd5 exd5 31.Qb4+ with a winning advantage as Black cannot
coordinate his rooks) 29.Bf6 Rg8 30.Rd1 and Black's position collapses.
27.Rxd7!
An elegant 'petite combinaison à la Capablanca' nets a queen.
27...Kxd7 28.Rb4
The rest of the game does not require any comment.
28...Qxb4 29.Bxb4 Rhc8 30.Bd6 Rc4 31.Nd2 Rd4 32.c3 Rd3 33.c4 Rxd2 34.Qxd2
bxc4 35.Qxh6 1-0 Tripled pawns for White This position type was hotly debated in the final Candidates Match between Spassky and Korchnoi in 1977. White has ugly pawns, but one of them is 'extra'. This semi- open position may also favour White's bishop pair. The battle is strategically very complex. I would rather be White, but in the mentioned match Korchnoi was quite successful as Black. White changes the central pawn structure with dxc5 Game 36 Alexei Shirov 2740 Zsuzsa Polgar 2550 Pardubice 1994
1.e4 e6 2.d4 d5 3.Nc3 Bb4 4.e5 c5 5.a3 Bxc3+ 6.bxc3 Ne7 7.Nf3
One of White's main choices versus the Winawer. As mentioned throughout the chapters of this book, where I find it relevant to provide useful information about opening theory, I do. But this is a middlegame book, so this game serves to show a frequently seen change in the central pawn structure caused by White taking dxc5.
7...Bd7
Black has tried many moves here, and readers interested in theory should also study
7...Qc7, 7...Nbc6, 7...b6 and 7...Qa5 - all lines that are played by (many) top players.
So any reader/player interested in opening theory has a lot of work to do here.
8.dxc5
White voluntarily creates 'Irish pawns' for himself, so what does he get in return? First of all, one of those pawns is an extra pawn, and secondly the position gets an open character, which should benefit White's bishop pair. Black gets one of his pawns back (collecting either the e5- or the c5-pawn), but this opens diagonals for White's dark squared bishop. This particular line was hotly debated in quite a number of games in the Spassky-Korchnoi Candidates match in 1977 and I advise any reader who is willing to broaden their knowledge of this type of position to go through those games.
8...Qc7
Black wants to place her bishop on a4 and then develop her knight to c6. The immediate 8...Nbc6, intending to take White's e5-pawn, was played in a number of top (rapid) games: 9.Bd3 Ng6 10.Rb1 (10.O-O leads to equality; after 10...Ncxe5
11.Nxe5 Nxe5 12.Bf4 Qf6 we have an approximately equal opposite-coloured
bishops position: 13.Qd2 Nxd3 14.cxd3 d4 15.c4 O-O 16.Bd6 Rfe8 17.f4 Bc6 with a later draw in Shirov-Nikolic, Monte Carlo 1995) 10...Ncxe5 and now: A) 11.Nxe5 Nxe5 12.Rxb7 leads to equality after 12...Qc8, but when Black in a classical time control game gave a 'healthy pawn' to reach a position with opposite- coloured bishops with 12...O-O? 13.Bxh7+ Kxh7 14.Qh5+ Kg8 15.Qxe5 f6 16.Qg3, he probably hoped that he would hold it despite the two-pawn deficit, but he blunders a full piece: 16...e5?? (16...Rf7 was needed) 17.c6 1-0 Beliavsky-Uhlmann, Sarajevo 1982; B) Anand wanted to create dynamics, and a possibility to throw in the h4-h5 push at the moment of his choosing, with 11.h4: 11...Qc7 12.O-O. An interesting situation! To take on d3 and improve White's pawn structure, or to take on f3 and grab a pawn on h4? Black goes for the latter: 12...Nxf3+? (correct was
12...Nxd3 13.cxd3 f6 and White has just a slight advantage at best) 13.Qxf3 Nxh4
14.Qg3!.
Most probably, Black did not take this option seriously enough as White's pawn structure now looks horrible: 14...Qxg3 15.fxg3 Ng6 16.Rxb7. White has a terrible pawn structure, but his rook on the 7th is a tremendous force here. White has a clear advantage and went on to win in Anand-Nikolic, Monte Carlo 1995.
9.Bd3 Ba4 10.Rb1
A useful move: the rook is on an open file plus White has Rb4 ideas.
10.O-O and 10.Be3 were tried in the mentioned Candidates match Spassky-Korchnoi,
Belgrade 1977.
10...Nec6
Polgar wants to hit the e5-pawn while stopping Rb4 ideas.
10...Nd7...
allows the rook lift 11.Rb4!, and ideed after 11...Bc6 12.O-O Nxc5 (12...Nxe5? is a bad idea as Black's queen gets exposed and it loses too much time: 13.Nxe5 Qxe5
14.Re1 Qc7 (14...Qxc3 15.Bb2) 15.Bf4+-) 13.Rg4 and White had an initiative and
the advantage, though Black eventually emerged victorious in Spassky-Korchnoi, Belgrade 1977.
11.O-O Nd7 12.Be3 h6
White has the advantage here, but this time loss will prove important.
12...Ndxe5 is wrong due to the simple 13.Nxe5 Nxe5 14.Bf4 and Black does not
have a satisfactory way to solve the pin problem. Black's best was the immediate 12...O-O-O when White should go for the game idea
13.Rb4! Nxb4 14.axb4, but here Black's survival chances are better than in the
game.
13.Re1 O-O-O
Black did not have much choice as after 13...O-O? White removes the pawn defences in front of Black's king by means of a piece sacrifice: 14.Qd2 Ncxe5 15.Nxe5 Nxe5 analysis diagram
16.Bxh6! Nxd3 17.cxd3. White now threatens a double rook lift and has a winning
attack; for example, 17...f6 18.Re3. Shirov now executes a thematic exchange sacrifice to improve his pawn structure:
14.Rb4! Nxb4 15.axb4 a6 16.Nd4
White has a clear advantage here and easy play. He has sacrificed only a pawn, so his attack is almost 'free of charge', combined with central domination and a queenside pawn roll. Black is in bad shape here. Polgar does her best to regroup and put up a defence.
16...Nb8! 17.Qa1 Bc6
18.f4 g6 19.Bf2 Rhg8 20.Rb1 Kd7 21.Bh4 Rc8 22.Bf6 Ke8
23.b5
White is in a hurry to win. The prophylactic king move (similar to Kasparov-Short earlier in this chapter) was perhaps the best practical execution: 23.Kh1!. White's b4-b5 push will now come with more power (as Black doesn't have the ...Qxc5 check) and Black doesn't have a good defence, e.g. 23...Nd7 24.b5 axb5 25.Nxb5 Bxb5 26.Bxb5 Qxc5 (26...Kf8 loses to 27.Bxd7! Qxd7 28.Qa7 Rc7 and Black's king is boxed in and his Rg8 is hopelessly placed; 29.Rb6) 27.Qa4 Qc7 analysis diagram Black is under a deadly pin. White now easily wins by opening the position further with 28.c4! and Black's position collapses.
23...axb5 24.Nxb5
Since Black doesn't have any useful moves, the silicon brain prefers to improve White's position before capturing on b5 with 24.Qa7!.
24...Bxb5 25.Bxb5+
25...Nc6
The only way to stay in the game was 25...Kf8! with ...Rg7 to follow. Yes, White is better but he would still need to 'bring it home'. Now White's queen enters forcefully, the terrible position of Rg8 tells, and the game is over:
26.Qa7! Kd7 27.Bxc6+ Qxc6 28.Rb6! Qxc5+ 29.Kh1
White has two terrible threats: 30.Rxb7+ and 30.Rd6+, and sadly Black has to move her king back to e8, disconnecting the rooks.
29...Ke8 30.Qxb7 Rc7 31.Qa6
Black does not get the time to threaten perpetual check or something similar.
31...Rc8
32.Qa4+! Kf8 33.Rb7
The threat is 34.Rxf7.
33...Rg7 34.Qd7 1-0
Chapter 6 Italian, Petroff, Ruy Lopez For the huge area of 1...e5 systems, I have opted for examples that were likely to occur in the reader's practical play, coming from the often-played openings the Italian, the Petroff, and the Ruy Lopez. The 'Italian Dilemma' White's Bh4 pin is annoying for Black, and the most clear-cut way to solve it is a radical one - pushing ...g5. The problem, next to the weakening of the kingside, is that White may sacrifice a piece on g5. Most of the time, this is easy to calculate, but it may simply work for White. After facing (and not successfully solving) this dilemma often in my career with Black, I ended up playing ...h6 most of the time, to stop the annoying Bg5 pin. A typical Italian sacrifice We have an instructive recent top-level game (and one in the comments) where the piece sacrifice on g5 works excellently for White. Game 37 Anish Giri 2768 Ding Liren 2788 Bucharest 2023
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bc4 Nf6
Probably Anish was inspired by the following game played by Magnus Carlsen six years prior: 3...Bc5 4.c3 Nf6 5.d3 O-O 6.Bg5. This standard pin is a bit unpleasant for Black. Like in our main game, the pin cannot really be ignored. Retreating bishop to e7 looks a bit passive, while the most radical way (chasing White's bishop away by pushing ...g7-g5) gives White tactical possibilities like in our main game.
6...d6 (due to his pawn still being on d7, Black can opt for 6...h6 7.Bh4 Be7 with
play similar to the recent game Yu Yangyi-Dardha - see the comment to 6...d6 below)
7.Nbd2! (a well-thought-out move-order. By delaying castling, as Anish will also do
in our main games, White keeps sacrificial possibilities on g5, while by defending his e4-pawn he also - sort of - rules out Black's ...Ne7 plan solving the pin on the h4-d8 diagonal. In case of 7.O-O, Black has a well-known plan to solve the pin by playing 7...Ne7!. Black wants to play ...Ng6 with ...h6 to follow, and is not worried about the doubling of his f-pawns: 8.d4 Bb6 and now, compared to 7.Nbd2!, White's e4-pawn is hanging. After 9.Nbd2 Black plays 9...Ng6 with ...h6 to follow, while after 9.Bxf6 gxf6, Black's bishop pair, the open g-file (for Rg8) and the potential ...f6-f5 pawn push compensate for the doubling of the f-pawns) 7...h6 (now, the unpinning 7...Ne7 does not work as White's e4-pawn is defended and White gets a stable advantage after the simple 8.d4. Black is forced to make a concession, giving up the centre with 8...exd4 - with the e4-pawn defended, 8...Bb6? now simply drops a pawn after 9.dxe5 dxe5 10.Nxe5 Qd6 11.Nd3 - 9.cxd4 Bb6 10.Bxf6 gxf6 11.O-O and White has a pleasant advantage) 8.Bh4. Now, Karjakin opts for the most radical way to solve the annoying pin: 8...g5
9.Nxg5! If I remember correctly, when watching this game live, Magnus was still
blitzing out his moves here. He goes for a well-known tactical theme (though it was at the time a novelty in this particular position), and was most probably still in his opening preparation. Karjakin now took his time (interestingly enough, he had underestimated this known sacrificial idea just like Ding) 9...hxg5 10.Bxg5. As the usual result of this thematic sacrifice, White has already grabbed two pawns for the knight, while Black is still far from solving the annoying pin) 10...Kg7 (10...Be6
11.Qf3 Kg7 transposes to the text) 11.Qf3 Be6 (nowadays, theoretical preparation
has advanced to lower-section Olympiad teams and at the Chennai 2022 Olympiad we saw 11...Rh8 12.Nf1 (12.O-O-O is a better execution) 12...Na5 (12...Be6 transposes to 11...Be6 12.Nf1 Rh8) 13.Ne3 Nxc4? (13...c6! was necessary) 14.Nd5 Nxd5 15.Bxd8 Rxd8 16.exd5 and later White won in Mwadzura-Montes Orozco, Chennai 2022). White has a big advantage here, however the execution is far from easy, even for a World Champion. White can successfully capitalize on his pin and gain a full point, but precision is needed. Flashy Stockfish or other top engine assessments are not easy to prove in a human game. Magnus went on to get an advantage, but did not derive the most from it and Karjakin ultimately escaped with a draw: A) 12.b4 Bb6
13.Bd5!. An unpleasant move! The 13...Bxd5 14. exd5 transaction is not really an
option here, as White will get a great outpost on e4 for his Nd2, while the standard ...Nb8-bd7 plan will cost a pawn on b7. Karjakin goes for the best defense: 13...a5!
14.b5 Nb8 15.Bxb7 (White got a third pawn for his knight while the pin on the h4-d8
diagonal still persists) 15...Ra7 (Black's most precise defence was 15...Nbd7! and after 16.Bc6! (White should not be greedy as after 16.Bxa8 Qxa8 the h4-d8 pin has been solved while Black's pieces start to coordinate nicely) 16...Rh8 17.h4 Qf8
18.Nc4 Rc8. White has three pawns for a piece and clear pressure, but Black has
decent survival chances). A1) 16.Bd5 was a logical move, but not the best: 16...Nbd7 17.Nc4 Bxd5 18.exd5 Qe8 (other options were 18...Rg8 or 18...Rh8). The pin has finally been solved! White has pressure, and Black was certainly not out of the woods yet, but Karjakin defended well and the game ended in a draw, Carlsen- Karjakin, Wijk aan Zee 2017; A2) Strongest for White was 16.Bc6!, insisting on eliminating the Nb8 and making it harder for Black to solve the pin. Black is in big trouble, e.g.: A21) 16...Bd7? does not work after 17.h4! Nxc6 (17...Rh8 18.Nc4+-) 18.bxc6 Bxc6 19.Rh3 with Rg3 to follow, with a winning advantage for White; A22) In case of 16...Nxc6 17.bxc6, the only way to get out of the pin here is by giving an exchange: 17...Rh8. Now White takes space: 18.d4! Rh6 (18...exd4?? blunders into 19.e5 dxe5 20.Ne4) 19.Bxh6+ Kxh6 20.d5 Bg4 21.Qg3 and White has a material and positional advantage and should win; A23) 16...Nbd7 17.Bxd7 Bxd7. Here it looks to me that the most logical human continuation is 18.h4 (my Stockfish wants me to play 18.Nc4 Bxb5 19.h4 and says White has a winning advantage) when Black tries to solve the pin with 18...Qe7: analysis diagram
19.h5! and the next couple of moves look forced: 19...Qe6 20.h6+ Kg6 21.h7! Kxg5
22.h8R Rxh8 23.Rxh8 Qg4 24.Ke2. Black's pieces have no coordination - the Ra7 is
hopelessly out of play. White should win this, e.g. 24...Bxb5 25.Rah1 Bd7 26.R8h6; B) The best execution was 12.Nf1!, provided that White is precise. analysis diagram Black cannot solve his problems here: B1) 12...Bxf2+? does not really help as after 13.Ke2! (13.Kxf2? is wrong due to the simple 13...Nxe4+ 14.dxe4 Qxg5) Black has won a pawn but has opened the f-file, so White now easily gets a decisive advantage, e.g. 13...Bb6 14.h4 Rh8 15.Nh2. The open f-file indeed comes in handy for White here, and the only way for Black to resist a bit longer is 15...Nb8 16.Raf1 Nbd7; however, Black loses material after the simple 17.Bxe6 fxe6 18.Ng4 Rf8 19.Bh6+ Kh7 20.Bxf8 Qxf8 21.Nxf6+; B2) Black's best defence is to improve the knight and rook: 12...Nb8 13.Ng3 Rh8. analysis diagram Here White has an important idea that will be repeated in the main Giri-Ding game:
14.Nf5+! Bxf5 (14...Kg6? is out of the question due to the simple 15.h4) 15.Qxf5
Nbd7 and the combination of the h4-d8 pin plus pressure on the f-file (the f7-square) proves decisive here. White opens files and gets his piece back with a winning advantage: 16.O-O-O Qe7 17.h4 Rag8 18.d4! exd4 (or 18...Bb6 19.f4+-) 19.cxd4. Black is forced to give his piece back: 19...Qxe4 (or 19...Bb6 20.Rhe1) 20.Bxf6+ Nxf6 21.Qg5+ Qg6 22.Qxg6+ Kxg6 23.dxc5 with a winning advantage for White.
4.d3 Bc5 5.Bg5 h6 6.Bh4
6...d6
With the pawn still on d7, one thematic way for Black to solve the pin problem is
6...Be7. At the moment of writing, we have one recent top-level game that went
7.Bg3 (strategically Black is happy with the trade after 7.O-O d6 8.a4 Nh5 9.Bxe7
Qxe7=) 7...d6 8.a4 O-O 9.Nbd2 (counterplay for Black? Push the f-pawn!) 9...Kh8! (given that Black has a change of heart in a few moves, he could have opted for the immediate 9...g6 10.a5 a6 11.O-O Nh5) 10.a5 a6 11.c3
11...g6!?. Not a mistake, however I don't like Black's unnecessary mixing of plans.
Black wants to play ...Nh5 but has weakened his kingside. 11...Nh7 is perhaps not precise because White probably has some advantage after 12.Qb3 f5 13.exf5 Rxf5
14.O-O, but consistent and strong was 11...Ng8!. Black has active counterplay and is
not worse, e.g. 12.Qb3 (or 12.d4 f5) 12...f5 13.exf5 Bf6! with ...Nge7 to follow. After 11...g6, White takes central action: 12.d4 exd4 13.cxd4. Black reacts correctly:
13...Nxe4 14.Nxe4 d5 15.Bxd5 Qxd5 16.Nc3 Qc4 17.d5 Re8 18.dxc6.
A tough decision: which check should Black give? 18...Bf6+ (18...Bb4+! was stronger when after 19.Ne5 f6 20.Qd2 Kg7 21.O-O-O fxe5 we have an unclear, dynamically balanced game) 19.Ne2 Bg4 20.O-O Qxe2. White has an advantage here, but does not play accurately: 21.cxb7 Qxd1. Now after 22.Rfxd1 Rab8 23.Ra4 Bxf3
24.gxf3 Rxb7 the endgame was drawish, but thanks to a few mistakes by Black
White managed to win in Yu Yangyi-Dardha, Sharjah 2023. The double attack on Black's f7- and b7-pawns would have yielded White an advantage: 21.Qb3! Qe6 (or 21...Bxf3 22.gxf3 bxc6 23.Qxf7) 22.Qxb7.
7.c3 a5
I assume that further theoretical discussions will be in the direction of 7...g5 8.Bg3 a6 9.Nbd2 Ba7 as in Giri-Keymer, Wijk aan Zee 2023.
8.Nbd2 Ba7
Probably inspired by Magnus's game, Anish keeps delaying his kingside castling:
9.a4!
We have had 9.O-O g5 in a couple of top-level games.
9...O-O
If Black wanted to push his g-pawn, it was a good idea to do it now. For example,
9...g5 10.Bg3 Qe7.
10.h3!
Similar to Karjakin, Ding Liren now goes for a radical solution to the h4-d8 pin problem:
10...g5
If Black plays 10...Qe7, we see another idea behind 10.h3: 11.g4! and Black is in trouble: 11...g5 12.Bg3 (12.Nxg5!? comes into consideration even here) 12...Kg7
13.Qe2 Be6 14.O-O-O! with h4 to follow. White has a strong attack - Black may
collapse in a few moves. Probably Black's best option was 10...Be6 and after 11.g4 (11.Nh2!? is another plan to consider) 11...Bxc4 12.dxc4 (12.Nxc4 leads to an equal ending after 12...d5!
13.exd5 Qxd5 14.Bxf6 e4! 15.dxe4 Qxe4+ 16.Qe2 Rfe8 17.Qxe4 Rxe4+ 18.Kf1
Rxc4 and a draw is the most likely result) 12...g5! 13.Bg3 Qd7. analysis diagram I prefer White here, but my Stockfish gives me zeroes after 14.Qe2 Ne7 15.O-O-O Ng6! (15...Qxa4? is not a good idea as White is fast with his attack after 16.h4)
16.h4 Kg7 17.hxg5 hxg5 18.Nxg5 Rh8 with apparent equality.
11.Nxg5!
Bingo! Giri gets a Carlsen-Karjakin kind of situation.
11...hxg5 12.Bxg5 Kg7 13.Qf3 Be6
Similar to Carlsen-Karjakin, White should emerge victorious here with precise play, but it's not easy! Like Carlsen, Giri does not execute in the most precise way and gives his opponent a chance to escape. Probably still in his home prep, Anish started with a precise move:
14.Nf1! Rh8
Another logical way to defend was 14...Nb8 when after 15.Ng3 Rh8 16.Nf5+! Bxf5
17.Qxf5 Nbd7 we reach a situation similar to what could have happened in Carlsen-
Karjakin: the h4-d8 pin combined with pressure on the f-file (the f7-square) should prove decisive. White opens files and gets a winning advantage with 18.h4. 18.Rf1 is also good, and 18.O-O-O should also work well for White.
15.Ne3
Black has different options to try to solve his problems but, provided White plays accurately, none of them seems to work.
15...Qe7
15...Nb8 16.Nf5+! transposes to lines given in the previous comment.
If Black removes White's annoying knight with 15...Bxe3 then White takes advantage of the open f-file: 16.fxe3 Nb8 (16...Bxc4 doesn't work due to 17.O-O)
17.O-O Nbd7 18.Qg3! and White wins: 18...Nh5 19.Bxe6 Nxg3 20.Rxf7+ Kg6
21.Bxd8.
A different order of capturing with 15...Bxc4 does not work either due to 16.Ng4!. Black prevents an immediate annihilation with 16...Bxf2+ 17.Nxf2 Be6, but the f- file and the pin now prove decisive: 18.O-O Kg6 19.h4 Nb8 20.d4 and White should win. Giri has the right idea but does not execute in the most precise way:
16.O-O-O
More accurate was to 'pro-actively' start the pressure on the f-file with 16.Rf1!. Black has nothing better than 16...Nb8 and now White goes for the already mentioned idea 17.Nf5+! Bxf5 18.Qxf5 Nbd7 19.O-O-O and he will push f4, opening the f-file. Black can't get out of the pin or create real counterplay, e.g. 19...c6 20.Kc2 (removing his king from the ...Be3 check; now White is ready to push f4) 20...b5
21.Ba2 bxa4 22.f4 and Black cannot defend against White's multiple threats.
The immediate 16.Nf5+ is not clear as after 16...Bxf5 17.Qxf5 Black has 17...Nd8! with ...Ne6 to follow.
16...Rag8
Ding Liren does not take his chance. If the f-file is now opened, White does not have an imminent threat, so this was the moment for Black to remove White's knight: A) 16...Bxe3+! 17.fxe3 Nb8. Black is on time now, and we probably have a dynamic balance: 18.Rhf1 Nbd7. analysis diagram The position is tense, and it is easy to go wrong, White needs to be smart to keep the balance here! A1) 19.Qg3 is wrong due to 19...Nh5!; A2) 19.Rf2 does not work in view of 19...Bxc4 20.dxc4 Qe6! 21.Rdf1 Nh7 and Black wins, e.g. 22.Qg3 (or 22.Qxf7+ Qxf7 23.Rxf7+ Kg6) 22...Nxg5 23.Qxg5+ Kf8 24.Rxf7+ Qxf7 25.Rxf7+ Kxf7 and Black has too many pieces for the queen; A3) After 19.Bd5! the engine gives zeroes, while in case of 19...c6 (also possible is
19...Rh7 or 19...Kg6) we see the reason why White inserted 19.Bd5! as now 20.Qg3!
leads to a balanced position: 20...Nh5 (alternative, Black can go for an interesting, materially unbalanced position with 20...Kf8!? 21.Rxf6 Nxf6 22.Qf3 Nxd5!
23.Bxe7+ Nxe7 with unclear play) 21.Bxe7+ Nxg3 22.Bxe6 Nxf1 23.Bxd7 Nxe3
24.Re1 Nxg2 (24...f6? loses to 25.Rxe3 Kf7 26.Bxd6 Rad8 27.Rf3 Rxd7 28.Bxe5)
25.Rg1 f6!. White cannot collect black knight and keep both of his bishops: 26.Bf5
Kf7 27.Rxg2 Kxe7 28.Rg7+ Kf8 29.Rxb7 Rg8 and it is easier for White here, but a draw is the most likely result. B) In case of 16...Nb8 analysis diagram White goes for a well-known solution: 17.Nf5+! Bxf5 18.Qxf5 Nbd7 19.Rhe1! (ruling out Black's ...Be3+ and getting ready for the files to open) 19...Rh5 20.f4 exf4 21.d4 and White wins soon. Anish understands that he has given Ding a chance, and now he continues precisely!
17.Rhf1!
Now White has a winning advantage, and Giri executes with a steady hand.
17...Nb8
White goes for a direct execution, immediately opening the centre.
18.d4
The idea we already established as good for White, 18.Nf5+ Bxf5 19.Qxf5 should work here as well as after 19...Nbd7 20.Kb1 with f4 to follow, Black cannot keep his stuff together.
18...exd4 19.cxd4 Nbd7
20.e5
Direct and strong. White gets his piece back while the attack continues.
20.h4 was probably working too.
20...dxe5 21.d5 Qb4 22.Qe2 Bxh3
Another way to give back the piece was 22...Bxe3+ 23.fxe3 Bg4 (23...Nxd5 loses to
24.Bxd5 Qc5+ 25.Qc2) 24.Bxf6+ Nxf6 25.hxg4 Rh4.
analysis diagram Black's king remains weak: 26.Rf5! Rxg4 27.Bd3 Nxd5 28.e4 Nf6 29.Rdf1 Rg6
30.g4 with g5 to follow, and Black will soon get mated.
23.gxh3 Kf8 24.h4
Ding Liren hopes to create practical survival chances:
24...Bd4 25.Rxd4!
25.Bb5 also looks good.
25...exd4 26.Nf5 Rxg5
26...Ne5 loses to 27.Bb5.
27.hxg5 Ne5
