28.Bb5 Nfg4 29.Kb1 Kg8 30.Nh6+! Nxh6 31.Qxe5 Ng4 32.Qxc7
Now White is up material while his attack still continues.
32...Kg7
33.g6! Rf8
Or 33...Kxg6 34.Rg1 f5 35.f3.
34.gxf7 d3 35.Rg1 Rxf7 36.Qc3+
And facing a piece-down endgame, Black resigned. Rook lift ideas in the Petroff The Petroff Defence (or in some opening manuals 'the Russian') has a reputation of a quiet, solid choice where Black aims to equalize the game. Well, in many lines of the Petroff, this reputation is entirely mistaken, as we get sharp dynamic play. One of the ways for White to seize the initiative is by using a rook lift. Sometimes the rook is lifted via the fifth rank (Rb1-Rb5) and sometimes also via the second rank (Ra2-Re2). Usually we get sharp play, and the games I have selected show the dynamics. Game 38 Nigel Short 2650 Valery Salov 2630 Amsterdam 1989 The rook lift ideas we are about to see in this game were novel at the time and were difficult to evaluate in the pre-computer era. The positions we will reach are very dynamic, with razor-sharp tactics - perhaps not what you would expect in a 'quiet Petroff'.
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nf6 3.Nxe5 d6 4.Nf3 Nxe4 5.d4 d5 6.Bd3 Bd6 7.O-O O-O 8.c4 c6
9.Nc3 Nxc3 10.bxc3 Bg4
Nowadays, theoretical discussions go in the direction of 10...dxc4 11.Bxc4 Bf5.
11.cxd5
If White places his rook on b1 with 11.h3 Bh5 12.Rb1, we can get different types of positions: 12...dxc4 13.Bxc4 b5 14.Bd3 Nd7 with comfortable play for Black, as for example in Ljubojevic-Khalifman, Reykjavik 1991.
11...cxd5 12.Rb1 b6
Black's best move is considered to be 12...Nd7. We can get dynamic positions where White sacrifices a pawn: 13.h3 Bh5 14.Rb5 Nb6 (modern theory considers that
14...Nf6 equalizes) 15.c4! Bxf3 16.Qxf3 dxc4 17.Bc2. We have quite a number of
top-level games with this position. White definitely has compensation for the sacrificed pawn but perhaps not more. White now goes for a nice rook lift that is worth remembering:
13.Rb5!
Nowadays, armed with strong engines, we all understand that White stands better here. In the ensuing positions we have sharp tactical play, offering plenty of ideas to a gifted player and very difficult to assess clearly without silicon assistance. In the late 1980s and early 1990, this was a hotly debated line on top level.
13...Bc7
Black can opt for a positional pawn sacrifice here, provided they understand the dangers: 13...Nd7 14.Rxd5 Nf6 15.Rb5 Qc7 16.h3. analysis diagram And now Black can play for a light- square blockade with 16...Be6 17.Qc2 Bc4
18.Rf5 Nd5.
14.c4
Direct, but best for White was to ask Black a difficult question about his bishop!
14.h3! Black does not have a good retreat, so he has to harass White's rook: 14...a6
(14...Bh5? loses to the simple 15.c4 Qd6 16.g4 Bg6 17.Rxd5) when White's best option is to go for an exchange sac with 15.hxg4! (my Stockfish is also enthusiastic about the slightly different exchange sacrifice 15.Rxd5! Qxd5 16.hxg4 Nd7 17.Qc2 with more than just compensation for White) 15...axb5 16.Qc2 g6. It is obvious that White has great compensation here, but in pre-computer days, judging the size of it was far from easy. It took top players quite some time to understand that White has a clear advantage here. Precision is needed! Black's biggest problem is that he is unable to coordinate his pieces. In the following game, Ivanchuk masterfully increased the pressure and finished with a direct mating attack: 17.Bxb5! (in a top level game two years prior, White opted for a more direct but less precise continuation: 17.Bh6 Re8 18.Bxb5 Re4 19.g5; the Bh6 stands great if aided by more forces to mate Black's king, but the way the game went, the bishop remained out of play and Black saved a draw in Short-Gelfand, Brussels Candidates 1991) 17...Qd6 (on 17...Nd7, White should, like Ivanchuk, continue limiting Black's pieces with 18.g5! as getting the exchange back with
18.Bh6 allows the good defensive resource 18...Nf6! 19.Bxf8 Kxf83) 18.g3! and
now: A) If 18...Nc6, White can continue similar to Ivanchuk in the game: 19.Kg2 f5
20.Rh1! and Black is in trouble (20.Bf4 Qd7 21.g5 is less good; White gets a
dominant knight but trades off too many pieces: 21...Bxf4 22.gxf4 Qc7 23.Bxc6 Qxc6 24.Ne5 Qd6 25.a4 Ra5 26.Rb1 and the game Sokolov-Nikcevic, Vrnjacka Banja 1998, later ended in a draw) 20...Rae8 21.Ng5 Re7 22.Rh6 and Black cannot keep his kingside intact, e.g. 22...Nd8 23.Bd3! Nf7 24.Nxf7 Rexf7 25.gxf5 Qd7
26.g4;
B) 18...Ra7 19.Re1 Nd7 20.g5! (limiting the Nd7 and preparing the Nf3-h2-g4 manoeuvre) 20...Rd8. Sensing that Black does not have any counterplay, White takes his time: 21.a4! Nb8. analysis diagram Now the time has come for a mating attack! 22.Nh2! Qf8 23.Ng4 Bd6 24.Kg2 (Black's situation is hopeless, White's attack wins by itself) 24...Kh8 25.Rh1 Qg7
26.Nf6 h5 27.Qd1 and not willing to wait for 28.Rxh5, Black resigned in Ivanchuk-
Bareev, Linares 1993.
14...Qd6?!
This lands Black in trouble. A few months later, Jan Timman vs Nigel Short came up with the best continuation for Black here: 14...dxc4!. The position is very tactical, and dynamically balanced. In the pre-computer era, it could easily take days of analyses to investigate all the possibilities in such positions. A) 15.Bxc4 should not worry Black: 15...Qd6 16.g3 Nc6; B) Jan must have unvestigated the tactical 15.Bxh7+!? Kxh7 16.Rg5. analysis diagram B1) 16...Bxf3? is a blunder that loses by force: 17.Qxf3 Nd7 18.Qh3+ (18.Qh5+ also wins: 18...Kg8 19.Rxg7+! Kxg7 20.Bh6+ Kf6 21.Bg5+; Black gets enough material for the queen but his pieces coordinate badly and king is overexposed)
18...Kg8 19.Rh5 f6 20.Re1!, cutting off the escape route; Black's king will soon be
mated; B2) Modern-day silicon brain analyses point out an interesting tactical line ending in equality: 16...Bc8! 17.d5 and now Black needs to make a prophylactic decision:
17...Kg8! (moving the king to a safer place) 18.Qd4 f6 19.Rh5. White has a strong
attack, but he is also a piece down. Best play probably leads to a draw: 19...Nc6!
20.Qxc4 b5! 21.Qxc6 (21.Qh4 Ne7 doesn't look like a winning attempt) 21...Qe8
22.Qxa8 Qxh5 23.Qxa7 Bd6 24.Qd4 Bb7 25.Rd1 Rd8 and Black has enough
compensation to hold; C) Short went for arguably the most logical continuation: 15.Be4. Here Timman missed a beautiful positional sacrifice: 15...Nd7! (15...Nc6 was played in Short-Timman, Hilversum m 1989) and if 16.Re1?! (Black has full compensation after 16.Bxa8 Qxa8) analysis diagram Black insists on sacrificing an exchange: 16...Nf6! 17.Bxa8 Qxa8 18.h3 Be6 and Black has a great game here, White faces a struggle for a draw.
15.Re1!
White is not afraid of ghosts and lets Black execute his threat.
15...Bxf3 16.Qxf3 Qxh2+ 17.Kf1
White is better developed as the black queen on h2 can easily be a liability here. However, the position is still complicated (a lot of tactics) and mistakes start to happen:
17...Nc6?!
17...Nd7 18.Rxd5 Nf6 19.Rf53 gave Black better surviving chances.
18.Rxd5 Rae8
18...g6? loses to 19.Rd7.
19.Be3?
Throwing away a relatively easy win. The way to execute was 19.Rxe8! Qh1+ (19...Rxe8? loses to 20.Rh5 Qd6 21.Ba3 (or 21.Bxh7+ Kf8 22.Ba3) 21...Nb4
22.Bxh7+ Kf8 23.Bg6! and Black loses decisive material or gets mated, or both)
20.Ke2 Rxe8+ 21.Be3 (the bad position of Black's Q, B, N plus K decides) 21...Qa1
22.Qh3 g6 23.Qd7.
19...g6
Now Black is back in the game!
19...f5 was also possible.
White decides to stop 20...Nb4, but this costs time.
20.a3
20...Qh1+?!
This check actually improves White's king. Black had to exploit White's weak king with 20...f5!. Now the Qh2 is suddenly well placed, and White's best option is to simplify into a drawish endgame: 21.Qh3 (21.g3 f4 22.gxf4 Bxf4 does not look like a winning attempt) 21...Qxh3 22.gxh3 f4
23.Bd2 Rxe1+ 24.Kxe1 Rd8 25.Rxd8+ Bxd8 and a draw is the most likely outcome
here.
21.Ke2 Qh4
Now White pursues a good idea to utilize the h-file, but he does not do it in the most precise way:
22.g4
Correct was 22.Kd1! (removing Black's 22...Nxd4 threat). Now White's king is relatively safe while he has different threats - Rd7 or g4 followed by Rh1: 22...Qe7. analysis diagram Naturally, White is not going to allow 23... Qxa3: 23.c5! bxc5 and now is the time to utilize the h-file: 24.Rh1! cxd4 25.Qh3 f5. Black escapes mate, however lands in a bad position: 26.Bc4 Kh8 27.Bg5 Qg7 28.Bh6 Qe7 29.Bxf8 Rxf8 30.Re1 Qg7
31.Qd3 and White has a large advantage and should win.
22...f5!
Black now has enough counterplay to hold!
23.Rd7 f4! 24.Rh1
24...Qf6?
A crucial blunder. This sharp game could have ended peacefully had Black gone for 24...Nxd4+!
25.Rxd4 Qg5! (25...Qf6? does not stop Qd5+ and loses to 26.Rd7) and we get a
drawish endgame after 26.Qd5+ (26.Qh3 is not a winning attempt as White may only get himself in trouble after the simple 26...Re7) 26...Qxd5 27.cxd5 Bd6 or
27...fxe3=.
25.Qd5+
Black now loses decisive material.
25...Re6
Or 25...Rf7 26.Rxh7.
26.g5
Too many black pieces are hanging.
26...Nxd4+ 27.Kd1 1-0
Game 39 Judit Polgar 2722 Anatoly Karpov 2693 Hoogeveen 2003 I like this game because, similar to Short-Salov, it contains a rook lift on the fifth rank by White. This time, however, the fifth rank is empty at the moment of the rook lift. White gets a choice between doubling on the e-file, keeping the pressure and building up a kingside attack, or a typical promising exchange sacrifice on e6. Judit decides to keep up the pressure on the e-file, developing a kingside attack and ending the game with a beautiful 'Lasker double bishop sacrifice'!
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nf6 3.Nxe5 d6 4.Nf3 Nxe4 5.d4 d5 6.Bd3 Be7 7.O-O Nc6 8.c4 Nb4
9.Be2 O-O
10.a3
This move is no longer considered dangerous for Black due to the improvement on move 13. The current main line here (also involving a rook lift!) is 10.Nc3 Bf5 11.a3 Nxc3 12.bxc3 Nc6 13.Re1 Re8 14.Ra2! Nakamura-Nepomniachtchi, Madrid Candidates 2022. This rook lift idea, which is seen in a number of top-level games in these Petroff positions, is aimed at increasing the pressure on the e-file - see further on in this chapter.
10...Nc6 11.cxd5 Qxd5 12.Nc3 Nxc3 13.bxc3
13...Qd6
This devastating Karpov loss had such an impact that the text was not repeated in any subsequent top game! Theory considers 13...Bf5, preventing Rb1, to be Black's best option here, Kasparov-Adams, Linares 2002. Please check the comments in the next game Nakamura-Nepomniachtchi. Also possible is 13...Na5 14.Rb1 a6 (14...c6 should also be good for equality here)
15.Ne5 Bf5 16.Bf3 Qe6 (if Black wants less complications he can opt for 16...Be4)
17.Rb2 Bxa3 18.Re2 Bxc1 19.Nc6 Qf6 20.Nxa5 Bf4 21.Nxb7 Rab8 with a dynamic
balance, leading to a draw in Svidler-Adams, San Luis FIDE Wch 2005. White has a good central pawn structure here - while she improves the development of her pieces, her mobile pawn centre (due to White's pawn still being on c3!) does not give Black any target for counterplay against the d4-pawn.
14.Rb1
Polgar prepares her rook lift.
14.Bd3 was a good alternative.
14...b6 15.Re1 Be6
15...Bf6 was definitely a better choice, though White still has the intiative after
16.Bd3 Bg4 17.h3 Bh5 18.g4 Bg6. Here, White improves his position with a
standard rook lift: 19.Rb2! Rae8 20.Rbe2.
16.Bd3
White has a number of good options here; Polgar goes for the fifth rank rook lift.
16...Rae8
17.Rb5
My Stockfish advises the 'bold and direct' 17.Rxe6!. This typical exchange sacrifice, aimed to destroy Black's pawn structure, works well here: 17...fxe6 (17...Qxe6?? blunders into 18.d5) 18.Qc2! and here the best practical decision for Black is to sacrifice back an exchange: 18...Rxf3! (if 18...h6, 19.Qe2 and White builds up a powerful Q+B battery) 19.Bxh7+ Kf8 20.gxf3 Bf6.
17.Rb2 followed by Rbe2 is a more standard idea - see Nakamura-Nepomniachtchi
and similar examples in the comments to that game. White has the initiative after
17...Bd5 18.Rbe2! Bxf3 19.gxf3 Rd8 20.Bb1.
17...Na5?
In a higher sense, a losing mistake. Black had to go for 17...Bf6!. The logical decision for White here is to gain the bishop pair: 18.Ng5 (should White naively think Black had blundered into 18.d5?, he would only be in trouble after 18...Bg4!) 18...Bxg5 19.Rxg5. White has somewhat better chances, but there is a big fight ahead.
18.Rbe5!
White's pieces are ready to strike at the black king!
18...Nc6
Polgar has her dream position now. She can place her rook on e2, keeping pressure on the e-file, and has a great version of the Nakamura-Nepomniachtchi game, or she can opt for a very promising exchange sacrifice. Judit chooses the former. Happy with her position, she decides to keep all her options open:
19.R5e2
Here, the typical exchange sac 19.Rxe6! worked even better than two moves ago:
19...fxe6 20.Qc2 h6 (20...Rxf3 now leads to a lost position for Black after 21.Bxh7+
Kf8 22.gxf3 Bf6 23.Bg6 Rd8 24.Bh5) 21.Qe2 and the powerful Q+B battery should decide here.
19...Bd7
If Black plays 19...Bg4, White has a direct tactical refutation based on the e-file pin:
20.d5! Bxf3 (or 20...Na5 21.Re4 Bf5 22.R4e3 Bxd3 23.Qxd3 and tripling on the e-
file nets White a piece) 21.gxf3 Na5 22.Re5 with Qe2 to follow, and the e-file pin nets White a piece. Polgar feels that the time has come for the execution:
20.d5! Na5 21.Ne5 Bf6 22.Bf4 Bxe5 23.Bxe5
Very probably, Judit already had Lasker's bishop sacrifice in mind here. 23.Rxe5 also led to a crushing attack: 23...Qxa3 (or 23...Rxe5 24.Bxe5 Qxa3 25.Qh5) 24.Qh5 g6 25.Qh4.
23...Qxa3 24.Re3 Qc5?
Allowing a beautiful finish. Black had to stop the 'Lasker motif' with 24...g6; however, after 25.h4 White's attack should crush through (weak black king, knight out of play).
25.Bxh7+ Kxh7 26.Qh5+ Kg8 27.Bxg7 1-0
Game 40 Hikaru Nakamura 2760 Ian Nepomniachtchi 2766 Madrid Candidates 2022 We have quite a number of top-level games where the typical Petroff rook lift is predominantly aimed at controlling the e-file. White plays Ra2, most of the time to transfer this rook to e2, when the play often becomes tactical. The e-file pressure White gets is just one of the ingredients in the position, and White needs good tactical skills and a feel for the initiative to prevail. I will now show a number of top- level examples of such play. My aim is to show middlegame ideas; however, as usual I will try to provide some relevant opening information as well.
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nf6 3.Nxe5 d6 4.Nf3 Nxe4 5.d4 d5 6.Bd3 Nc6 7.O-O Be7 8.c4 Nb4
9.Be2 O-O 10.Nc3
There is a game by Kasparov that is instructive for understanding this type of position: 10.a3 Nc6 11.cxd5 Qxd5 12.Nc3 Nxc3 13.bxc3 and now: A) For 13...Qd6 see the previous game Polgar-Karpov; B) 13...Na5 does not have the best reputation, and for good reason: 14.Ne5 Bf5
15.c4 Qd6
16.c5! and Black's forces suddenly lack coordination: 16...Qd5 17.Bf3 Be4 18.Bxe4
Qxe4 19.Bd2! Nc6 20.Re1. Black cannot prevent material loss: 20...Qh4. B1) Now Leko went for the human continuation, netting a clear positional advantage: 21.Nf3 Qh5 22.Rb1. Black has problems defending his queenside pawns. White had a clear advantage and went on to win in Leko-Adams, San Luis FIDE Wch 2005. It is perhaps good to note that 22...b6? (22...Rab8 does not solve the problems due to 23.Bf4) simply loses material to 23.cxb6 cxb6 24.Rc1; B2) Stockfish gives as more precise 21.g3 Qf6 22.Nd7 Qxd4 23.Nxf8 Bxc5
24.Nxh7! Qxf2+ 25.Kh1 and while it is easy for Stockfish to blink 'large advantage
for White' here, the position remains complicated and precise play is needed. For example, 25...Rd8 analysis diagram
26.Qg4! Qd4 27.Qf5! (the ending after 27.Qxd4 Bxd4 28.Rab1 Kxh7 is far less clear
as Black has decent drawing chances) 27...Qd5+ 28.Qxd5 Rxd5 29.Ng5 Rxd2
30.Re8+ Bf8 31.Rae1 and White should win.
C) 13...Bf5 14.Re1 Rfe8 15.Bf4 Rac8 16.c4. Now, compared to our main game, Black is forced to go with his queen to the centre: 16...Qe4 (16...Qd7? 17.d5 leads to a large advantage for White) 17.Be3
17...Bf6 (White's rook is still on a1 and here Black has an equalizing idea, shown by
Kramnik, in 17...Qc2!; Nepomniachtchi didn't have this queen trade option vs Nakamura as White's rook was already on a2: 18.d5 Na5 19.Nd4 Qxd1 20.Rexd1 Bd7 and the position was equal in Leko-Kramnik, Brissago Wch m 2004). Garry now went for a typical rook lift: 18.Ra2!. White does not have an immediate threat though and Black can choose between different useful moves: 18...b6 (18...Rcd8 certainly came into consideration) 19.h3 (preparing an interesting pawn push!)
19...Na5.
The way Kasparov tried to obtain the initiative here is useful to remember: 20.g4! Bg6 21.g5 Be7 22.Ne5 Bd6. C1) Here, an interesting idea was to try to lock out Black's queen: 23.Bf3 Qh4
24.Bg4 Bxe5 25.dxe5 (25.Bxc8?! looks very risky for White: 25...Rxc8 26.dxe5
Qxh3, though the engine still gives zeros). C11) It is quite possible that the situation after Black's exchange sac 25...Nxc4
26.Bxc8 Rxc8 did not look clear to Kasparov.
analysis diagram Yes, the silicon brain gives White a clear advantage, but precision is needed:
27.Qd7! (27.Qd4? is wrong as after 27...Qxd4 28.Bxd4 c5 Black's queenside pawns

Open files The advantage of controlling an open file is a well-known strategic concept. The diagrammed position shows the most common situation: White has the advantage since he exerts pressure on the pen file and controls it, and Black is trying to equalize by neutralizing that advantage. Barring unexpected factors (blunders), we usually have two basic scenarios: A) Black successfully trades pieces, making a draw, or B) Black suffers (and eventually loses). In our selected game Deac-Robson (see below), it was 'B'. An open file may also produce more dynamic and tactical play. In the other three games I have selected, White controls the e-file, but he connects this strategic trump card to tactical motifs and ideas. Game 41 Sanan Sjugirov 2663 Arjun Erigaisi 2634 Riga 2021
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nf6 3.Nxe5 d6 4.Nf3 Nxe4 5.d4 d5 6.Bd3 Bd6 7.O-O O-O 8.c4
8...c6
8...Be6 has a bad reputation, and for a good reason. Black lands in a passive position
after 9.Re1 Re8. Now White takes space, a typical plan in such positions, and gains a comfortable advantage with 10.c5! 13.Bxe4 Bd5 and for the sacrificed pawn Black has control of the central light
squares and should probably hold this) 12.bxc3 Nd7 13.Bf4 (Black's counterplay attempt does not bring him the desired result) 13...b6 14.Qc2 Nf8
15.Ne5! Bf6 (grabbing a pawn with 15...bxc5 is not advisable as after 16.Bb5 f6
17.Nc6 Qc8 18.c4! Black will soon start losing material) 16.Bb5 Bxe5 17.Bxe5 Bd7
18.c6. White had an advantage and went on to win in Kamsky-Jussupow, Tilburg
1992.

13.Nh4
The dynamic 13.d5!? has been tried in a number of top-level games. In my opinion, Black can hold here, but he needs to be well prepared. Readers interested in theoretical research are advised to investigate this dynamic option.
13...Be6
Let's stop here and take stock. We now get one of the typical Petroff pawn structures.
14.Bxe6 Qxg5 15.Nf3 Qa5 16.Bb3 Nd7 17.Qd3 Rae8 18.Rfe1 Nf6
White has a strong light-squared bishop, but is difficult to further exploit its active position. White has somewhat more space and mobile central pawns, but it's not easy to push them and Black may challenge White's centre by pushing ...c5. The only open file, the e-file, may be of importance, but at the moment it's not clear how either side can take control of it. Most probably, the objective assessment should be: 'dynamic balance/slight pull for White'. Both sides make a couple of useful moves first:

22.Rxe8 Rxe8 23.Ng5 Re7.

Game 42 Bogdan-Daniel Deac 2693 Ray Robson 2689 Prague 2023 Modern theoretical/middlegame discussions in the Ruy Lopez Anti-Berlin variation are centred around White's attempt to prove that only the control of the open e-file is sufficient to put Black under long-term pressure. This game is a good example, and I will also put some other relevant examples in the comments. White tries to combine the control of the e-file, more space, and tactical elements, to put long-term pressure on Black. With precise play Black should be okay, but in human play it's easy to err. Also, in normal circustances, White has pressure while being on the safe side - Black rarely wins those positions, and this often puts White in a psychologically comfortable situation.
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5 Nf6 4.O-O Nxe4 5.Re1 Nd6
6.Nxe5
Recently, White players have also tried 6.a4!?, which is based on a strategic idea: White tries to get a favourable pawn structure on the queenside after 6...a6 (6...Be7 is considered Black's best - see the next game, Grandelius-Howell) 7.Nxe5 Be7 8.Bf1 Nxe5 9.Rxe5 O-O 10.d4 and compared to our current main game we have the moves
6.a4 a6 included. This favours White in quite a number of lines, for example
10...Ne8 11.Nc3 Bf6 12.Re1 d5 13.a5 c6.
White's pawn being on a5 and Black's on a6 clearly favour White here as Black's queenside can be easier targeted. Grischuk achieved the same and got a nice advantage in his game vs Gukesh (see the comments to 13.Qc3!? in this game)!
6...Be7 7.Bf1 Nxe5 8.Rxe5 O-O 9.d4 Bf6 10.Re1
Though the pawn structure is symmetrical, White has some advantage. He has more space and easier development, and controls the only open file. Black aims to exchange a few pieces, finish development and equalize. In practical human vs human play, Black's road to equality is not exactly straightforward, as precision is needed and it is easy to err. We regularly see quite a number of top players stumbling on this road as Black.
10...Re8
At the moment of writing, the text is the most played move here. Black's plan is clear: trade a pair of rooks and finish development. Black's main alternative is 10...Nf5. Here, the most ambitious for White is to take space: 11.d5 Re8 (if Black starts with 11...d6, White plays 12.Nd2 and after 12...Re8
13.Ne4 the situation is not pleasant for Black as White has more space while Black
has problems to develop his cramped pieces) 12.Rxe8+ Qxe8 13.Qd3 d6 14.Nd2. An important moment! Black (Aronian) follows the logic: 'I have less space, let's trade some pieces'. It's sound logic but it doesn't work here. As I said, in human over-the-board play the road to equality is not easy as Black needs to be precise!
14...Bg5 (Black needed to hit White's centre to look for immediate activity with
14...c6! 15.c3 - very probably, Levon did not like 15.Ne4, but Black has suffient
dynamics for equality after 15...Be5! - 15...cxd5 16.Qxd5 Qc6! 17.Qxc6 bxc6
18.Ne4 Be7 and the position is equal; the game Jones-Jakovenko, Khanty-Mansiysk
2019, later ended in a draw) 15.Nf3 Bxc1 16.Rxc1 and Black has a difficult life ahead! White will control the e-file, and keep and increase his space advantage while Black struggles to find any counterplay: 16...Bd7 17.Re1 Qd8 18.Qc4. White's pieces are well coordinated, he has firm grip on the position, and counterplay with ...c6 will work less good now, e.g. 18...g6 19.h3 Ng7 20.Re3 a5
21.a4. It's not easy to give Black good advice, so Aronian says: 'Okay, I don't have
any counterplay but my position is solid, show me your plan'. 21...Ne8 22.Qd4 Ng7. Now White (Magnus Carlsen) took space on the kingside with 23.g4!. Black hopes that with the pawn on g4, White's king position will be weaker and his central counterplay has better chances to work: 23...c6 24.c4! 31.Qxg6 1-0 Carlsen-Aronian, Stavanger 2018.
11.Bf4 Rxe1 12.Qxe1 Ne8
Black's plan is clear: push ...d5, develop the Bc8, preferably trade a few more pieces and make a draw. White has different options, and he can choose between a technical and a tactical approach.
13.c3
A) The tactical option is 13.Nc3!?. White wants to build on his development advantage and does not mind sacrificing (a) pawn(s) in the process. We now get interesting tactical play where White is taking risks too. This has been tried in quite a few games at top level. The ensuing positions are probably dynamically balanced. For readers with a good memory who are willing to put in some serious computer- supported work, it can be rewarding. I will give a couple of interesting examples. The most principled decision, accepting the sacrifice, is Black's best here: 13...Bxd4! (White has a safe advantage after 13...d6 14.Bd3) 14.Nd5 and now: A1) 14...c6 leads more or less by force to a slight technical advantage for White:
15.Ne7+ Kf8 16.Nxc8 Qf6! 17.Rd1! Rxc8 18.Qe4
18...Bc5 19.Rxd7 Rd8 20.Rxd8 Qxd8. White has the bishop pair advantage and a
safe long-term pull. It does not hurt to mention that this is one of those typical positions where the bishop pair brings White an advantage while 'pure bishop vs knight' would be equal. In the few top games we have, White pressed a bit but ultimately it ended in a draw as in, for example, Dominguez Perez-So, Zagreb rapid 2022; A2) 14...d6 15.Bg5 f6 16.Bh4 (on the top level, White has also tried other moves here, like 16.Be3, 16.Qe4 or 16.Bc4) 16...g5! (16...Bxb2 is certainly playable; however, after 17.Rb1 Be5 18.f4

20.fxe5 dxe5 21.Bd3.
Black has collected some pawns but has a weak king. An important moment! White had to sense which was the right trade: 21.Bxf5! (White went for a different trade: 21.Qb4 Qe7 22.Qxe7 Nxe7 with still some pressure, but the game soon dried up and Black held a draw in Grischuk-Gukesh, Dubai 2023) 21...Bxf5 22.Qb4!. Black has serious problems here as we will get a position with opposite-coloured bishops where White is in command. White has pressure here, based on his control of the open file. The only way for Black to stay in the game is 20...Qd8! 21.Qxb7 Rb8 22.Qxa7! Ra8 23.Nxc6 Rxa7 24.Nxd8 Rxa2 25.c4! Kf8!. White will be a pawn up, but Black has drawing chances.
13...d5 14.Nd2
14.Bd3 has been tried in a number of top games (including Carlsen-Karjakin during
their World Championship match in New York 2016). Black responds with 14...g6 followed by ...Ng7 and ...Bf5.
14...Bf5
Black has alternatives in 14...c6 and 14...g6.
15.Qe3 c6 16.Re1
White has pressure here, based on his control of the e-file. He will combine it with pushing his kingside pawns. Can Black equalize? Yes, probably. Is it easy? No. Your engine will give you close to zeroes but do not mistake this for 'easy equality'! Black needs to be precise and it is easy to err.
16...h6 17.h3
One top-level game went 17.Qg3 Bg6 (after the logical trade 17...Bg5 18.h4! Bxf4
19.Qxf4 Black still needs to work to equalize fully) 18.Nf3 Qb6
19.b4 (this is not precise and allowing Black counterplay; 19.Re2! followed by Ne5
would have kept Black under pressure) 19...a5! 20.a3 axb4 21.axb4 Qd8. The open a-file gives Black counterplay, and the game was later drawn in So-Aronian, Istanbul ol 2012.
17...Bg5
White does control the e-file, but how to improve? Push the kingside pawns!
18.g4!
The alternative pushing idea is 18.Bxg5 Qxg5 19.f4 (19.g4 Qxe3! is fine for Black, as the queen trade brings him closer to equality here) 19...Qg6 20.g4 Nd6! with ...Re8 to follow - by pushing pawns White has also abandoned a few squares. Black should hold equality here, but in a game between humans, the position looks easier to play for White. The first question for Black is: should he still allow White to take Bxg5, changing the pawn structure, or not? Robson decides not to react.
18...Bg6
18...Bxf4 19.Qxf4 Bc2 was the alternative. After 20.Nf3, the engine will give many
lines that end close to zero. But the position still looks easier to play for White, who controls the e-file and has the plan to push his kingside pawns.
19.Bxg5 hxg5 20.Nf3 f6 21.h4 gxh4
21...Qd7 was the alternative, but White does have an advantage after 22.Bh3 Be4
23.hxg5 Bxf3 24.Qxf3 fxg5 25.Qe3. His command of the e-file matters, while Black
needs to protect his g-pawn and has to worry that the c3-c4 push, bringing White's bishop into play, does not come at an unfortunate moment.
22.Nxh4
22...Be4?
The decisive mistake! Having the pawn on f3 will come in handy for White. The immediate 22...Bh7 was necessary. Now, after 23.Bd3 Bxd3 24.Qxd3 (similar to the game) Black has 24...Nd6!. White's best reply is 25.Nf5 (now 25.Ng6 does not win as Black has an important tempo with 25...Qd7! and 26.Ne7+ - 26.f3 Re8 is equal - 26...Kf7 doesn't give White more than a draw: 27.Qg6+ Kf8 28.Qh5 Re8
29.Qh8+ Kf7 30.Qh5+ Kf8) 25...Nxf5 26.Qxf5 and the position is still not equal but
Black is moving in the right direction.
23.f3 Bh7 24.Bd3! Bxd3 25.Qxd3
Due to White's e-file pressure combined with Black's weak king, Black can no longer be saved.
25...Rc8

How to crush Black? Push pawns!

36.g5! Ng8 37.Qh8 1-0
To prevent mate, Black will first lose his queen, and then some. Game 43 Nils Grandelius 2672 David Howell 2646 London ENG-SWE Challenge Match 2022
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5 Nf6 4.O-O Nxe4 5.Re1 Nd6 6.a4!?
As I explained in the comments to the game Deac-Robson, this line, which is still considered to be a relative sideline, has a strategic idea behind it. White hopes that Black will push 6...a6, when he will get a strategically favourable version of the main 6.Ne5 lines. David Howell ignores White and continues developing:
6...Be7
White's positional idea behind 6.a4!? becomes clear in the following continuation:
6...a6 7.Nxe5 Be7 8.Bf1
A) If Black plays 8...O-O, White takes space with 9.d4 Nf5 and here White has a choice between playing for a positional advantage or making an interesting pawn sacrifice. A1) With his development advantage and control of the e-file, White has interesting tactical ideas like the pawn sacrifice 10.Nc3!? Ncxd4 analysis diagram
11.Nd5 (11.g4? is not a piece win due to (simple) 11...Nh4 12.Qxd4 d6). White has

Rxf6 16.Re8+) 14.Qxh4 Nxh4 15.Nxc7 Rb8. In general, Black would prefer to have some of the light pieces already traded in positions like these. B) 8...Nxe5 9.Rxe5 O-O 10.d4 Ne8 11.Nc3 Bf6 12.Re1 d5 13.a5!. The advantages White gets by the inclusion of 6.a4 a6 are becoming clear. White will have an advantage on the queenside, where the pawn structure is fixed, plus regular e-file pressure and kingside attack ideas. 13...c6 14.Bf4. B1) Now Aronian, playing Black, went for a standard bishop trade: 14...Bg5 15.Be5 Bf6 16.Qd2 Bxe5 17.Rxe5. White has a good situation here: e-file control, a nice outpost for his knight on the queenside (on c5), and the option to push his kingside pawns like in Deac-Robson. Also, we can compare this position to Grischuk-Gukesh from the comments to Deac- Robson: White has similar trumps here. After 17...Nf6 18.f3 Be6 19.Na4 Qd6 20.b4 Nd7 21.Re3 Rae8 22.Rae1 Black was doomed to passive defence but managed to survive to a draw in Niemann-Aronian, Saint Louis 2022; B2) The silicon brain suggests a radical solution to the problem: 14...b5!?, which is definitely not an easy decision for a human player. White should opt for a pawn sacrifice: 15.axb6! Qxb6 analysis diagram
16.Na4! (another option is 16.Bd3 Bxd4 17.Qd2 when White definitely has

7.Nc3 O-O 8.d4!
White has a development advantage and wants to keep the momentum.
8...Nxd4!?
Black accepts the challenge! A huge majority of top players decided on the relatively boring 8...e4 when we get a standard small plus for White after 9.Nxe4 Nxe4
10.Rxe4.
9.Nxd4 exd4 10.Nd5
Due to his development advantage, White has a strong initiative and Black needs to be careful - the price of each move here is high!
10...Re8?
This logical move is a mistake that should have been (almost) losing. Black's best is 10...Bh4!, but this is just the first in a series of accurate moves Black needs to play to keep the balance. Now the move that puts Black most to the test is

1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5 Nf6 4.O-O Nxe4 5.Re1 Nd6 6.Nxe5 Be7 7.Bf1 Nxe5
8.Rxe5 O-O 9.d4 Bf6 10.Re2!?
This rook retreat is considerably less often played than the main line 10.Re1, but there is definitely logic behind it. White steers the game into slightly different directions as now Black reacts differently.
10...b6
Let's first try to understand the difference with the 10. Re1 retreat. Should Black stick to the same plan and continue 10...Re8, then the rook on e2 offers White a number of advantages because if Black captures ...Rxe2 and White recaptures Qxe2, White's queen will be considerably better placed on e2 than on e1. White has two ways to try to take advantage of this situation: A) 11.Bf4 Rxe2 12.Qxe2. Black is not forced to accept White's pawn sacrifice here, but then White has a risk-free advantage: 12...Bxd4. White has different options (move-orders) here, perhaps the most precise being 13.Bxd6 cxd6 14.Na3. analysis diagram Black needs to try to develop with 14...d5! (grabbing another pawn loses too much time: 14...Bxb2? 15.Re1 Qf8 16.Nb5 - Black cannot complete his development and White soon wins decisive material, e.g. 16...Bf6 17.Nc7 Rb8 18.Qb5! Be7 19.Re3! b6 20.Qe2) 15.Re1 Qf8 16.Nb5 Bb6 17.Nc3! Ba5 18.Qb5 Bxc3 19.bxc3. White gets his pawn back, and Black still needs to develop his queenside, so his problems are not solved yet; B) Another way for White to obtain an edge is 11.c3 and after 11...Rxe2 (we can also use the move-order 11...Nf5 12.Bf4 Rxe2 - not 12...c6?? as with White's rook on e2 instead of e1, this loses on the spot to the simple 13.Bc7! - 13.Qxe2) 12.Qxe2. White's queen being on e2 instead of e1 brings White a couple of advantages: his rook can move directly to e1, exerting immediate e-file pressure, the queen can move to f3 and also White sometimes has Qb5 ideas. For example: B1) If 12...Ne8, White can now take space similar to Carlsen-Aronian (see the comments to Deac-Robson): 13.d5 (another option is 13.Bf4 d5 14.Nd2 and now in case of 14...Bf5 we see the advantage of the queen being on e2: 15.Qb5!) 13...c6 and now it comes in handy for White to have 14.Qf3!, with an advantage in Kovalenko- Omelja, Bialystok 2017; B2) 12...Nf5 13.Bf4 c6. Now White has a good option in 14.g4! Nh4 - thanks to the queen being on e2, this knight jump does not pose any threat, so White can calmly continue 15.Bd6. C) The pawn sacrifice 11.Nc3 is more of a rapid play weapon as Black is fine after
11...Rxe2 12.Qxe2.
Black has to accept the sacrifice: 12...Bxd4! (12...Qe8 leads to an inferior ending for Black after 13.Nd5 Bd8 14.Qf3 Qe4 15.Qxe4 Nxe4 16.Bf4 c6 17.Nc7 Bxc7 18.Bxc7 d5 19.f3 and due to his bishop pair, White had a clear advantage and went on to win in Hansen-Hammer, PRO League rapid 2017) 13.Nd5 c6. Now, White has different ways to get sufficient compensation, but not more: C1) On 14.Bf4, a tactical resource keeps the balance: 14...Bxf2+! 15.Qxf2 Ne4
16.Qd4 cxd5;
C2) Or 14.Ne7+ Kf8 and White has many ideas but nothing substantial, e.g. 15.Bg5 (15.Bf4 Ne8!) 15...f6 16.Nxc8 Rxc8 17.Qd3 Qb6; C3) 14.Nc7 Qxc7 15.Bf4 b6. Now, to keep the momentum, it makes sense for White to sacrifice another pawn: 16.Rd1! (16.c3 looks about equal after 16...Bf6 17.Rd1 Bb7) 16...Bxb2 17.Bxd6 Qd8. White has great domination, but the silicon brain gives zeroes... in human play this can be unpeasant for Black. With the text, Black wants to play ...Ba6 followed by a trade of the light-squared bishops.
11.Re1
McShane first trades a pair of rooks and then develops his Bc8 - probably not the most precise order.
11...Re8
A more accurate development is 11...Bb7. We have a number of top-level games here, most of which end in draws. I would like to explore one a bit further: 12.a4!?. The next few moves are logical: 12...Bg5 13.Nc3 Bxc1 14.Qxc1 Qf6 15.Qd2. An important moment! Black continues his 'trading plan' but this allows White to get an edge: 15...Rae8 (it was better to stop White's a-pawn advance with 15...a5!. David Navara most likely refrained from this decision because he did not like his position after 16.Re5 Rae8 17.Rae1. But Black is able to neutralize White's e-file pressure and equalize with 17...Re6! 18.Nb5 Nxb5 19.Bxb5 and now the 'dynamic defense' 19...Rfe8! 20.Qc3 Rxe5 21.dxe5 Qg6 22.f3 Bc6 with equality) 16.a5!. Now White has an edge. Black continues his 'trading plan': 16...Ne4 17.Nxe4 Rxe4
18.Rxe4 Bxe4 19.Re1 Bf5 20.Qf4 d6.
White has an advantage here, but probably Grandelius did not get the most out of the position. Most precise looks 21.a6! - the e-file is important here! Endings will be unpleasant for Black, for example 21...Be6 22.Qxf6 gxf6 23.Bb5 and Black faces a difficult time (e-file, structural problems, space...). The game went 21.c3 Be6
22.Qxf6 gxf6 and White had an advantage but Black managed to survive in
Grandelius-Navara, Malmö 2022.
12.Bf4 Rxe1 13.Qxe1
A pair of rooks has been traded. Black faces problems similar to other examples in this chapter: White's e-file pressure, space, and Black's bishop on b7 will be passive too. Can Black equalize? Perhaps, but, as in several other examples in this chapter, though the silicon brain evaluates at close to zero, it's not easy in practical human play.
13...Ne8 14.Nc3 Bb7 15.Qd2 d5 16.Re1 Nd6
The counterplay attempt 16...c5 does not bring Black equality after 17.dxc5 bxc5. analysis diagram Due to the badly placed Ne8, this position with hanging pawns in the centre favours White. A) The easy positional way is 18.g3; Black needs to react and White has an advantage after 18...d4 19.Ne4 Be7 20.Bg2 Qb6 21.b3; B) White can also opt for more direct play with 18.Na4 Rc8 (this allows a tactical solution; the alternative 18...c4 also leads to White's advantage after 19.Nc3)
19.Nxc5! Rxc5 20.Qb4 Rxc2 21.Qxb7 and Black's best way to stay in the game is to
aim for an inferior endgame: 21...Bd4 22.Qxd5 Bxf2+ 23.Kh1 Rc8 24.Qxd8 Rxd8
25.Re2 Bb6 26.Rd2 Rc8 27.g3 and Black will struggle for a draw.
17.Bd3
Black goes for immediate counterplay, allowing a nice tactical idea:
17...c5
Changing the central pawn structure with 17...Nc4 18.Bxc4 dxc4 19.Be5 leads to positions that look pleasant for White to me due to the well-placed Nc3 and Black's doubled c-pawns. Probably McShane was thinking the same, but my Stockfish gives close to zeros after 19...Bxe5 20.Rxe5 h6 or immediately 19...h6. An interesting idea was 17...Bxd4!? 18.Bxh7+ Kxh7 19.Qxd4 Kg8. White spots the tactical solution!
18.dxc5! bxc5 19.Nxd5! Bxd5 20.Bxh7+ Kxh7 21.Qxd5
Black is still very much in the game, but precise defense is needed.
21...Be7
A) If 21...Nb5, White grabs a third pawn with 22.Qxf7 and Black's king is weak. After the arguably best 22...Rc8, 23.c3 is better for White, and 22...Bxb2? blunders a piece to 23.Qf5+ Kg8 24.Qe6+ Kh8 25.Qh3+ Kg8 26.Qb3+; B) 21...Ne8 22.Qxf7 Bxb2 does not work for Black due to his weak king: 23.Qh5+ Kg8 24.Rd1 Qc8 25.Qd5+ Kh7 (25...Kh8 loses to 26.Rd3). White has a winning advantage but he still needs to take care: 26.Qe4+! (on 26.Rd3? Black has 26...Nf6!)
26...Kh8 (or 26...Kg8 27.Rd3). Now, with Black's king on the eighth rank, 27.Rd3
wins as 27...Nf6 loses to 28.Rh3+ Qxh3 29.Qxa8+ check!
21...Qa5 was a good defensive resource, e.g. 22.c3
analysis diagram
22...Re8! 23.Rxe8 Nxe8 24.Qe4+ g6 25.Qxe8 Qxa2 - it's not easy to say, but Black
might be closer to a draw than White to a win here. Now White allows Black to escape:
22.Re3
White missed a nice solution with 22.Kf1!. White king is no longer threatened by a back rank mate, while also it will be hiding from checks. Black's best is 22...Rc8 (22...Rb8 loses to 23.Rxe7 Qxe7 24.Bxd6; 22...Kg8 runs into 23.Rxe7) 23.Bxd6 Bxd6 24.Rd1 Qf6 25.Qxd6 Qxb2 26.Qd3+. Now that the smoke has cleared, White is a pawn up in a technical endgame.
22.Bxd6 Bxd6 23.Rd1 leads to a draw after 23...Rb8 24.Qxd6 Qxd6 25.Rxd6 Rxb2.
22...Bf8
This move does not lose, but it forces Black to find a difficult defensive resource a few moves down the road.
22...Rb8! was a relatively easy draw: 23.Bxd6 (23.Rh3+ Kg8 24.Qh5 leads to a draw
by perpetual check with 24...f6 25.Qh7+ Kf7 26.Qh5+ Kf8) 23...Bxd6 (23...Qxd6 leads to a drawn endgame: 24.Qe4+ Kg8 25.Qxe7 Qxe7 26.Rxe7 Rxb2). White's best here is to agree to a draw by move repetition 24.Qh5+ (24.Qxf7 is not mate as Black has 24...Bf4!) 24...Kg8 25.Rh3 Kf8 26.Qe2 Kg8 27.Qh5 Kf8.
23.Rh3+ Kg8 24.Qh5 f6 25.Qh8+ Kf7 26.Qh5+ Kg8 27.g4!
White correctly decides to play on and put Black to the test!
27...Qe8?
The losing mistake. To stay in the game, Black had to come up with a difficult-to- find defensive resource: 27...c4! 28.g5 Qa5!. Black's queen is active and White's king is exposed - Black saves the game! White's best chance is 29.Qh7+ (29.Bxd6 is equal after the easy moves 29...Qe1+ 30.Kg2 Qe4+ 31.Kf1 fxg5=) 29...Kf7 30.Bxd6 Bxd6 31.Qh5+. analysis diagram Black still needs to play a few only moves: 31...Ke6! 32.Re3+ Be5! 33.Qg4+ (or
33.f4 Qd2!) 33...f5 34.Qxc4+ Qd5 35.Qa6+ Qd6 and the game is saved.
28.Qh7+ Kf7 29.Qh5+ Kg8 30.Qh7+ Kf7
Naturally White plays on!
31.Re3! Qc6
As usual, to get to the opponent's king, pawn defences need to be removed:
32.g5! fxg5 33.Bxd6 Bxd6
33...Qxd6 loses to 34.Rf3+ Ke7 35.Qe4+.
34.Qf5+ Kg8 35.Qe6+ Kf8
And White concludes the game with a nice move:
36.Re5! 1-0
A typical Ruy Lopez Breyer position Diagrammed is a typical Ruy Lopez position. This particular position comes from the Breyer Variation but we may get something similar from other closed Ruy Lopez variations. Black has a well-placed knight on the c5-outpost, while White has almost everything else. Many great players, Magnus Carlsen included, have opted for this position as Black. I have some experience with it, and I did not like it because White has different plans while Black does not have clear and easy to execute counterplay. One of White's main plans is to open up the kingside and get an attack there by pushing his f-pawn to f4. The selected game and those included in the comments show White's plans and the problems Black faces in these positions. The f4 push in a closed centre Game 45 Maxime Vachier-Lagrave 2780 Bassem Amin 2704 France tt 2019 In this game I will show a typical type of position coming from the Breyer Variation - as said, similar positions may come from other classical Ruy Lopez variations. The central structure is fixed, Black's knight is (sort of) well placed on c5, while White has many other things! White's main plan is to push the f-pawn to f4, opening up the kingside to get an attack. As a consequence, Black will get a central outpost on e5 (usually for a knight) but nevertheless Black struggles to find active counterplay. I never felt comfortable in such situations as Black and ended up playing a different type of Breyer where the central structure was not fixed with 15...c5 16.d5 c4. This game, and a number of examples given in the comments, illustrate well the kind of problem Black faces or may easily face.
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5 a6 4.Ba4 Nf6 5.O-O Be7 6.Re1 b5 7.Bb3 d6 8.c3 O-O 9.h3
Nb8 10.d4 Nbd7 11.Nbd2 Bb7 12.Bc2 Re8 13.Nf1 Bf8 14.Ng3 g6 15.a4 c5 An important strategic decision, fixing the pawn structure. Black does not have to do this - he can keep the central pawn structure fluid and opt for other main lines here, like for example 15...Bg7.
16.d5 c4 17.Bg5
A typical pawn structure in this line. Black is happy getting his knight to c5, White is preparing a kingside attack, mostly introduced by the with f4 push. White has more space and can combine kingside and queenside plans, as he does in this game. Black's Bb7 often turns out passive, while it is strategically clever for him to trade off his dark-squared bishop - but on the other hand, that bishop protects his king. This strategically complex position has been played in many top-level games. I am personally not really fond of Black's strategy and after years of experience, I think that with this fixed central structure we have to favour White here, primarily due to his space advantage and the options to play on both sides of the board. Let's see how the play develops.
17...Nc5
Black can also place his knight on c5 in a slightly different version: 17...h6 18.Be3 Nc5 and now: A) 19.Qd2 h5 and now: A1) You may wonder what happens if White changes the pawn structure and doubles Black's pawns with 20.Bxc5 dxc5. We have some top-level games here. Yes, White did double Black's pawns, but he has also parted with his bishop pair. White has somewhat better chances. I like the following idea Anish Giri demonstrated in a blitz game: 21.Qe3 Rb8 (White's area of action will be the queenside) 22.Nf1 Bc8 23.N1d2 Bd7 24.axb5 axb5 25.Ra6. Black needs to worry about a timely b3-pawn push by White: 25...Rb6 26.Ra7 Qb8 27.Rea1 Qd6. White is doing great, Anish comes up with a nice idea: 28.Bd1! (28.b3 also leads to White's advantage as the queenside is favourably opened for him after 28...cxb3
29.Bxb3 with 30.c4 to follow and if 30...b4, 31.Ba4 with a favourable bishops trade)
28...Bg7 29.Be2! Reb8 30.Bxc4! (game over!) 30...bxc4 31.Nxc4 Qe7 32.Nxb6
Rxb6 33.b4 Bf8 34.R7a5 Bb5 35.bxc5 Qxc5 36.Qxc5 Bxc5 37.c4 Nxe4 38.Rxb5 and White won easily in Giri-Karjakin, Flor & Fjaere 2014; A2) 20.Bg5. Now where to place black bishop is not an easy choice: 20...Bg7 (20...Be7 is the alternative) 21.Rf1! (White is getting ready to push the f-pawn)
21...Qc7 22.Bh6. Fabiano Caruana faces an unpleasant dilemma: to trade or not to
trade? Both decision do not really look good for Black: 22...Bh8. White now had the right idea, but did not get it done in a precise way:
23.Ng5 (strongest was 23.Nh4!, for example 23...bxa4 24.f4! exf4 25.Rxf4 and
White has a winning attack. In Stockfish language, this is close to plus two) and now: A21) Black's best chance to get counterplay was by timely disturbing White's Ng3 with 23...h4! when after 24.Ne2 bxa4 25.f4 analysis diagram Black does not react in the centre but plays 25...Rab8!. White now struggles to get his attack going: 26.f5 Bc8! 27.Rab1 and here it is clever to trade a pair of rooks (fewer potential attackers, less headache!) 27...a3! 28.bxa3 Rxb1 29.Bxb1. analysis diagram Black's king is safe here, so now he can force the strategically good trade 29...Bg7! with a good game; A22) 23...Nh7 24.Nxh7 (the immediate 24.f4 was strong too) 24...Kxh7 25.Be3 Qe7
26.f4! (it is important to have both rooks on the board as f-file pressure is essential
for a successful attack here) 26...exf4 27.Bxf4 (27.Rxf4 was perhaps even stronger:
27...Be5 28.Raf1!.
analysis diagram A standard situation - Black's king is weak, the Bb7 is out of play, the Nc5 is standing there nicely and doing more or less nothing: 28...Bxf4 29.Rxf4 Nd7
30.Nxh5! and White wins after 30...gxh5 31.Qd1 or 30...Rh8 31.Qf2) 27...Kg8. Now
White doubles on the f-file, so having both rooks on the board is important here:
28.Rf3 Bg7 29.Raf1 Nd7.
Now White traded off the defender with 30.Bh6! Bxh6 31.Qxh6 Qf8 32.Qd2 (32.Nf5 was more efficient) 32...Ne5 33.Rf6 and we have a typical situation. Black has a nice knight on e5, while White has everything else. The knight on the e5-outpost is not enough to hold Black's position. White went on to win in Zherebukh-Caruana, Saint Louis 2017. You may wonder what Fabiano did wrong to get into such trouble? Actually, not much. The position is simply easier to play for White and it is easy for Black to get into terrible trouble. The game we are following was a classical time limit game in the United States Championships. B) White can also play slightly differently, though following a similar plan, with
19.Kh1!? (White plans to push f4 later on and places his king on a better square)
19...Rb8 20.Qd2 h5 21.axb5 axb5
22.Ng5! White is now ready for the f-pawn push: 22...Bg7 23.f4! exf4 24.Bxf4. As
already seen, most of the time Black's counterplay idea in these positions is to timely disturb White's Ng3 and this is what Short, playing Black, does now: 24...h4 25.Nf1 Nh5 26.Be3 Bf6. It looks as if Black has sufficient tactical resources as White's king on h1 can now also come under check. How should White continue? 27.Nh2! Bc8 (the pawn grab
27...Ng3+ is not a good idea here as White develops a strong attack after 28.Kg1

Bb7, Ra8 are out of play - actually a typical situation in this line; White has a winning attack) 28.Nhf3 (White has the right idea, but he could have executed it in a better way. Pro-actively removing the king from check first with 28.Kg1! was strong here. You may wonder, after Black's knight jump to g3, what difference does it make? 28...Ng3. analysis diagram Well, it does as White now has a nice tactic: 29.Nxf7! Kxf7 30.Bxc5 dxc5 31.Nf3 and since the e-pawn push is coming, Black will have to give some material back, White has a clear advantage) 28...Ng3+ 29.Kh2 Ncxe4 30.Nxe4 Nxe4 31.Bxe4 Rxe4
32.Ba7! (White did not want to get greedy with 32.Bb6 Qxb6 33.Rxe4 Bf5 when

34.Bd4. White has a promising attack. Black's king is weak, his Ra8 is out of play,
his Bc8 is not doing much and his pawn on h4 is likely to fall... still, Black managed to survive to a draw in Stellwagen-Short, Hoogeveen 2004.
18.Nh2
White can also play a bit differently. Executing a simlar plan starting with 18.Qd2 also looks promising. The Nf3 will move and the f-pawn push will come. A few top games went 18...Be7 19.Bh6 Nfd7. Black's queenside counterplay is rather slow and White can quickly develop a promising attack: A) 20.Nh2 and now: bxa4 A1) In case of 20...Nxa4, the following variation illustrates how easily things can go wrong for Black: 21.Ng4!? (White can of course opt for 21.Bxa4 bxa4 22.Rxa4 a5
23.Be3 with an advantage - here he can combine queenside play with the f-pawn
push) 21...Nxb2. Black is now two pawns up, and White starts action: 22.f4 Bh4 (White has a promising attack, minimally worth the sacrificed material, after
22...exf4 23.Qxf4) 23.Bg5! Bxg5 24.fxg5 Rf8 25.Rf1. Now Black has to react:
25...f6 26.gxf6 Nxf6 27.Nh6+ Kh8 (again, the f-file is important; having both rooks
on the board is essential for White's attack) 28.Rf3 Qe7 29.Raf1. Black cannot hold his kingside, this position is plus three according to Stockfish! It's very easy for Black to get into trouble in these positions!; A2) If 20...bxa4, White goes for the attack with 21.Ng4 (21.Bxa4 also leads to White's advantage) 21...Nb3 22.Bxb3 axb3
23.f4! Black's Bb7 is a 'dead piece'. 23...Nf6. Here, strongest for White was 24.Rf1
with an attack that should have resulted in a full point. The game went 24.Ne3 exf4
25.Bxf4 and White had an advantage but Black survived to a draw in Borisek-Amin,
Batumi 2018. B) Starting off with 20.Rf1 with Nh2 and f4 to follow is fine for White too; C) An example of 'non principled play' by White was seen some years ago at the Qatar masters (with a classical time limit): 20.a5 Bc8!. Now White placed both of his rooks on passive positions because was overly concerned about losing his a-pawn:
21.Ra3? Nb7 22.Rea1 Ndc5. Now White wants to attack, but without rooks it does
not work the same way: 23.Nh2 Bd7 24.Be3 Bh4. Despite placing both his rooks in passive positions, White's worries about the a5-pawn are not over as Black can play ...Na4 and still collect that pawn. Black easily won in Mista-Giri, Doha 2014;
18...h6 19.Be3 h5 20.Qf3 Nh7 21.Qe2 Qc7
Retreating the knight with 21...Nf6 is not an easy move to make but it would have stopped the development in the game.
22.Ngf1!
A strong move! The knight goes to d2 when: a) after the f4 push, the move ...h4 can never disturb White; and b) Black also needs to worry about a timely b4 or b3 pawn push, opening up the queenside.
