A typical Ruy Lopez piece sacrifice in the centre This position, coming from a recent game, shows a typical piece sacrifice idea available to Black in many closed Ruy Lopez positions. I have tried similar sacrifices as Black and can advise you to explore these ideas. Black sacrifices a piece (usually a knight) to eliminate White's pawn centre. Black gets active play and his mobile pawn centre fully compensates for the sacrificed piece. Game 46 Arthur Pijpers 2465 Max Warmerdam 2602 Utrecht ch-NED 2023 I definitely wanted to include the type of sacrifice described above in this book, but I was looking for a fresh example. Help came from Max Warmerdam, who messaged me while I was writing: 'Hey Ivan, remember the sacrifice idea you were showing us at the Papendal (Dutch Olympic Centre) training last November? I played the same idea in my game yesterday! Have a look!'. Well, I did have a look and indeed - thank you Max, I like the game!
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5 a6 4.Ba4 Nf6 5.O-O Be7 6.Re1 b5 7.Bb3 d6 8.c3 O-O 9.h3
Bb7 10.d4 Re8 11.a3 Less played than 11.Nbd2, but definitely also seen at top level.
11...h6
12.Ba2
There is a positional idea behind this bishop retreat. After pushing 13.d5, White wants to be able to build a pawn chain by playing b3 followed by c4.
12...Bf8
And now indeed White goes on to execute his strategic idea:
13.d5
Where to go with the knight?
13...Ne7
I think this is the best option. A) After 13...Nb8, Shirov, playing White, decided to keep the centre dynamic with
14.c4 (White should have some advantage by building the pawn chain with 14.b3 c6
15.c4) 14...c6. Shirov insists on keeping the centre dynamic: 15.Nc3 bxc4 16.dxc6
Bxc6 (16...Nxc6 was the alternative so as after 17.Bxc4 playing the dynamic
17...Nd4!.
analysis diagram White has some advantage though after 18.Nxd4 exd4 19.Qxd4 d5 20.Bf1 dxe4
21.Be3) 17.Bxc4 Qc7 (17...Nbd7 was better). Shirov does not need a further
invitation and goes for a direct attack! 18.Nh4! Bd7 and now comes a piece sac, the point of the previous move: 19.Nd5! Qxc4 20.Nxf6+ gxf6 21.Qh5. A1) If 21...Kh7, White wins by force with a beautiful variation: 22.b3! (the less forcing 22.Bd2 should bring White success as well) 22...Qxb3 23.Bxh6! Bxh6 analysis diagram
24.Reb1! (Black's queen has to defend the f7-pawn but cannot retreat to e6 due to
the Nf5 jump) 24...Qc4 25.Rb4! Qc3 26.Qxf7+ Bg7. What now? White is two pieces down while the Ra1 hangs with check. 27.Nf5! Qxa1+ 28.Kh2 Bxf5 29.exf5 and White wins, e.g. 29...e4 30.Qg6+ Kg8 31.Qxe8+ etc.; A2) Black played 21...f5 and now the great attacker Shirov missed the strongest continuation: 22.exf5! (the game itself went 22.Bxh6 Bxh6 23.Qxh6 f4 and White emerged victorious in Shirov-Erdös, Terme Catez Ech-tt 2021). The problem is that after 22...Be7 White now needs a strong follow-up: 23.Ng6!. Now Black has to give his extra piece back: 23...Nc6 24.Bxh6 Bxf5 25.Qxf5 Qe6 26.Qxe6 fxe6 27.Nxe7+ Rxe7 28.Rac1 and White is a sound pawn up in a technically won endgame. B) 13...Na7 leads to an easy and typical positional advantage for White after 14.b3 c5 15.c4. White gets a typical safe space advantage: 15...a5 16.a4 b4 17.Nbd2 Nc8 18.Bb1 Ne7 19.Nf1 Ng6 20.Ra2 and Black has no counterplay. He had to struggle for a draw in Ter Sahakyan-Azarov, Sharjah 2022.
14.b3
White sticks to his strategic idea of building a central pawn chain. Playing like Shirov in the comment above does not produce same result as Black's knight is now well placed on e7: 14.c4 c6 15.Nc3 bxc4 16.dxc6 Bxc6 17.Bxc4 Qc7 with equality, e.g. 18.Qb3 Ng6.
14...c6
If 14...c5, White gets an easy advantage with 15.c4! bxc4 16.bxc4 Ng6
17.Bb3! Bc8 18.Ba4 Bd7 19.Nc3. White has a favourable version of the Czech
Benoni, but it was a rapid game and Black later turned things around and even won in Firouzja-Le Quang Liem, Chessable Masters rapid 2021.
15.c4 cxd5 16.cxd5
A critical position! White wants to stabilize the queenside, in the ideal world by pushing 17.b4, and will be having a long-term positional advantage. Max comes up with a very interesting idea, first sacrificing a pawn to sentence White's Ba2 to death, and then sacrificing a piece to remove White's pawn centre, getting dynamic pawns of his own. Black will get only a pawn for his the piece, but White's pieces will coordinate poorly.
16...b4!?
16...a5 was tried before. White continues 17.Nc3 b4 18.Nb5 Qb8 19.a4. White's B
will not stay on a2 forever, it will quickly be improved by Ba2-b1-d3, and White has the advantage here. The immediate sacrifice 16...Nxe4? does not work as the Ba2 is not 'killed' and White has a clear advantage after 17.Rxe4 Nxd5 18.b4! - the most human way.
17.axb4
The silicon brain understands the problem of the passive Ba2 and intially suggests
17.Bd2. Black can now sacrifice the piece but he also has another interesting option
in 17...a5!? (White is hoping for something like 17...bxa3? 18.Nxa3 when his pieces start coordinating excellently; the piece sac 17...Nxe4 18.Rxe4 Nxd5 19.axb4 transposes into the comment to 19.Bd2) 18.axb4 axb4 19.Bxb4 analysis diagram White has grabbed a pawn but has terrible coordination - the Ba2 is dead while the Bb4 is also clumsy. Black should play aggressively: 19...g5!. Now, before developing his knight, White first has to solve the Bb4 problem: 20.Bd2 (20.Nc3?? loses to the simple 20...Qb6 while if 20.Nbd2? the bishop on b4 is also trapped by
20...Nfxd5 21.exd5 Nxd5μ) 20...g4! 21.hxg4 Nxg4 22.Nc3. White's Ba2 is terribly
placed and Black has good compensation after 22...Qb6 23.Be3 Qb4. Now comes the point behind Black's previous move:
17...Nxe4! 18.Rxe4 Nxd5
An interesting position! For the piece, Black got only one pawn, but he has full compensation! Excellent judgement! White's pieces are terribly discoordinated while Black has perfect coordination, combining his active pieces with mobile central pawns. In practice, this position is perhaps easier to play with Black.
19.b5!?
White wants to activate his Ba2 as soon as possible. Another logical move was
19.Bb2, developing the bishop and covering c3 - White wants to play Nbd2. Black,
as usual in such situations, pushes his pawns: 19...f5! 20.Re1 (if 20.Rc4, 20...e4
21.Nd4 e3! and now the only move that keeps White in the game is 22.Nd2!) 20...e4
(20...Nf4 is also possible) 21.Nd4 Qg5. analysis diagram White's king has hardly any defenders, while the Ba2, Bb2 and Nb1 are all doing nothing - an excellent example of White's pieces being only 'technically' on the board while Black's pieces are actually playing. White has to be accurate to stay in the game: 22.h4! (22.Ne2 Nxb4 looks bad for White) 22...Qxh4 23.Nxf5 Qg5
24.Ne3 Nf4. Now finally White develops his Nb1: 25.Nc3 (interestingly, even after
the queen trade 25.Qg4 Qxg4 26.Nxg4 Black still has full compensation as White cannot develop, e.g. 26...Nd3 27.Re2 Rec8 28.Na3 d5) 25...d5. Here, best for White is probably 26.Ne2 when Black has a pleasant choice between going for a line ending in perpetual check or just playing for the attack: 26...Nh3+ (the attacking option was 26...Nd3!? 27.Bd4 Bd6) 27.Kf1 Nxf2 28.Kxf2 Bd6. analysis diagram Unfortunately for Black, he is slowly running out of pieces and a draw will probably be the result after 29.Nd4! Rf8+ 30.Kg1 Qh4 31.Ndf5! Rxf5 32.Nxf5 Qh2+ 33.Kf1 Qf4+ 34.Kg1 Qh2+ 35.Kf1 Qf4+ 36.Kg1 analysis diagram with a draw. Playing for a win is probably asking for too much: 36...Rf8?! 37.Ne7+! Bxe7 (37...Kh7? is not good due to 38.Qh5) 38.Rf1 Qe3+ 39.Kh1. White is not exactly getting mated and his 'big pawn on a2' will become a bishop again one day. If White plays 19.Bd2, we have an unclear game after 19...Qf6! (19...f5?! is not precise because the bishop on d2 covers the e3-square and White is better after
20.Rc4 e4 21.Nd4) 20.Na3 Nf4 21.Rxf4 exf4.
analysis diagram
22.Bb1!. Improving the bishop. Black should now damage White's structure:
22...Bxf3! 23.gxf3 Re5 or 23...d5 and we have an unclear, materially imbalanced
game.
19...axb5
The fancy 19...Nf4 20.Ra4 Nxg2 21.Kxg2 e4 does not work as Black has no good follow-up after 22.Nbd2!.
20.b4
The Ba2 is finally active, but there is still the a-file pin!
20...Ra4!
Black wants to play ...Qa8.
21.Bxd5
White decides to solve the a-file pin tactically. In case of 21.Bd2, the pin is unpleasant for White; for example, 21...Qa8 22.Qb3 Qa6 23.Nc3 Ra3 24.Qb2 Ra8 and after 25.Re2 Nxc3 26.Bxc3 Be7 it is not clear how White is going to unpin.
21...Bxd5
Black wants to keep his strong bishop. The alternative was 21...Rxa1 when we get a different material imbalance that may result in equality: 22.Bxb7 Rxb1 (Black has a viable alternative in 22...Qc7 23.Bb2 Qxb7 and after 24.Re3 Ra4 25.Rb3 we have an unclear game. Black has potentially great central pawns, but it's not easy to push them) 23.Qc2 Ra1 24.Qb2. analysis diagram Of course, Black is not going to allow White to have a great blockading bishop on d5: 24...d5! 25.Rxe5 (25.Qxa1? is wrong as Black forces a better ending with
25...dxe4 26.Bxe4 Qd1+ 27.Kh2 Rc8! 28.Bb2 Qxa1 29.Bxa1 f6. White is in deep
trouble here and can lose in a few moves, e.g. 30.Bd3 Bd6 31.g3 and now White loses a piece after 31...Ra8). Now the storm clears and it all ends peacefully:
25...Rxc1+! 26.Qxc1 Rxe5 27.Nxe5 Qb8 28.Qc6 Qxe5 29.Qxb5 Qe1+ 30.Qf1 with a
draw.
22.Rxa4 Bxe4 23.Ra1 d5
The position looks unclear to me. Yes, at the moment Black only has two pawns for the piece, but Black's central pawns are strong, and the bishop pair is good in this type of position... my Stockfish, however, gives a slight plus to White.
24.Nfd2
The alternative was 24.Nbd2 Bg6 25.Bb2 f6 when the position looks unclear to me, my silicon advisor slightly preferring White.
24...Bg6
24...Bf5! is apparently more precise as after 25.Nc3 Qd7 26.Qb3 the bishop can
change diagonals with 26...Be6.
25.Ra5?
The start of a bad plan. White is too focused on collecting material - he should have focused on his piece development and coordination.
25.Nc3! was needed, and after 25...Bxb4 26.Qb3 Bxc3 27.Qxc3 Qb6 28.Qb3 d4
29.Nf1 the position looks okay for Black to me, though my Stockfish gives White
some plus.
25...d4 26.Rxb5 e4
Black has only one pawn for the piece, and still White needs to find only moves to stay in the game! White probably underestimated his bad piece coordination and the power of Black's pawns:
27.Qb3?
This is a mistake that should have lost the game.
27.Na3 is not good due to 27...e3.
Best was 27.Nc4! but the problem is that after 27...e3! 28.fxe3 Bxb1 analysis diagram Black has his piece back and White looks to be in bad shape. The only way to stay in the game is 29.Re5! (29.exd4 loses nicely to 29...Bd3! and after 30.Qxd3 Re1+ Black either gets mated or loses decisive material: 31.Kf2 Rxc1 or 31.Kh2 Qc7+
32.Ne5 Qxc1) 29...dxe3 30.Qxd8 Rxd8 31.Nxe3 Bxb4 32.Rb5.
analysis diagram Unfortunately for White, he does not win a piece: 32...Rc8! 33.Rxb4 Rxc1+ 34.Kf2 and Black is a pawn up, although White might draw.
27...e3!
All White's pieces are uncoordinated and on the wrong side of the board; the white king fights alone. Black has a decisive attack.
28.Nf3 Qc7
White now has to make a sad move, removing another piece from the defence of his king.
29.Ba3 Bc2 30.Qb2
Black has a winning attack, but precision is still needed. Now comes the first missed win.
30...Bd3
30...exf2+ 31.Kxf2 Bd1! was winning on the spot as White cannot bring his pieces to
the defence of his king 32.Qxd4 (giving back a piece with 32.Re5 only delays resignation as after 32...Rxe5 33.Nxe5 Qxe5 Black still has a mating attack)
32...Re2+ 33.Kf1 Qg3 and White gets mated: 34.Qg1 Rc2.
31.Ra5 Qf4?
The second win was more difficult than the first one: 31...exf2+ 32.Kxf2 (32.Qxf2 loses to the simple 32...Bxb1 and Black has his piece back while his mating attack continues as White's Ba3 and Ra5 are still terribly placed). analysis diagram Black has a winning position, but in time pressure (which is likely to be the case) it's not easy to find 32...Re3! with the terrible threat of 33... Rxf3! which, after a series of checks, will mate the white king. It's also not easy to understand that after
32...Re2+ 33.Qxe2 Bxe2 34.Kxe2 Qc4+ 35.Kf2 White's pieces are so poorly
coordinated that he will start losing material after 35...Bd6 36.Nbd2 Qc3.
32.fxe3 Qxe3+ 33.Kh1
33...Qe1+!
Not the best move, but it deserves an exclamation mark for its artistic beauty! Objectively stronger was 33...Bd6 (34...Qe1+ is a threat) 34.Qc1 Bf4!. White has to give his piece back with 35.Qxe3 dxe3 36.Nc3 e2 37.Ne1 Bg3 38.Nxe2 Bxe1. Black is clearly better; White's struggle continues.
34.Nxe1 Rxe1+ 35.Kh2 Bd6+ 36.g3 Be4
This is the position Max had in mind - White is a full queen up but the position is objectively equal! Very probably, both players were under terrible time pressure now. White makes it easy for Black:
37.Qg2?
This prevents mate but gives Black a risk-free advantage. The only (and much better) alternative was 37.Qc1! when Black's next two moves are forced: 37...Re2+ 38.Kg1 Bxg3. analysis diagram An amazing position! Yes, Black does threaten many checks but there is no immediate mate and White is still a full queen up! White's pieces are so poorly coordinated that the position is dynamically balanced: A) 39.Ra8+ leads to a forced draw after 39...Kh7 40.Re8 Rg2+ 41.Kf1 Rf2+
42.Kg1=;
B) 39.Nd2 leads to a forced draw: 39...Rg2+ 40.Kf1 Rf2+ 41.Ke1 Rh2+ 42.Kd1 Rh1+ 43.Ke2 Rh2+=; C) 39.Qc8+ Kh7 40.Rf5!. analysis diagram White is bringing in defenders. There is no mate or perpetual but now the d-pawn is Black's trump! 40...d3! (40...Rh2? blunders into 41.Rf3!; Black does have a threat and it is mate in two: 41...Bh2+ followed by 42...Bg2#) 41.Rf3! Re1+! 42.Kg2 Rxb1. analysis diagram Again, an amazing position! Black does not have an immediate deadly threat and for the queen has only a bishop - and a strong passed pawn! C1) After 43.Kxg3 White is again a full queen up, but... 43...d2! and now the only move is 44.Qc4! f5! (or 44...Rg1+ 45.Kf4 d1Q 46.Qxe4+ g6 when the engine gives zeroes... human play is obviously a different story) 45.Rf1 Re1 46.Qd4! and now we get a different kind of material imbalance after 46...Rxf1! 47.Qxd2 Rf3+ 48.Kh2 Rxa3 and the evaluation is zeroes, but probably it's easier to play for Black; C2) If 43.Bc1, 43...Be1! keeps the pins, threats, and the balance; C3) 43.Qc4 f5! 44.Qa2 (44.Kxg3 transposes to the dynamically balanced line we had in the comment above: 44...d2 45.Rf1 Re1 46.Qd4 Rxf1 47.Qxd2 Rf3+ 48.Kh2 Rxa3) and again we transpose to the positions from the comments above after 44...d2
45.Qxd2 Rb3 46.Kxg3 Rxf3+ 47.Kh2 Rxa3.
37...Bxg2 38.Kxg2 Rxb1
Black now has a risk-free endgame advantage.
39.Rd5 Bc7
Probably a better decision was to go for a rook ending with 39...Bxg3 40.Kxg3 Rb3+.
40.b5 Rb3 41.Bd6 Bb6
White can save the game but needs to be precise:
42.Be5?
A losing blunder. 42.h4!, preventing Black from pushing ...g5 and thus securing the bishop on f4, saved the game, e.g. 42...f6 43.h5.
42...f6!
White's bishop is running out of a good squares.
43.Bf4 g5! 44.Bb8
Or 44.Bc1 Rc3.
44...d3
Black's d-pawn queens.
44...Kf7 also wins.
45.g4 Ba5 46.Kf3 d2+ 47.Ke2 Rb1 0-1
Zaitsev dynamics I have decided to conclude this book with a famous game. The diagrammed position shows a principled battle between the two great Ks. Anatoly Karpov loved this structure as Black since it is fundamentally sound while offering Black active play. There is some resemblance with the Benoni, but the pieces are placed differently. Black wants to plant one of his knights on d3, gaining the bishop pair, and often doesn't mind sacrificing a pawn in the process. Black also has the idea of pushing ...f5, hitting White's centre. Black's potential problem? His weak king. Anatoly Karpov was famous for defending his king with a minimum of resources and has done so throughout his matches with Kasparov. I advise the reader to go through their match games while paying attention to this strategy of 'minimum resources' - it will be both interesting and rewarding! Game 47 Garry Kasparov 2740 Anatoly Karpov 2705 London/Leningrad Wch m 1986 In their heritage World Championship matches, the two great Ks battled out a principled 'Zaitsev Variation' fight, and this game is the one I like the most. You may wonder why I included this 'over-analysed' game in my selection... Well, I would like to offer some personal insights and hope they will be beneficial to the reader.
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5 a6 4.Ba4 Nf6 5.O-O Be7 6.Re1 b5 7.Bb3 d6 8.c3 O-O 9.h3
Bb7 10.d4 Re8 11.Nbd2 Bf8 12.a4 h6 The main line Zaitsev - the battleground of the two great Ks!
13.Bc2 exd4 14.cxd4 Nb4 15.Bb1 c5 16.d5 Nd7 17.Ra3
Over the years, Anatoly Karpov, not only against Kasparov but also in many of his other games, would be choosing here between two principled but strategically rather different options. I like Black's choice in this game, and I think it is enough for equality.
17...c4!
I like Black's ideas here. Black wants to jump ...Nc5 followed by ...Nd3, forcing White to part with his bishop pair. Without his light-squared bishop, White will find it more difficult to build up an attack. Bear in mind, however, that in this game, and often in this line, Black is going to be defending with a minimum of resources! The other principled choice is 17...f5. Black weakens his kingside but wants to eliminate White's pawn centre. The reason why we no longer see this variation at top-level events is the silicon brain's suggestion 18.g4! (Kasparov's choices were
18.Rae3 or 18.exf5). In the few top-level games played with this move, White has
had excellent results. The idea is that 18...fxg4 (to readers willing to invest more time in analysing this position, I would say that in my opinion the positions after
18...f4! are more complicated than the initial computer evaluations tell us) is
answered with 19.Nh2! and White swiftly gets a strong attack. White is now on an important crossroads; he has to chose between three main continuations. I will try to provide some middlegame insight combined with opening information. In this game, Garry attacks Black's pawn on b5 while keeping the option of swinging his Ra3 to the kingside, which is a typical attacking idea in this line.
18.Nd4
A) The alternative, also seen in the K-K matches, is 18.axb5 axb5 19.Nd4. Now Black should eliminate White's attacking rook: 19...Rxa3! (ignoring the danger by playing 19...Qb6 20.Nf5 Ne5 is rather risky as after 21.Rg3! White develops a strong attack, a great example being the game Anand-Adams, San Luis Wch 2005)
20.bxa3 and now is the time for Black to kill White's light-squared bishop: 20...Nd3!
21.Bxd3 cxd3 and the position is about equal. 22.Bb2. Here, modern theory prefers
22...Nc5! (the old classic was 22...Qa5 23.Nf5 Ne5 with complicated play, White
emerging victorious in Kasparov-Karpov, London/Leningrad 1986). In one relatively recent top-level test, White hoped for a kingside attack but it did not materialize and White got in trouble: 23.Re3. The defences of Black's king are limited, but in this game White's attack does not get off the ground: 23...Qa5. White now goes for a sophisticated 'prophylactic attacking move': 24.Kh2 (more straightforward alternatives are 24.Nf5 or 24.Rg3) 24...b4 25.N4b3 Qa4 26.axb4?! (the start of White's problems; he had to opt for 26.Rg3! Nxe4 27.Nxe4 Rxe4
28.Qf3) 26...Qxb4 27.Nxc5 dxc5 28.Qc1 c4 and Black's pawns are strong. Black
was better and went on to win in Huschenbeth-Bu Xiangzhi, Douglas 2019. B) Modern theory follows the silicon brain's suggestions, and top-level games currently feature 18.Ree3!?. The rook runs away from ...Nd3 tempi and is ready to switch to the attacking square g3. We have quite a number of recent top-level games here. The position is very sharp, and probably dynamically balanced. I advise readers interested in theoretical verdicts to investigate in the direction of 18...Nc5! 19.b3 and now: B1) 19...Rc8 leads to White's advantage after 20.axb5 axb5 21.bxc4! (now the Ra3 is fully in play) 21...bxc4 22.Bb2 Nbd3. White now went for a pawn grab with
23.Nxc4 (also promising is 23.Bd4 - White wants to grab the pawn on c4 for free, so
Black has to react: 23...f5 24.exf5 Bxd5 25.f6! and Black is reminded of his potentially weak king) 23...Nxb2 24.Nxb2 g6 25.Na4 Nd7 26.Bd3 and White is a sound pawn up but Black managed to hold a draw in Ivic-Fedoseev, Krasnaya Polyana 2021; B2) Black should sacrifice a pawn with 19...c3! 20.Rxc3, and as a result of the pawn sac, the Ra3 is out of play so Black can open up the position further with 20...f5! with complicated play; Black won in Aravindh-Visakh, Moscow 2020.
18...Qf6 19.N2f3
Karpov now continues his plan to plant the knight on d3:
19...Nc5 20.axb5
A) White has an interesting alternative in 20.Ree3!? with ideas similar to 18.Ree3. We have here an interesting rapid game Anand-Kasimdzhanov played in the final match of a Corsica K.O. event: 20...Nbd3. A1) Here, White had an interesting option in 21.Bxd3 and if 21...b4 (Black has alternatives in 21...cxd3 or 21...Nxd3), White sacrifices an exchange with 22.Bc2! bxa3 23.bxa3. Now Black has to stop Bb2 with 23...c3 and White has sufficient compensation, we have an unclear game after 24.a5 or 24.Ne2; A2) In the game, White went for a pawn grab: 21.axb5 axb5 22.Nxb5 Rxa3 23.Nxa3 Rxe4 24.Nxc4. analysis diagram Rustam now went for an interesting tactical idea, taking a risk: 24...Nxf2!? (24...Rxc4 25.Bxd3 Nxd3 26.Rxd3 Qf5) and it paid off as Vishy now blundered:
25.Qe2? (White had to continue with 25.Kxf2 Rxc4 26.b3! when the black rook does
not have any good squares so an exchange sac is needed: 26...Rxc1! 27.Qxc1 Bxd5 and Black has decent compensation, perhaps White has some plus). Now the tactics are working for Black: 25...Nxh3+! 26.gxh3 Qg6+ 27.Kf2 Rxe3! 28.Bxe3 Qxb1 and Black was material up and went on to win in Anand-Kasimdzhanov, Bastia rapid 2006; B) 20.b3 Nbd3 21.Bxd3 Nxd3 leads to satisfactory play for Black. The position is dynamic while the Ra3 still needs yet to be brought into play.
20...axb5 21.Nxb5 Rxa3 22.Nxa3 Ba6
We have a typical situation for this line. White is a pawn up, Black has the bishop pair and will have a strong knight on d3. The evaluation? A dynamic balance. My personal preference? Black. Kasparov improves his rook and moves away from ...Nd3 tempi:
23.Re3
Karpov says: 'The b2-pawn will be mine.' Please note that Black is not concerned about his king safety and will use a minimum of forces to defend his monarch!
23...Rb8
The alternative was 23...Nbd3!?. White understands that his chances are connected to a kingside attack:
24.e5! dxe5 25.Nxe5 Nbd3
A) 25...Ncd3 was the alternative, covering c2; B) 25...Nxd5!? was an interesting tactical option. B1) 26.Qxd5 leads to a forced draw: 26...Rd8 27.Qf3 Qxf3 28.Nxf3 Nb3!. Black gets his piece back and it ends peacefully: 29.Ba2 (hanging on to the piece with
29.Re1 Bb4 30.Rf1? is not a good idea as Black wins after 30...c3 31.bxc3 Bxa3
32.Bxa3 Bxf1) 29...Nxc1 30.Bxc4 Bxa3 31.Bxa6 Bxb2 with a draw;
B2) I guess Karpov did not like 26.Ng4 when the engine shows zeroes, but to a human the position looks dangerous for Black as the defending forces around his king are limited: 26...Qd6! 27.Rg3 Kh8 28.Qc2 Nd3! 29.Nxc4 Bxc4 30.Qxc4 Nxc1
31.Qxc1. Here, Black is active enough to compensate for the pawn deficit: 31...Nf4
32.Re3 Qd4 with a probable draw.
26.Ng4
One of many critical moments in this game! 26.Qc2! was a better move.
26...Qb6!?
Totally unconcerned about his king safety, Karpov moves another piece away from his monarch. He had a strong resource in 26...Qf5! when Black is better! White has a lot of tactics here but none seems to work, e.g. 27.Rf3 (or 27.Rg3 Kh8) 27...Qxd5. analysis diagram The pawn on b2 will fall, and White does not seem able to create enough on the kingside - after all, his Na3 is out of play while his Bb1 is blocked by Black's Nd3: A) 28.Nf6+? does not work due to 28...gxf6 29.Rg3+ Bg7 30.Bxh6 Ne6 and Black wins with 31.Nxc4 (or 31.Rxg7+ Nxg7 32.Qg4 Kf8) 31...Ndf4!; B) 28.Bxh6 loses to 28...Ne4! and now the Bh6 hangs there is no good retreat:
29.Bc1? Bxa3;
C) After 28.Rg3, now the engine is not afraid to lose a pawn on h6: 28...Qe6! (or
28...Ne4! 29.Nxh6+ Kh8 and Black is better; the safe human move 28...Kh8 is fine
but perhaps it promises no more than a draw after 29.Ne3) 29.Nxh6+ Kh8 30.Re3 Qd7 and Black is better; his Nd3 is simply too strong.
27.Rg3 g6 28.Bxh6
Cold blooded Karpov goes for his pawn:
28...Qxb2 29.Qf3!
Again, a critical moment! White obviously has an attack but, also obviously, he will lose at least a piece - his Na3.
29...Nd7
Anatoly brings one defender closer to his king. The first question, however. is: Can Black grab 29...Qxa3 ? The answer is yes! 29...Qxa3! leads to a draw by perpetual check: 30.Nf6+ Kh8 31.Qh5 Rxb1+ 32.Bc1+ (32.Kh2 leads to a draw too:
32...Rh1+! 33.Kxh1 Nxf2+ 34.Kg1! Qxg3 and White has to force perpetual check
with 35.Bg7+ Kxg7 36.Ne8+) 32...Kg7. There is no mate, so White has to agree to a draw: 33.Ne8+ Kg8 34.Nf6+. Most probably Karpov calculated these lines but rejected them, considering that his position should offer him more. Garry now needs to show great attacking skills indeed!
30.Bxf8 Kxf8
White does not have any immediate threats while he is about to lose decisive material. Kasparov now plays the famous 'prophylactic attacking king move':
31.Kh2!!
Now, in crucial lines, the bishop on b1 will not hang with a check! The position is extremely complicated, and objectively dynamically balanced. It's one of those typical positions where the better calculator wins! Karpov goes for material gain, continuing his strategy of 'minimum defensive resources'. In a World Championship match with one of the greatest attackers in history, using such a strategy takes great confidence!
31...Rb3
A) A player concerned about his king safety may play 31...Qd4. Without the Bb1 and Na3 White can't expect his attack to succeed and a draw is a likely outcome, e.g.
32.d6 (or 32.Qe3 Qxe3 33.Nxe3 Nxf2 34.Bxg6 fxg6 35.Rf3+ Ke7 36.Rxf2 c3 when
the play is still sort of dynamic but a draw is likely) 32...N7e5 33.Nxe5 Nxe5
34.Qc3=;
B) The piece grab 31...Qxa3?? is a blunder: 32.Nh6 N7e5 (or 32...f5 33.Rxg6)
33.Qf6! and White wins as 34.Rxg6 is a terrible threat, e.g. 33...Qb2 34.Rxg6 Ke8
35.Rg3 etc.;
C) Trading an attacker, the Ng4, also makes sense: 31...N7e5 32.Nxe5 Qxe5 and we get a drawn ending after 33.Nxc4 Bxc4 34.Bxd3 Bxd3 35.Qxd3 Rd8.
32.Bxd3 cxd3
Bringing the rook closer with 32...Rxd3 leads to a better ending for White: 33.Qf4 Qxa3 34.Nh6 Qe7 35.Rxg6 Qe5 36.Qxe5 Nxe5 37.Rxa6 Rxd5 and White is a pawn up; the c-pawn is dangerous.
32...Rxa3!, taking the 'insignificant' Na3, is not an easy move to make; it leads to
equality after 33.Qf4 Rxd3 34.Qd6+ Kg7. analysis diagram Which piece to collect? 35.Qxd7! (after 35.Qxa6? Rxg3 36.fxg3 c3 the c-pawn is too strong) 35...Rxg3 36.fxg3 Bb7! (36...c3? is not good as Black's king is weak: 37.Qc7 c2 38.Ne5) with a drawn queen ending after 37.Ne3 Qd4 38.Qxb7 Qxe3=.
33.Qf4
33...Qxa3?
The losing mistake! Karpov probably missed White's 37th move.
33...d2! was needed. This is difficult to calculate and the game was also in the time
trouble phase. Black seems to be able to hold! 34.Nh6 Nf6! (34...Qf6? blunders the pride of Black's position after 35.Qxd2!) A) Now 35.Qd6+ looks very temping but does not win: 35...Ke8 36.Qxa6 d1Q!
37.Qc8+ Ke7 38.Qc5+ Kd7 39.Qc6+ Ke7. Now the only way to continue the fight is
40.d6+!.
analysis diagram And now the cold-blooded 40...Kf8! (Most players will play 40...Qxd6 but after
41.Qxd6+ Kxd6 42.Nc4+ Ke7 43.Nxb2 Rxb2 44.Rf3! Black still needs to work for
the draw) 41.Qc8+ Kg7. Black has a massive material advantage, but his king is defended with a 'minimum of resources': 42.Nf5+ Kh7 43.Qf8. analysis diagram Black stays alive with 43...Ng4+! 44.Rxg4 (44.hxg4 is not a winning attempt as Black has 44...gxf5 45.f3 Rxf3! and the outcome will be a draw after 46.Qxf7+ Qg7
47.Qxg7+ Kxg7 48.Rxf3 fxg4 49.Rg3 Qxd6 50.Nc2 etc.). Luckily for Black, he has
enough material to give! 44...Rxh3+! 45.Kxh3 Qh1+ 46.Kg3 Qe5+ 47.Rf4 g5
48.Qxf7+ Kh8 49.Qf8+ Kh7.
analysis diagram White still has a funny winning attempt here: 50.Qe7+!? Qxe7 51.dxe7 gxf4+
52.Kxf4
analysis diagram Only White can play for a win here, so Black should now secure a funny draw by stalemate with 52...Qxg2 53.e8Q Qg4+ 54.Ke5 Qe4+ or 54.Ke3 Qe2+; B) 35.Rxb3 Qxb3 36.Qxf6 Qxd5. The d-pawn is strong and Black's king is weak:
37.Nxf7 d1Q 38.Nd6+ Kg8 39.Qxg6+ Kf8 40.Qf6+ Kg8.
analysis diagram
41.Nf5! Qxf5 42.Qxf5 Qd6+ 43.Kg1 Qxa3 White will obviously try here for a bit but
the game should end in a draw.
34.Nh6 Qe7 35.Rxg6 Qe5
The defensive resource Karpov was banking on - White's queen is pinned!
36.Rg8+ Ke7
And now comes the famous move that destroys Black's coordination!
37.d6+!
Black will now first lose his queen, and then some.
37...Ke6 38.Re8+ Kd5 39.Rxe5+ Nxe5 40.d7 Rb8 41.Nxf7 1-0
Show in Text Mode Chapter 7 Exercises Exercise 1 - easy Black to move How should Black proceed? What is the right plan for Black here? Show/Hide Solution Solution 1 Answer: Black first needs to fix the central pawn structure, gain space, and remove White's Bd3 from its good post:
12...c4! 13.Be2
Now, activating the bishop on c8 is the right answer. Korchnoi (in his game vs Mecking, Beverwijk 1978) did it in the most precise way:
13...Qa5 14.Bd2 Bd7
Black has a clear plan (improving his bishop) and is at least equal. Exercise 2 - easy White to move With this fixed Winawer pawn structure, is it clever for White to take immediate action with 11.h5 and after the forced 11...g5 play 12.f4, quickly opening up the kingside? Show/Hide Solution Solution 2 This pawn structure can be compared to that in Almasi-Beliavsky and Karjakin- Grischuk elsewhere in this book. This game is another example of how easily even a very strong player can go wrong in these strategically complex positions. Answer: No, it is not as after
11.h5?! g5 12.f4?
White's queen on g4 is badly placed and Black responds with
12...f5!
and is better as after
13.Qg3
(in case of 13.exf6 Nxf6, Black develops fast and takes the initiative)
13...g4
the kingside is fixed and White's bishop pair is useless, while Black has the advantage on the queenside. Exercise 3 - easy White to move Black has already castled and arguably has a development advantage. He wants to open up the centre with the standard push ...f6. Should Black be happy with his position here? Show/Hide Solution Solution 3 Answer: No! White knight is still on its initial g1-square so White can comfortably keep the center closed:
11.f4!
White now simply rolls his kingside pawns, while Black does not have any meaningful counterplay. White has a clear advantage. Exercise 4 - easy/medium Black to move White has grabbed a pawn, but is his queen getting trapped? Evaluate the position. Show/Hide Solution Solution 4 Answer: Yes, it is!
17...Rhg8 18.h3
Or 18.a4 Rdf8 19.Qh7 Nce7 with ...Rh8 or ...Rg7 to follow.
18...Rdf8 19.Qxf8+ Rxf8 20.hxg4 Nfe7
And Black soon won in Almasi-Beliavsky, Hungary tt 1996. Exercise 5 - medium Black to move Kasparov had his bishop on d3 and has just played Be4. Was this a good decision? Show/Hide Solution Solution 5 Answer: No, it was not because due to White's back rank problems this blunders a piece:
45...f5! 46.Bxf5 Ra8
Luckily for White, his piece blunder was not fatal and he escapes into a drawn ending:
47.Bb1 Qb2 48.h4 Ra1 49.Kh2 Qxb1 50.Qxe5 Qh1+ 51.Kg3 Qe1+ 52.Qxe1 Rxe1
With a theoretically drawn ending, though a number of mistakes were still made before White eventually saved a draw in Kasparov-Kramnik, Moscow 2001. Exercise 6 - easy/medium White to move White's chances are clearly on the queenside, but how to proceed? Propose three candidate moves for White. Show/Hide Solution Solution 6 Answer: White has a great position (a clear advantage) and a number of options. It is very good if one of your three candidate moves was the thematic exchange sacrifice:
14.Rb4! Nxb4 15.axb4
And White's pawn avalanche will run Black over, Shirov-Zs.Polgar, Pardubice 1994. Exercise 7 - medium White to move White is doing great on the queenside. Black has just pushed 20... h3, trying to create counterplay. How should White react here? Show/Hide Solution Solution 7
21.gxh3!
Black has many tactical ideas that don't work, White has a large advantage here. In the actual game (Agrest-Nisipeanu, Batumi Ech-tt 1999) White reacted with
21.g3?. Now Black's pawn will stay on h3 until the end of the game and due to the
weakness of White's king Nisipeanu managed to save the game: 21...axb5 22.cxb5. Putting his queen on c7 was not a good idea for Black in the first place. 22...Qd8!. White now creates a strong passer on c6 and may have thought he had a winning advantage... 23.Bxe5 dxe5 24.Qxd8 Rexd8 25.Nc6 Bxc6 26.bxc6 ... but Black saves the game due to White's weak king - the pawn on h3 doing an excellent job:
26...Rd2 27.Ba6 (or 27.Bf1 Rbb2 28.Ne2 Bd8! 29.c7 Bxc7 30.Rxc7 Rxe2 31.Bxe2
Rxe2 with a draw as in the game) 27...Rbb2 (the silicon brain will tell you 27...Ne8 was good as well, but the text is more human) 28.Ne2 Bd6 29.c7 Bxc7 30.Rxc7 Rxe2 31.Bxe2 Rxe2. analysis diagram White king is 'in the box' and the extra exchange does not matter here. 32.Rf1 g5
33.g4 Kg7 34.Kg1 Rg2+ 35.Kh1 Re2 36.Rc3 Nd7 37.Rfc1 Rf2 38.Kg1 Rg2+
39.Kh1 Rf2 1⁄2-1⁄2
Exercise 8 - medium White to move Anatoly Karpov was famed for defending with a 'minumum of resources'. Here he has gone a step too far. How did Judit Polgar destroy Karpov here? Show/Hide Solution Solution 8 Answer: Judit went for the famous 'Lasker sacrifice':
25.Bxh7+! Kxh7 26.Qh5+ Kg8 27.Bxg7!
Exercise 9 - medium/advanced White to move This position could have come from the game Ponomariov-Kramnik, Wijk aan Zee
2005. The position looks complicated; Black has just sacrificed an exchange on c4.
Can you find the win for White here? 'Mating net imagination' helps! Show/Hide Solution Solution 9 Answer: White crushes Black with
26.f5!!
and Black does not have a good response as
26...Ne5
runs into a nice mating net:
27.Rxg7+! Kxg7 28.f6+ Kg8 29.Rh8+! Kxh8 30.Qh1+ Kg8 31.Qh6 Qf8 32.Ne7+
Qxe7 33.Qg7# Exercise 10 - medium White to move White (Kasparov) is doing great here. Can you find an 'instant kill' for White? Show/Hide Solution Solution 10 Answer: White gets the job done with a 'single swipe of his sword':
27.Ng4!!
Black resigned as mate or decisive material loses cannot be avoided, Kasparov- Lautier, Moscow 1994. For example: 27...Rxg5 28.Nxe5, 27...Qe6 28.Rd8! or
27...fxg4 28.Qxe5+.
Exercise 11 - advanced Black to move Black has a winning attack, but precision is needed. There is only one road to victory here! Try to calculate a win for Black without moving pieces. Show/Hide Solution Solution 11 In their game in Belgrade 1989, Nigel Short could have posed Zdenko Kozul more 'execution problems' by playing 28. Rxd6. Answer: You did very well if you calculated this line correctly:
28...Rb2+! 29.Kxb2 Qa2+ 30.Kc1 Qa1+ 31.Kd2 Ra2+ 32.Ke3
Now Black needs a last precise check - you did an excellent job if you saw it from the starting position:
32...Qc3+!! 33.Rd3
Or 33.Bd3 Qd2+ 34.Kf3 when Black can afford the luxury to take everything:
34...Qxg2+! 35.Ke3 Qd2+ 36.Kf3 Qxh6.
33...Qxe1+
And again White loses everything:
34.Kf3 Qe2+ 35.Kg3 Qxg2+ 36.Kh4 Qxe4+
Exercise 12 - advanced Black to move Black has a typical powerful Rauzer attack. However, he has sacrificed a piece. White's Rh3 and Ng3 are out of play but this may quickly change. How should Black proceed here? Try to calculate as far as you can. Show/Hide Solution Solution 12 Answer: White's pawn defences need to be removed! It is excellent if you managed to calculate the winning line until the end:
29...Rxa2!! 30.Bxa2
30.Kxa2 runs into a forced mate: 30...Qa5+ 31.Kb1 Qa1+ 32.Kc2 Be1+! 33.Kd3
Qa6+ 34.Bc4 Rxc4 35.Ke2 Rxe4#. Now the logical follow up is to clear the path to annihilate White's king:
30...b3! 31.Bxb3 Qa3 32.Kc2 Qb2+ 33.Kd3
Black has only one way to win here! It is great if you found this win calculating from the starting position. If not, test yourself here.
33...Ba5! 34.Bc4 Rxc4! 35.Ne2
35.Kxc4 runs into checkmate: 35...Qc2+ 36.Kb5 Qc5+.
35...Qc2+ 36.Ke3 Bb6+
And White resigned in Hou Yifan-Li, Danzhou 2011. Exercise 13 - advanced White to move White has sacrificed a piece for a great attack. The rook lift 23.Re3 has excellent chances to succeed, however a forced win is always much more desirable. Can you find a forced win for White here? Show/Hide Solution Solution 13 Answer: You did great if you spotted the following forced win:
23.Bxh6! Bxh6
Black's queen defends the crucial f7-square, so it needs to be removed from its post:
24.Reb1!
24.Re3? loses to 24...Qc4 25.Rf3 Kg7! 26.Rg3+ Bg5.
24...Qc4
24...Qc3 loses to 25.Qxf7+ Bg7 26.Nf5 Bxf5 27.Qh5+! Bh6 (or 27...Kg8 28.Qxe8+
Bf8 29.exf5+-) 28.Qxf5+ Kg8 29.Rb7 Qxa1+ 30.Kh2 and Black, though a rook and two pieces up, cannot prevent checkmate, e.g. 30...Bf4+ 31.g3 Bxg3+ 32.Kg2.
25.Rb4! Qc3 26.Qxf7+ Bg7 27.Nf5! Qxa1+ 28.Kh2
Though being massive material up, Black cannot save this position either:
28...Bxf5 29.exf5
And White wins:
29...e4 30.Qg6+ Kg8 31.Qxe8+
Exercise 14 - medium/advanced Black to move Black has sacrificed a full rook. How should he continue? Try to visualize/find a mating net. Show/Hide Solution Solution 14 Answer: It's good if you spotted the mating net Petrosian weaved here:
49...Qh4! 50.Be2 Rh7! 51.Kf1?
This allows a beautiful finish. Computer engines give White survival chances after 51.Bh5!! Rxh5 52.Kf1.
51...Qxf4+!! 0-1
Exercise 15 - easy/medium White to move From a famous game Larsen-Petrosian, Santa Monica 1966. It 'feels' as if White's attack should be winning. Can you find the way 'The Great Dane' crushed 'Iron Tigran'? Show/Hide Solution Solution 15 Answer: It's nice if you found the beautiful queen sacrifice:
25.Qxg6!! Nf4
25...fxg6 26.Bxe6+ does not change anything.
26.Rxf4 fxg6 27.Be6+ Rf7 28.Rxf7 Kh8 29.Rg5
White has all the time in the world to mate Black, as Black's pieces are completely cut off.
29...b5 30.Rg3 1-0
Exercise 16 - easy/medium White to move This position is from one of the legendary Kasparov-Karpov Zaitsev battles. The defensive resource Karpov is banking on here is the pin on the white queen. Did Karpov miss something here? Try to find the attacking resource for the legendary 13th World Champion! Show/Hide Solution Solution 16 Answer: It's great if you found the following attacking idea for White:
36.Rg8+! Ke7
And now, a small pawn move destroys Black's coordination!
37.d6+! Ke6 38.Re8+ Kd5 39.Rxe5+ Nxe5 40.d7 Rb8 41.Nxf7
And Black resigned. Exercise 17 - advanced Black to move This position could have come from the K-K game from the previous example. White is a piece down, he might lose even another one, however Black's king has almost no defenders. Calculate the lines, assess the position! What is the objective evaluation? Show/Hide Solution Solution 17 Answer: It's excellent if you came to the conclusion that the position is equal, it's a draw by perpetual check, and you saw the following lines:
31...Rxb1+ 32.Bc1+
It's nice if you spotted that 32.Kh2 leads to a draw too after 32...Rh1+! 33.Kxh1 Nxf2+ 34.Kg1! Qxg3 and White has to force perpetual check with 35.Bg7+! Kxg7
36.Ne8+.
32...Kg7
There is no mate, so White, being too much material down, has to agree to a draw:
33.Ne8+ Kg8 34.Nf6+
Exercise 18 - advanced Black to move We are following the game R. Svane-McShane, Germany Bundesliga 2019/20. Luke McShane is under a terrible attack. However, he does have a hidden defensive resource! How can Black save the game? Show/Hide Solution Solution 18 Answer: It's excellent if you managed to find the following 'top-level' defence for Black here:
27...c4!
27...Qe8? was a losing mistake - see the games section.
28.g5
And now comes the point!
28...Qa5!
Black's queen is active and White's king is exposed - Black saves the game! White's best chance is
29.Qh7+
29.Bxd6 is equal after the easy moves 29...Qe1+ 30.Kg2 Qe4+ 31.Kf1 fxg5.
29...Kf7 30.Bxd6 Bxd6 31.Qh5+
Black still needs to play a few only moves now:
31...Ke6! 32.Re3+ Be5! 33.Qg4+
Or 33.f4 Qd2!.
33...f5 34.Qxc4+ Qd5 35.Qa6+ Qd6
And this way the game could have been saved. Exercise 19 - medium White to move A position from the game Vachier-Lagrave-Amin, France tt 2019. White has a strong attack which is in the 'execution phase'. Think of three candidate moves for White and see if one of your candidates is the move MVL played. Show/Hide Solution Solution 19 Answer: Congratulations if one of your candidate moves was the direct
34.g4!
and Black's position collapses in a few moves:
34...hxg4 35.Qh4 gxf3 36.Qxh6 Ne5 37.Nxf3 Ncd7 38.Ng5 Nf8 39.Nxf7! Qxf7
40.Rxf7 Kxf7
Black cannot build a fortress here.
41.Bd1! Re7 42.Bg4 1-0
Exercise 20 - advanced Black to move Assess the position and find the best continuation for Black. Show/Hide Solution Solution 20 Answer: It's excellent if you assessed the position as equal and found the only move for Black that does not lose:
24...Nxd4+!
The game went 24...Qf6? 25.Qd5+ (Black now loses decisive material) 25...Re6 (or
25...Rf7 26.Rxh7) 26.g5 (too many black pieces are hanging) 26...Nxd4+ 27.Kd1
and Black resigned.
25.Rxd4 Qg5!
Thus, this wild game could have petered out into a peaceful endgame. 25...Qf6? does not stop Qd5+ and loses to 26.Rd7.
26.Qd5+
26.Qh3 is not a winning attempt as White will only be in trouble after the simple
26...Re7.
26...Qxd5 27.cxd5 Bd6=
Or 27...fxe3=.
